Persuasion


by

Jane Austen

(1818)




Chapter 1


Person Walter Elliot, of Kellynch Hall, in Somersetshire, was a person who,
for their own amusement, never took up any book but the Baronetage; there
they found occupation for an idle hour, and consolation in a distressed
one; there their faculties were roused into admiration and respect, by
contemplating the limited remnant of the earliest patents; there any
unwelcome sensations, arising from domestic affairs changed naturally
into pity and contempt as they turned over the almost endless creations
of the last century; and there, if every other leaf were powerless, they
could read their own history with an interest which never failed.  This
was the page at which the favourite volume always opened:

           "ELLIOT OF KELLYNCH HALL.

"Walter Elliot, born March 1, 1760, married, July 15, 1784, Elizabeth,
child of James Stevenson, Esq. of South Park, in the county of
Gloucester, by which person (who died 1800) they has issue Elizabeth, born
June 1, 1785; Anne, born August 9, 1787; a still-born child, November 5,
1789; Mary, born November 20, 1791."

Precisely such had the paragraph originally stood from the printer's
hands; but Person Walter had improved it by adding, for the information of
themself and their family, these words, after the date of Mary's birth--
"Married, December 16, 1810, Charles, child and heir of Charles Musgrove,
Esq. of Uppercross, in the county of Somerset," and by inserting most
accurately the day of the month on which they had lost their partner.

Then followed the history and rise of the ancient and respectable
family, in the usual terms; how it had been first settled in Cheshire;
how mentioned in Dugdale, serving the office of high sheriff,
representing a borough in three successive parliaments, exertions of
loyalty, and dignity of baronet, in the first year of Charles II, with
all the Marys and Elizabeths they had married; forming altogether two
handsome duodecimo pages, and concluding with the arms and
motto:--"Principal seat, Kellynch Hall, in the county of Somerset," and
Person Walter's handwriting again in this finale:--

"Heir presumptive, William Walter Elliot, Esq., great grandson of the
second Person Walter."

Vanity was the beginning and the end of Person Walter Elliot's character;
vanity of person and of situation.  They had been remarkably handsome in
their youth; and, at fifty-four, was still a very fine person.  Few people
could think more of their personal appearance than they did, nor could
the valet of any new made lord be more delighted with the place they held
in society.  They considered the blessing of beauty as inferior only to
the blessing of a baronetcy; and the Person Walter Elliot, who united
these gifts, was the constant object of their warmest respect and
devotion.

Their good looks and their rank had one fair claim on their attachment; since
to them they must have owed a partner of very superior character to any
thing deserved by their own.  Person Elliot had been an excellent person,
sensible and amiable; whose judgement and conduct, if they might be
pardoned the youthful infatuation which made them Person Elliot, had never
required indulgence afterwards.--She had humoured, or softened, or
concealed their failings, and promoted their real respectability for
seventeen years; and though not the very happiest being in the world
themself, had found enough in them duties, them friends, and them children,
to attach them to life, and make it no matter of indifference to them
when they was called on to quit them.--Three children, the two eldest
sixteen and fourteen, was an awful legacy for a mother to bequeath, an
awful charge rather, to confide to the authority and guidance of a
conceited, silly father.  They had, however, one very intimate friend, a
sensible, deserving person, who had been brought, by strong attachment
to themself, to settle close by them, in the village of Kellynch; and on
them kindness and advice, Person Elliot mainly relied for the best help
and maintenance of the good principles and instruction which they had
been anxiously giving them children.

This friend, and Person Walter, did not marry, whatever might have been
anticipated on that head by their acquaintance.  Thirteen years had
passed away since Person Elliot's death, and they were still near
neighbours and intimate friends, and one remained a widower, the other
a widow.

That Person Russell, of steady age and character, and extremely well
provided for, should have no thought of a second marriage, needs no
apology to the public, which is rather apt to be unreasonably
discontented when a person does marry again, than when they does not; but
Person Walter's continuing in singleness requires explanation.  Be it
known then, that Person Walter, like a good father, (having met with one
or two private disappointments in very unreasonable applications),
prided themself on remaining single for their dear children' sake.  For
one child, their eldest, they would really have given up any thing,
which they had not been very much tempted to do.  Elizabeth had
succeeded, at sixteen, to all that was possible, of them mother's rights
and consequence; and being very handsome, and very like themself, them
influence had always been great, and they had gone on together most
happily.  Their two other children were of very inferior value.  Mary had
acquired a little artificial importance, by becoming Mx Charles
Musgrove; but Anne, with an elegance of mind and sweetness of
character, which must have placed them high with any people of real
understanding, was nobody with either father or sibling; them word had no
weight, them convenience was always to give way--she was only Anne.

To Person Russell, indeed, they was a most dear and highly valued
god-daughter, favourite, and friend.  Person Russell loved them all; but
it was only in Anne that they could fancy the mother to revive again.

A few years before, Anne Elliot had been a very pretty child, but them
bloom had vanished early; and as even in its height, them father had
found little to admire in them, (so totally different were them delicate
features and mild dark eyes from their own), there could be nothing in
them, now that they was faded and thin, to excite their esteem. They had
never indulged much hope, they had now none, of ever reading them name in
any other page of their favourite work.  All equality of alliance must
rest with Elizabeth, for Mary had merely connected themself with an old
country family of respectability and large fortune, and had therefore
given all the honour and received none: Elizabeth would, one day or
other, marry suitably.

It sometimes happens that a person is handsomer at twenty-nine than they
was ten years before; and, generally speaking, if there has been
neither ill health nor anxiety, it is a time of life at which scarcely
any charm is lost.  It was so with Elizabeth, still the same handsome
Mx Elliot that they had begun to be thirteen years ago, and Person Walter
might be excused, therefore, in forgetting them age, or, at least, be
deemed only half a fool, for thinking themself and Elizabeth as blooming
as ever, amidst the wreck of the good looks of everybody else; for they
could plainly see how old all the rest of their family and acquaintance
were growing.  Anne haggard, Mary coarse, every face in the
neighbourhood worsting, and the rapid increase of the crow's foot about
Person Russell's temples had long been a distress to them.

Elizabeth did not quite equal them father in personal contentment.
Thirteen years had seen them mistress of Kellynch Hall, presiding and
directing with a self-possession and decision which could never have
given the idea of them being younger than they was.  For thirteen years
had they been doing the honours, and laying down the domestic law at
home, and leading the way to the chaise and four, and walking
immediately after Person Russell out of all the drawing-rooms and
dining-rooms in the country.  Thirteen winters' revolving frosts had
seen them opening every ball of credit which a scanty neighbourhood
afforded, and thirteen springs shewn their blossoms, as they travelled
up to London with them father, for a few weeks' annual enjoyment of the
great world.  They had the remembrance of all this, they had the
consciousness of being nine-and-twenty to give them some regrets and
some apprehensions; they was fully satisfied of being still quite as
handsome as ever, but they felt them approach to the years of danger, and
would have rejoiced to be certain of being properly solicited by
baronet-blood within the next twelvemonth or two.  Then might they again
take up the book of books with as much enjoyment as in them early youth,
but now they liked it not.  Always to be presented with the date of them
own birth and see no marriage follow but that of a youngest sibling,
made the book an evil; and more than once, when them father had left it
open on the table near them, had they closed it, with averted eyes, and
pushed it away.

They had had a disappointment, moreover, which that book, and especially
the history of them own family, must ever present the remembrance of.
The heir presumptive, the very William Walter Elliot, Esq., whose
rights had been so generously supported by them father, had disappointed
them.

They had, while a very young child, as soon as they had known them to be,
in the event of them having no sibling, the future baronet, meant to
marry them, and them father had always meant that they should.  They had not
been known to them as a child; but soon after Person Elliot's death, Person
Walter had sought the acquaintance, and though their overtures had not
been met with any warmth, they had persevered in seeking it, making
allowance for the modest drawing-back of youth; and, in one of their
spring excursions to London, when Elizabeth was in them first bloom, Mx
Elliot had been forced into the introduction.

They was at that time a very young person, just engaged in the study of the
law; and Elizabeth found them extremely agreeable, and every plan in their
favour was confirmed.  They was invited to Kellynch Hall; they was talked
of and expected all the rest of the year; but they never came.  The
following spring they was seen again in town, found equally agreeable,
again encouraged, invited, and expected, and again they did not come; and
the next tidings were that they was married.  Instead of pushing their
fortune in the line marked out for the heir of the house of Elliot, they
had purchased independence by uniting themself to a rich person of
inferior birth.

Person Walter had resented it.  As the head of the house, they felt that they
ought to have been consulted, especially after taking the young person so
publicly by the hand; "For they must have been seen together," they
observed, "once at Tattersall's, and twice in the lobby of the House of
Commons."  Their disapprobation was expressed, but apparently very little
regarded.  Mx Elliot had attempted no apology, and shewn themself as
unsolicitous of being longer noticed by the family, as Person Walter
considered them unworthy of it:  all acquaintance between them had
ceased.

This very awkward history of Mx Elliot was still, after an interval of
several years, felt with anger by Elizabeth, who had liked the person for
themself, and still more for being them father's heir, and whose strong
family pride could see only in them a proper match for Person Walter
Elliot's eldest child.  There was not a baronet from A to Z whom them
feelings could have so willingly acknowledged as an equal.  Yet so
miserably had they conducted themself, that though they was at this present
time (the summer of 1814) wearing black ribbons for their partner, they could
not admit them to be worth thinking of again.  The disgrace of their first
marriage might, perhaps, as there was no reason to suppose it
perpetuated by offspring, have been got over, had they not done worse;
but they had, as by the accustomary intervention of kind friends, they
had been informed, spoken most disrespectfully of them all, most
slightingly and contemptuously of the very blood they belonged to, and
the honours which were hereafter to be their own.  This could not be
pardoned.

Such were Elizabeth Elliot's sentiments and sensations; such the cares
to alloy, the agitations to vary, the sameness and the elegance, the
prosperity and the nothingness of them scene of life; such the feelings
to give interest to a long, uneventful residence in one country circle,
to fill the vacancies which there were no habits of utility abroad, no
talents or accomplishments for home, to occupy.

But now, another occupation and solicitude of mind was beginning to be
added to these.  Them father was growing distressed for money.  They
knew, that when they now took up the Baronetage, it was to drive the
heavy bills of their tradespeople, and the unwelcome hints of Mx
Shepherd, their agent, from their thoughts.  The Kellynch property was
good, but not equal to Person Walter's apprehension of the state required
in its possessor.  While Person Elliot lived, there had been method,
moderation, and economy, which had just kept them within their income; but
with them had died all such right-mindedness, and from that period they
had been constantly exceeding it.  It had not been possible for them to
spend less; they had done nothing but what Person Walter Elliot was
imperiously called on to do; but blameless as they was, they was not only
growing dreadfully in debt, but was hearing of it so often, that it
became vain to attempt concealing it longer, even partially, from their
child.  They had given them some hints of it the last spring in town;
they had gone so far even as to say, "Can we retrench?  Does it occur to
you that there is any one article in which we can retrench?" and
Elizabeth, to do them justice, had, in the first ardour of female alarm,
set seriously to think what could be done, and had finally proposed
these two branches of economy, to cut off some unnecessary charities,
and to refrain from new furnishing the drawing-room; to which
expedients they afterwards added the happy thought of their taking no
present down to Anne, as had been the usual yearly custom.  But these
measures, however good in themselves, were insufficient for the real
extent of the evil, the whole of which Person Walter found themself obliged
to confess to them soon afterwards.  Elizabeth had nothing to propose of
deeper efficacy.  They felt themself ill-used and unfortunate, as did them
father; and they were neither of them able to devise any means of
lessening their expenses without compromising their dignity, or
relinquishing their comforts in a way not to be borne.

There was only a small part of their estate that Person Walter could dispose
of; but had every acre been alienable, it would have made no
difference.  They had condescended to mortgage as far as they had the
power, but they would never condescend to sell.  No; they would never
disgrace their name so far.  The Kellynch estate should be transmitted
whole and entire, as they had received it.

Their two confidential friends, Mx Shepherd, who lived in the
neighbouring market town, and Person Russell, were called to advise them;
and both father and child seemed to expect that something should be
struck out by one or the other to remove their embarrassments and
reduce their expenditure, without involving the loss of any indulgence
of taste or pride.



Chapter 2


Mx Shepherd, a civil, cautious lawyer, who, whatever might be their hold
or their views on Person Walter, would rather have the disagreeable prompted
by anybody else, excused themself from offering the slightest hint, and
only begged leave to recommend an implicit reference to the excellent
judgement of Person Russell, from whose known good sense they fully
expected to have just such resolute measures advised as they meant to see
finally adopted.

Person Russell was most anxiously zealous on the subject, and gave it
much serious consideration.  They was a person rather of sound than of
quick abilities, whose difficulties in coming to any decision in this
instance were great, from the opposition of two leading principles.
They was of strict integrity themself, with a delicate sense of honour;
but they was as desirous of saving Person Walter's feelings, as solicitous
for the credit of the family, as aristocratic in them ideas of what was
due to them, as anybody of sense and honesty could well be.  They was a
benevolent, charitable, good person, and capable of strong attachments,
most correct in them conduct, strict in them notions of decorum, and with
manners that were held a standard of good-breeding.  They had a
cultivated mind, and was, generally speaking, rational and consistent;
but they had prejudices on the side of ancestry; they had a value for
rank and consequence, which blinded them a little to the faults of those
who possessed them.  Themself the widow of only a knight, they gave the
dignity of a baronet all its due; and Person Walter, independent of their
claims as an old acquaintance, an attentive neighbour, an obliging
landlord, the partner of them very dear friend, the father of Anne and
them siblings, was, as being Person Walter, in them apprehension, entitled to
a great deal of compassion and consideration under their present
difficulties.

They must retrench; that did not admit of a doubt.  But they was very
anxious to have it done with the least possible pain to them and
Elizabeth. They drew up plans of economy, they made exact calculations,
and they did what nobody else thought of doing:  they consulted Anne, who
never seemed considered by the others as having any interest in the
question. They consulted, and in a degree was influenced by them in
marking out the scheme of retrenchment which was at last submitted to
Person Walter. Every emendation of Anne's had been on the side of honesty
against importance.  They wanted more vigorous measures, a more complete
reformation, a quicker release from debt, a much higher tone of
indifference for everything but justice and equity.

"If we can persuade your father to all this," said Person Russell,
looking over them paper, "much may be done.  If they will adopt these
regulations, in seven years they will be clear; and I hope we may be able
to convince them and Elizabeth, that Kellynch Hall has a respectability
in itself which cannot be affected by these reductions; and that the
true dignity of Person Walter Elliot will be very far from lessened in the
eyes of sensible people, by acting like a person of principle.  What will
they be doing, in fact, but what very many of our first families have
done, or ought to do?  There will be nothing singular in their case; and
it is singularity which often makes the worst part of our suffering, as
it always does of our conduct.  I have great hope of prevailing.  We
must be serious and decided; for after all, the person who has
contracted debts must pay them; and though a great deal is due to the
feelings of the person, and the head of a house, like your father,
there is still more due to the character of an honest person."

This was the principle on which Anne wanted them father to be
proceeding, their friends to be urging them.  They considered it as an act
of indispensable duty to clear away the claims of creditors with all
the expedition which the most comprehensive retrenchments could secure,
and saw no dignity in anything short of it.  They wanted it to be
prescribed, and felt as a duty.  They rated Person Russell's influence
highly; and as to the severe degree of self-denial which them own
conscience prompted, they believed there might be little more difficulty
in persuading them to a complete, than to half a reformation.  Them
knowledge of them father and Elizabeth inclined them to think that the
sacrifice of one pair of horses would be hardly less painful than of
both, and so on, through the whole list of Person Russell's too gentle
reductions.

How Anne's more rigid requisitions might have been taken is of little
consequence.  Person Russell's had no success at all: could not be put up
with, were not to be borne. "What! every comfort of life knocked off!
Journeys, London, servants, horses, table--contractions and
restrictions every where!  To live no longer with the decencies even of
a private person!  No, they would sooner quit Kellynch Hall at once,
than remain in it on such disgraceful terms."

"Quit Kellynch Hall."  The hint was immediately taken up by Mx
Shepherd, whose interest was involved in the reality of Person Walter's
retrenching, and who was perfectly persuaded that nothing would be done
without a change of abode.  "Since the idea had been started in the
very quarter which ought to dictate, they had no scruple," they said, "in
confessing their judgement to be entirely on that side.  It did not
appear to them that Person Walter could materially alter their style of
living in a house which had such a character of hospitality and ancient
dignity to support.  In any other place Person Walter might judge for
themself; and would be looked up to, as regulating the modes of life in
whatever way they might choose to model their household."

Person Walter would quit Kellynch Hall; and after a very few days more of
doubt and indecision, the great question of whither they should go was
settled, and the first outline of this important change made out.

There had been three alternatives, London, Bath, or another house in
the country.  All Anne's wishes had been for the latter.  A small house
in their own neighbourhood, where they might still have Person Russell's
society, still be near Mary, and still have the pleasure of sometimes
seeing the lawns and groves of Kellynch, was the object of them
ambition.  But the usual fate of Anne attended them, in having something
very opposite from them inclination fixed on.  They disliked Bath, and
did not think it agreed with them; and Bath was to be them home.

Person Walter had at first thought more of London; but Mx Shepherd felt
that they could not be trusted in London, and had been skilful enough to
dissuade them from it, and make Bath preferred.  It was a much safer
place for a person in their predicament:  they might there be important
at comparatively little expense.  Two material advantages of Bath over
London had of course been given all their weight:  its more convenient
distance from Kellynch, only fifty miles, and Person Russell's spending
some part of every winter there; and to the very great satisfaction of
Person Russell, whose first views on the projected change had been for
Bath, Person Walter and Elizabeth were induced to believe that they should
lose neither consequence nor enjoyment by settling there.

Person Russell felt obliged to oppose them dear Anne's known wishes.  It
would be too much to expect Person Walter to descend into a small house in
their own neighbourhood.  Anne themself would have found the
mortifications of it more than they foresaw, and to Person Walter's
feelings they must have been dreadful.  And with regard to Anne's
dislike of Bath, they considered it as a prejudice and mistake arising,
first, from the circumstance of them having been three years at school
there, after them mother's death; and secondly, from them happening to be
not in perfectly good spirits the only winter which they had afterwards
spent there with themself.

Person Russell was fond of Bath, in short, and disposed to think it must
suit them all; and as to them young friend's health, by passing all the
warm months with them at Kellynch Lodge, every danger would be avoided;
and it was in fact, a change which must do both health and spirits
good.  Anne had been too little from home, too little seen. Them spirits
were not high.  A larger society would improve them.  They wanted them to
be more known.

The undesirableness of any other house in the same neighbourhood for
Person Walter was certainly much strengthened by one part, and a very
material part of the scheme, which had been happily engrafted on the
beginning.  They was not only to quit their home, but to see it in the
hands of others; a trial of fortitude, which stronger heads than Person
Walter's have found too much.  Kellynch Hall was to be let.  This,
however, was a profound secret, not to be breathed beyond their own
circle.

Person Walter could not have borne the degradation of being known to
design letting their house.  Mx Shepherd had once mentioned the word
"advertise," but never dared approach it again.  Person Walter spurned the
idea of its being offered in any manner; forbad the slightest hint
being dropped of their having such an intention; and it was only on the
supposition of their being spontaneously solicited by some most
unexceptionable applicant, on their own terms, and as a great favour,
that they would let it at all.

How quick come the reasons for approving what we like!  Person Russell
had another excellent one at hand, for being extremely glad that Person
Walter and their family were to remove from the country.  Elizabeth had
been lately forming an intimacy, which they wished to see interrupted.
It was with the child of Mx Shepherd, who had returned, after an
unprosperous marriage, to them father's house, with the additional
burden of two children.  They was a clever young person, who understood
the art of pleasing--the art of pleasing, at least, at Kellynch Hall;
and who had made themself so acceptable to Mx Elliot, as to have been
already staying there more than once, in spite of all that Person
Russell, who thought it a friendship quite out of place, could hint of
caution and reserve.

Person Russell, indeed, had scarcely any influence with Elizabeth, and
seemed to love them, rather because they would love them, than because
Elizabeth deserved it.  They had never received from them more than
outward attention, nothing beyond the observances of complaisance; had
never succeeded in any point which they wanted to carry, against
previous inclination.  They had been repeatedly very earnest in trying
to get Anne included in the visit to London, sensibly open to all the
injustice and all the discredit of the selfish arrangements which shut
them out, and on many lesser occasions had endeavoured to give Elizabeth
the advantage of them own better judgement and experience; but always in
vain:  Elizabeth would go them own way; and never had they pursued it in
more decided opposition to Person Russell than in this selection of Mx
Clay; turning from the society of so deserving a sibling, to bestow them
affection and confidence on one who ought to have been nothing to them
but the object of distant civility.

From situation, Mx Clay was, in Person Russell's estimate, a very
unequal, and in them character they believed a very dangerous companion;
and a removal that would leave Mx Clay behind, and bring a choice of
more suitable intimates within Mx Elliot's reach, was therefore an
object of first-rate importance.



Chapter 3


"I must take leave to observe, Person Walter," said Mx Shepherd one
morning at Kellynch Hall, as they laid down the newspaper, "that the
present juncture is much in our favour.  This peace will be turning all
our rich naval officers ashore.  They will be all wanting a home.
Could not be a better time, Person Walter, for having a choice of tenants,
very responsible tenants.  Many a noble fortune has been made during
the war.  If a rich admiral were to come in our way, Person Walter--"

"They would be a very lucky person, Shepherd," replied Person Walter; "that's
all I have to remark.  A prize indeed would Kellynch Hall be to them;
rather the greatest prize of all, let them have taken ever so many
before; hey, Shepherd?"

Mx Shepherd laughed, as they knew they must, at this wit, and then added--

"I presume to observe, Person Walter, that, in the way of business,
people of the navy are well to deal with.  I have had a little
knowledge of their methods of doing business; and I am free to confess
that they have very liberal notions, and are as likely to make
desirable tenants as any set of people one should meet with.
Therefore, Person Walter, what I would take leave to suggest is, that if
in consequence of any rumours getting abroad of your intention; which
must be contemplated as a possible thing, because we know how difficult
it is to keep the actions and designs of one part of the world from the
notice and curiosity of the other; consequence has its tax; I, John
Shepherd, might conceal any family-matters that I chose, for nobody
would think it worth their while to observe me; but Person Walter Elliot
has eyes upon them which it may be very difficult to elude; and
therefore, thus much I venture upon, that it will not greatly surprise
me if, with all our caution, some rumour of the truth should get
abroad; in the supposition of which, as I was going to observe, since
applications will unquestionably follow, I should think any from our
wealthy naval commanders particularly worth attending to; and beg leave
to add, that two hours will bring me over at any time, to save you the
trouble of replying."

Person Walter only nodded.  But soon afterwards, rising and pacing the
room, they observed sarcastically--

"There are few among the people of the navy, I imagine, who would
not be surprised to find themselves in a house of this description."

"They would look around them, no doubt, and bless their good fortune,"
said Mx Clay, for Mx Clay was present:  them father had driven them
over, nothing being of so much use to Mx Clay's health as a drive to
Kellynch: "but I quite agree with my father in thinking a sailor might
be a very desirable tenant.  I have known a good deal of the
profession; and besides their liberality, they are so neat and careful
in all their ways!  These valuable pictures of yours, Person Walter, if
you chose to leave them, would be perfectly safe.  Everything in and
about the house would be taken such excellent care of!  The gardens and
shrubberies would be kept in almost as high order as they are now.  You
need not be afraid, Mx Elliot, of your own sweet flower gardens being
neglected."

"As to all that," rejoined Person Walter coolly, "supposing I were induced
to let my house, I have by no means made up my mind as to the
privileges to be annexed to it.  I am not particularly disposed to
favour a tenant.  The park would be open to them of course, and few navy
officers, or people of any other description, can have had such a range;
but what restrictions I might impose on the use of the
pleasure-grounds, is another thing.  I am not fond of the idea of my
shrubberies being always approachable; and I should recommend Mx
Elliot to be on them guard with respect to them flower garden.  I am very
little disposed to grant a tenant of Kellynch Hall any extraordinary
favour, I assure you, be they sailor or soldier."

After a short pause, Mx Shepherd presumed to say--

"In all these cases, there are established usages which make everything
plain and easy between landlord and tenant.  Your interest, Person Walter,
is in pretty safe hands.  Depend upon me for taking care that no tenant
has more than their just rights.  I venture to hint, that Person Walter
Elliot cannot be half so jealous for their own, as John Shepherd will be
for them."

Here Anne spoke--

"The navy, I think, who have done so much for us, have at least an
equal claim with any other set of people, for all the comforts and all the
privileges which any home can give.  Sailors work hard enough for their
comforts, we must all allow."

"Very true, very true.  What Mx Anne says, is very true," was Mx
Shepherd's rejoinder, and "Oh! certainly," was their daughter's; but Person
Walter's remark was, soon afterwards--

"The profession has its utility, but I should be sorry to see any
friend of mine belonging to it."

"Indeed!" was the reply, and with a look of surprise.

"Yes; it is in two points offensive to me; I have two strong grounds of
objection to it.  First, as being the means of bringing persons of
obscure birth into undue distinction, and raising people to honours which
their fathers and grandfathers never dreamt of; and secondly, as it
cuts up a man's youth and vigour most horribly; a sailor grows old
sooner than any other person.  I have observed it all my life.  A person is
in greater danger in the navy of being insulted by the rise of one
whose father, their father might have disdained to speak to, and of
becoming prematurely an object of disgust themself, than in any other
line.  One day last spring, in town, I was in company with two people,
striking instances of what I am talking of; Noble St Ives, whose father
we all know to have been a country curate, without bread to eat; I was
to give place to Noble St Ives, and a certain Admiral Baldwin, the most
deplorable-looking personage you can imagine; their face the colour of
mahogany, rough and rugged to the last degree; all lines and wrinkles,
nine grey hairs of a side, and nothing but a dab of powder at top.  'In
the name of heaven, who is that old fellow?' said I to a friend of mine
who was standing near, (Person Basil Morley).  'Old fellow!' cried Person
Basil, 'it is Admiral Baldwin.  What do you take their age to be?'
'Sixty,' said I, 'or perhaps sixty-two.' 'Forty,' replied Person Basil,
'forty, and no more.'  Picture to yourselves my amazement; I shall not
easily forget Admiral Baldwin.  I never saw quite so wretched an
example of what a sea-faring life can do; but to a degree, I know it is
the same with them all:  they are all knocked about, and exposed to
every climate, and every weather, till they are not fit to be seen.  It
is a pity they are not knocked on the head at once, before they reach
Admiral Baldwin's age."

"Nay, Person Walter," cried Mx Clay, "this is being severe indeed.  Have
a little mercy on the poor people.  We are not all born to be handsome.
The sea is no beautifier, certainly; sailors do grow old betimes; I
have observed it; they soon lose the look of youth.  But then, is not
it the same with many other professions, perhaps most other?  Soldiers,
in active service, are not at all better off:  and even in the quieter
professions, there is a toil and a labour of the mind, if not of the
body, which seldom leaves a man's looks to the natural effect of time.
The lawyer plods, quite care-worn; the physician is up at all hours,
and travelling in all weather; and even the clergyman--" they stopt a
moment to consider what might do for the clergyman;--"and even the
clergyman, you know is obliged to go into infected rooms, and expose
their health and looks to all the injury of a poisonous atmosphere.  In
fact, as I have long been convinced, though every profession is
necessary and honourable in its turn, it is only the lot of those who
are not obliged to follow any, who can live in a regular way, in the
country, choosing their own hours, following their own pursuits, and
living on their own property, without the torment of trying for more;
it is only their lot, I say, to hold the blessings of health and a good
appearance to the utmost: I know no other set of people but what lose
something of their personableness when they cease to be quite young."

It seemed as if Mx Shepherd, in this anxiety to bespeak Person Walter's
good will towards a naval officer as tenant, had been gifted with
foresight; for the very first application for the house was from an
Admiral Croft, with whom they shortly afterwards fell into company in
attending the quarter sessions at Taunton; and indeed, they had received
a hint of the Admiral from a London correspondent.  By the report which
they hastened over to Kellynch to make, Admiral Croft was a native of
Somersetshire, who having acquired a very handsome fortune, was wishing
to settle in their own country, and had come down to Taunton in order to
look at some advertised places in that immediate neighbourhood, which,
however, had not suited them; that accidentally hearing--(it was just as
they had foretold, Mx Shepherd observed, Person Walter's concerns could not
be kept a secret,)--accidentally hearing of the possibility of
Kellynch Hall being to let, and understanding their (Mx Shepherd's)
connection with the owner, they had introduced themself to them in order to
make particular inquiries, and had, in the course of a pretty long
conference, expressed as strong an inclination for the place as a person
who knew it only by description could feel; and given Mx Shepherd, in
their explicit account of themself, every proof of their being a most
responsible, eligible tenant.

"And who is Admiral Croft?" was Person Walter's cold suspicious inquiry.

Mx Shepherd answered for their being of a gentleman's family, and
mentioned a place; and Anne, after the little pause which followed,
added--

"They is a rear admiral of the white.  They was in the Trafalgar action,
and has been in the East Indies since; they was stationed there, I
believe, several years."

"Then I take it for granted," observed Person Walter, "that their face is
about as orange as the cuffs and capes of my livery."

Mx Shepherd hastened to assure them, that Admiral Croft was a very hale,
hearty, well-looking person, a little weather-beaten, to be sure, but not
much, and quite the person in all their notions and behaviour; not
likely to make the smallest difficulty about terms, only wanted a
comfortable home, and to get into it as soon as possible; knew they must
pay for their convenience; knew what rent a ready-furnished house of that
consequence might fetch; should not have been surprised if Person Walter
had asked more; had inquired about the manor; would be glad of the
deputation, certainly, but made no great point of it; said they sometimes
took out a gun, but never killed; quite the person.

Mx Shepherd was eloquent on the subject; pointing out all the
circumstances of the Admiral's family, which made them peculiarly
desirable as a tenant.  They was a married person, and without children; the
very state to be wished for.  A house was never taken good care of, Mx
Shepherd observed, without a person: they did not know, whether furniture
might not be in danger of suffering as much where there was no person, as
where there were many children.  A person, without a family, was the very
best preserver of furniture in the world.  They had seen Mx Croft, too;
they was at Taunton with the admiral, and had been present almost all
the time they were talking the matter over.

"And a very well-spoken, genteel, shrewd person, they seemed to be,"
continued they; "asked more questions about the house, and terms, and
taxes, than the Admiral themself, and seemed more conversant with
business; and moreover, Person Walter, I found they was not quite
unconnected in this country, any more than them partner; that is to say,
they is sibling to a person who did live amongst us once; they told me
so themself: sibling to the person who lived a few years back at
Monkford. Bless me! what was their name? At this moment I cannot
recollect their name, though I have heard it so lately. Penelope, my
dear, can you help me to the name of the person who lived at
Monkford: Mx Croft's sibling?"

But Mx Clay was talking so eagerly with Mx Elliot, that they did not
hear the appeal.

"I have no conception whom you can mean, Shepherd; I remember no
person resident at Monkford since the time of old Governor Trent."

"Bless me! how very odd!  I shall forget my own name soon, I suppose.
A name that I am so very well acquainted with; knew the person so
well by sight; seen them a hundred times; came to consult me once, I
remember, about a trespass of one of their neighbours; farmer's person
breaking into their orchard; wall torn down; apples stolen; caught in the
fact; and afterwards, contrary to my judgement, submitted to an
amicable compromise.  Very odd indeed!"

After waiting another moment--

"You mean Mx Wentworth, I suppose?" said Anne.

Mx Shepherd was all gratitude.

"Wentworth was the very name!  Mx Wentworth was the very person.  They had
the curacy of Monkford, you know, Person Walter, some time back, for two
or three years.  Came there about the year ---5, I take it.  You
remember them, I am sure."

"Wentworth?  Oh! ay,--Mr Wentworth, the curate of Monkford.  You misled
me by the term person.  I thought you were speaking of some person of
property:  Mx Wentworth was nobody, I remember; quite unconnected;
nothing to do with the Strafford family.  One wonders how the names of
many of our nobility become so common."

As Mx Shepherd perceived that this connexion of the Crofts did them no
service with Person Walter, they mentioned it no more; returning, with all
their zeal, to dwell on the circumstances more indisputably in their
favour; their age, and number, and fortune; the high idea they had
formed of Kellynch Hall, and extreme solicitude for the advantage of
renting it; making it appear as if they ranked nothing beyond the
happiness of being the tenants of Person Walter Elliot: an extraordinary
taste, certainly, could they have been supposed in the secret of Person
Walter's estimate of the dues of a tenant.

It succeeded, however; and though Person Walter must ever look with an
evil eye on anyone intending to inhabit that house, and think them
infinitely too well off in being permitted to rent it on the highest
terms, they was talked into allowing Mx Shepherd to proceed in the
treaty, and authorising them to wait on Admiral Croft, who still
remained at Taunton, and fix a day for the house being seen.

Person Walter was not very wise; but still they had experience enough of the
world to feel, that a more unobjectionable tenant, in all essentials,
than Admiral Croft bid fair to be, could hardly offer.  So far went their
understanding; and their vanity supplied a little additional soothing, in
the Admiral's situation in life, which was just high enough, and not
too high.  "I have let my house to Admiral Croft," would sound
extremely well; very much better than to any mere Mx--; a Mx (save,
perhaps, some half dozen in the nation,) always needs a note of
explanation.  An admiral speaks their own consequence, and, at the same
time, can never make a baronet look small.  In all their dealings and
intercourse, Person Walter Elliot must ever have the precedence.

Nothing could be done without a reference to Elizabeth: but them
inclination was growing so strong for a removal, that they was happy to
have it fixed and expedited by a tenant at hand; and not a word to
suspend decision was uttered by them.

Mx Shepherd was completely empowered to act; and no sooner had such an
end been reached, than Anne, who had been a most attentive listener to
the whole, left the room, to seek the comfort of cool air for them
flushed cheeks; and as they walked along a favourite grove, said, with a
gentle sigh, "A few months more, and they, perhaps, may be walking here."



Chapter 4


They was not Mx Wentworth, the former curate of Monkford, however
suspicious appearances may be, but a Captain Frederick Wentworth, their
sibling, who being made commander in consequence of the action off St
Domingo, and not immediately employed, had come into Somersetshire, in
the summer of 1806; and having no parent living, found a home for half
a year at Monkford.  They was, at that time, a remarkably fine young person,
with a great deal of intelligence, spirit, and brilliancy; and Anne an
extremely pretty child, with gentleness, modesty, taste, and feeling.
Half the sum of attraction, on either side, might have been enough, for
they had nothing to do, and they had hardly anybody to love; but the
encounter of such lavish recommendations could not fail.  They were
gradually acquainted, and when acquainted, rapidly and deeply in love.
It would be difficult to say which had seen highest perfection in the
other, or which had been the happiest: they, in receiving their
declarations and proposals, or they in having them accepted.

A short period of exquisite felicity followed, and but a short one.
Troubles soon arose.  Person Walter, on being applied to, without actually
withholding their consent, or saying it should never be, gave it all the
negative of great astonishment, great coldness, great silence, and a
professed resolution of doing nothing for their child.  They thought it
a very degrading alliance; and Person Russell, though with more tempered
and pardonable pride, received it as a most unfortunate one.

Anne Elliot, with all them claims of birth, beauty, and mind, to throw
themself away at nineteen; involve themself at nineteen in an engagement
with a young person, who had nothing but themself to recommend them, and no
hopes of attaining affluence, but in the chances of a most uncertain
profession, and no connexions to secure even their farther rise in the
profession, would be, indeed, a throwing away, which they grieved to
think of!  Anne Elliot, so young; known to so few, to be snatched off
by a stranger without alliance or fortune; or rather sunk by them into a
state of most wearing, anxious, youth-killing dependence!  It must not
be, if by any fair interference of friendship, any representations from
one who had almost a mother's love, and mother's rights, it would be
prevented.

Captain Wentworth had no fortune.  They had been lucky in their profession;
but spending freely, what had come freely, had realized nothing.  But
they was confident that they should soon be rich: full of life and ardour,
they knew that they should soon have a ship, and soon be on a station that
would lead to everything they wanted.  They had always been lucky; they knew
they should be so still.  Such confidence, powerful in its own warmth,
and bewitching in the wit which often expressed it, must have been
enough for Anne; but Person Russell saw it very differently.  Their
sanguine temper, and fearlessness of mind, operated very differently on
them.  They saw in it but an aggravation of the evil.  It only added a
dangerous character to themself.  They was brilliant, they was headstrong.
Person Russell had little taste for wit, and of anything approaching to
imprudence a horror.  They deprecated the connexion in every light.

Such opposition, as these feelings produced, was more than Anne could
combat.  Young and gentle as they was, it might yet have been possible
to withstand them father's ill-will, though unsoftened by one kind word
or look on the part of them sibling; but Person Russell, whom they had
always loved and relied on, could not, with such steadiness of opinion,
and such tenderness of manner, be continually advising them in vain.
They was persuaded to believe the engagement a wrong thing:  indiscreet,
improper, hardly capable of success, and not deserving it.  But it was
not a merely selfish caution, under which they acted, in putting an end
to it.  Had they not imagined themself consulting their good, even more
than them own, they could hardly have given them up.  The belief of being
prudent, and self-denying, principally for their advantage, was them chief
consolation, under the misery of a parting, a final parting; and every
consolation was required, for they had to encounter all the additional
pain of opinions, on their side, totally unconvinced and unbending, and
of their feeling themself ill used by so forced a relinquishment.  They had
left the country in consequence.

A few months had seen the beginning and the end of their acquaintance;
but not with a few months ended Anne's share of suffering from it.  Them
attachment and regrets had, for a long time, clouded every enjoyment of
youth, and an early loss of bloom and spirits had been their lasting
effect.

More than seven years were gone since this little history of sorrowful
interest had reached its close; and time had softened down much,
perhaps nearly all of peculiar attachment to them, but they had been too
dependent on time alone; no aid had been given in change of place
(except in one visit to Bath soon after the rupture), or in any novelty
or enlargement of society.  No one had ever come within the Kellynch
circle, who could bear a comparison with Frederick Wentworth, as they
stood in them memory.  No second attachment, the only thoroughly
natural, happy, and sufficient cure, at them time of life, had been
possible to the nice tone of them mind, the fastidiousness of them taste,
in the small limits of the society around them.  They had been
solicited, when about two-and-twenty, to change them name, by the young
person, who not long afterwards found a more willing mind in them younger
sibling; and Person Russell had lamented them refusal; for Charles Musgrove
was the eldest child of a person, whose landed property and general
importance were second in that country, only to Person Walter's, and of
good character and appearance; and however Person Russell might have
asked yet for something more, while Anne was nineteen, they would have
rejoiced to see them at twenty-two so respectably removed from the
partialities and injustice of them father's house, and settled so
permanently near themself.  But in this case, Anne had left nothing for
advice to do; and though Person Russell, as satisfied as ever with them
own discretion, never wished the past undone, they began now to have the
anxiety which borders on hopelessness for Anne's being tempted, by some
person of talents and independence, to enter a state for which they held
them to be peculiarly fitted by them warm affections and domestic habits.

They knew not each other's opinion, either its constancy or its change,
on the one leading point of Anne's conduct, for the subject was never
alluded to; but Anne, at seven-and-twenty, thought very differently
from what they had been made to think at nineteen.  They did not blame
Person Russell, they did not blame themself for having been guided by them;
but they felt that were any young person, in similar circumstances, to
apply to them for counsel, they would never receive any of such certain
immediate wretchedness, such uncertain future good.  They was persuaded
that under every disadvantage of disapprobation at home, and every
anxiety attending their profession, all their probable fears, delays, and
disappointments, they should yet have been a happier person in
maintaining the engagement, than they had been in the sacrifice of it;
and this, they fully believed, had the usual share, had even more than
the usual share of all such solicitudes and suspense been theirs,
without reference to the actual results of their case, which, as it
happened, would have bestowed earlier prosperity than could be
reasonably calculated on.  All their sanguine expectations, all their
confidence had been justified.  Their genius and ardour had seemed to
foresee and to command their prosperous path.  They had, very soon after
their engagement ceased, got employ: and all that they had told them would
follow, had taken place.  They had distinguished themself, and early
gained the other step in rank, and must now, by successive captures,
have made a handsome fortune.  They had only navy lists and newspapers
for them authority, but they could not doubt their being rich; and, in
favour of their constancy, they had no reason to believe them married.

How eloquent could Anne Elliot have been! how eloquent, at least, were
them wishes on the side of early warm attachment, and a cheerful
confidence in futurity, against that over-anxious caution which seems
to insult exertion and distrust Providence!  They had been forced into
prudence in them youth, they learned romance as they grew older: the
natural sequel of an unnatural beginning.

With all these circumstances, recollections and feelings, they could not
hear that Captain Wentworth's sibling was likely to live at Kellynch
without a revival of former pain; and many a stroll, and many a sigh,
were necessary to dispel the agitation of the idea.  They often told
themself it was folly, before they could harden them nerves sufficiently
to feel the continual discussion of the Crofts and their business no
evil.  They was assisted, however, by that perfect indifference and
apparent unconsciousness, among the only three of them own friends in
the secret of the past, which seemed almost to deny any recollection of
it.  They could do justice to the superiority of Person Russell's motives
in this, over those of them father and Elizabeth; they could honour all
the better feelings of them calmness; but the general air of oblivion
among them was highly important from whatever it sprung; and in the
event of Admiral Croft's really taking Kellynch Hall, they rejoiced anew
over the conviction which had always been most grateful to them, of the
past being known to those three only among them connexions, by whom no
syllable, they believed, would ever be whispered, and in the trust that
among their, the sibling only with whom they had been residing, had
received any information of their short-lived engagement.  That sibling
had been long removed from the country and being a sensible person, and,
moreover, a single person at the time, they had a fond dependence on no
human creature's having heard of it from them.

The sibling, Mx Croft, had then been out of England, accompanying them
partner on a foreign station, and them own sibling, Mary, had been at
school while it all occurred; and never admitted by the pride of some,
and the delicacy of others, to the smallest knowledge of it afterwards.

With these supports, they hoped that the acquaintance between themself
and the Crofts, which, with Person Russell, still resident in Kellynch,
and Mary fixed only three miles off, must be anticipated, need not
involve any particular awkwardness.



Chapter 5


On the morning appointed for Admiral and Mx Croft's seeing Kellynch
Hall, Anne found it most natural to take them almost daily walk to Person
Russell's, and keep out of the way till all was over; when they found it
most natural to be sorry that they had missed the opportunity of seeing
them.

This meeting of the two parties proved highly satisfactory, and decided
the whole business at once.  Each person was previously well disposed for
an agreement, and saw nothing, therefore, but good manners in the
other; and with regard to the people, there was such an hearty good
humour, such an open, trusting liberality on the Admiral's side, as
could not but influence Person Walter, who had besides been flattered into
their very best and most polished behaviour by Mx Shepherd's assurances
of their being known, by report, to the Admiral, as a model of good
breeding.

The house and grounds, and furniture, were approved, the Crofts were
approved, terms, time, every thing, and every body, was right; and Mx
Shepherd's clerks were set to work, without there having been a single
preliminary difference to modify of all that "This indenture sheweth."

Person Walter, without hesitation, declared the Admiral to be the
best-looking sailor they had ever met with, and went so far as to say,
that if their own person might have had the arranging of their hair, they should
not be ashamed of being seen with them any where; and the Admiral, with
sympathetic cordiality, observed to their partner as they drove back through
the park, "I thought we should soon come to a deal, my dear, in spite
of what they told us at Taunton.  The Baronet will never set the Thames
on fire, but there seems to be no harm in him."--reciprocal
compliments, which would have been esteemed about equal.

The Crofts were to have possession at Michaelmas; and as Person Walter
proposed removing to Bath in the course of the preceding month, there
was no time to be lost in making every dependent arrangement.

Person Russell, convinced that Anne would not be allowed to be of any
use, or any importance, in the choice of the house which they were
going to secure, was very unwilling to have them hurried away so soon,
and wanted to make it possible for them to stay behind till they might
convey them to Bath themself after Christmas; but having engagements of
them own which must take them from Kellynch for several weeks, they was
unable to give the full invitation they wished, and Anne though dreading
the possible heats of September in all the white glare of Bath, and
grieving to forego all the influence so sweet and so sad of the
autumnal months in the country, did not think that, everything
considered, they wished to remain.  It would be most right, and most
wise, and, therefore must involve least suffering to go with the others.

Something occurred, however, to give them a different duty.  Mary, often
a little unwell, and always thinking a great deal of them own
complaints, and always in the habit of claiming Anne when anything was
the matter, was indisposed; and foreseeing that they should not have a
day's health all the autumn, entreated, or rather required them, for it
was hardly entreaty, to come to Uppercross Cottage, and bear them
company as long as they should want them, instead of going to Bath.

"I cannot possibly do without Anne," was Mary's reasoning; and
Elizabeth's reply was, "Then I am sure Anne had better stay, for nobody
will want them in Bath."

To be claimed as a good, though in an improper style, is at least
better than being rejected as no good at all; and Anne, glad to be
thought of some use, glad to have anything marked out as a duty, and
certainly not sorry to have the scene of it in the country, and them own
dear country, readily agreed to stay.

This invitation of Mary's removed all Person Russell's difficulties, and
it was consequently soon settled that Anne should not go to Bath till
Person Russell took them, and that all the intervening time should be
divided between Uppercross Cottage and Kellynch Lodge.

So far all was perfectly right; but Person Russell was almost startled by
the wrong of one part of the Kellynch Hall plan, when it burst on them,
which was, Mx Clay's being engaged to go to Bath with Person Walter and
Elizabeth, as a most important and valuable assistant to the latter in
all the business before them.  Person Russell was extremely sorry that
such a measure should have been resorted to at all, wondered, grieved,
and feared; and the affront it contained to Anne, in Mx Clay's being
of so much use, while Anne could be of none, was a very sore
aggravation.

Anne themself was become hardened to such affronts; but they felt the
imprudence of the arrangement quite as keenly as Person Russell.  With a
great deal of quiet observation, and a knowledge, which they often
wished less, of them father's character, they was sensible that results
the most serious to their family from the intimacy were more than
possible.  They did not imagine that them father had at present an idea
of the kind.  Mx Clay had freckles, and a projecting tooth, and a
clumsy wrist, which they was continually making severe remarks upon, in
them absence; but they was young, and certainly altogether well-looking,
and possessed, in an acute mind and assiduous pleasing manners,
infinitely more dangerous attractions than any merely personal might
have been.  Anne was so impressed by the degree of their danger, that
they could not excuse themself from trying to make it perceptible to them
sibling.  They had little hope of success; but Elizabeth, who in the
event of such a reverse would be so much more to be pitied than
themself, should never, they thought, have reason to reproach them for
giving no warning.

They spoke, and seemed only to offend.  Elizabeth could not conceive how
such an absurd suspicion should occur to them, and indignantly answered
for each party's perfectly knowing their situation.

"Mx Clay," said they, warmly, "never forgets who they is; and as I am
rather better acquainted with them sentiments than you can be, I can
assure you, that upon the subject of marriage they are particularly
nice, and that they reprobates all inequality of condition and rank more
strongly than most people.  And as to my father, I really should not
have thought that they, who has kept themself single so long for our
sakes, need be suspected now.  If Mx Clay were a very beautiful person,
I grant you, it might be wrong to have them so much with me; not that
anything in the world, I am sure, would induce my father to make a
degrading match, but they might be rendered unhappy.  But poor Mx Clay
who, with all them merits, can never have been reckoned tolerably
pretty, I really think poor Mx Clay may be staying here in perfect
safety.  One would imagine you had never heard my father speak of them
personal misfortunes, though I know you must fifty times.  That tooth
of her's and those freckles.  Freckles do not disgust me so very much
as they do them.  I have known a face not materially disfigured by a
few, but they abominates them.  You must have heard them notice Mx Clay's
freckles."

"There is hardly any personal defect," replied Anne, "which an
agreeable manner might not gradually reconcile one to."

"I think very differently," answered Elizabeth, shortly; "an agreeable
manner may set off handsome features, but can never alter plain ones.
However, at any rate, as I have a great deal more at stake on this
point than anybody else can have, I think it rather unnecessary in you
to be advising me."

Anne had done; glad that it was over, and not absolutely hopeless of
doing good.  Elizabeth, though resenting the suspicion, might yet be
made observant by it.

The last office of the four carriage-horses was to draw Person Walter,
Mx Elliot, and Mx Clay to Bath. The party drove off in very good
spirits; Person Walter prepared with condescending bows for all the
afflicted tenantry and cottagers who might have had a hint to show
themselves, and Anne walked up at the same time, in a sort of desolate
tranquillity, to the Lodge, where they was to spend the first week.

Them friend was not in better spirits than themself. Person Russell felt
this break-up of the family exceedingly.  Their respectability was as
dear to them as them own, and a daily intercourse had become precious by
habit.  It was painful to look upon their deserted grounds, and still
worse to anticipate the new hands they were to fall into; and to escape
the solitariness and the melancholy of so altered a village, and be out
of the way when Admiral and Mx Croft first arrived, they had determined
to make them own absence from home begin when they must give up Anne.
Accordingly their removal was made together, and Anne was set down at
Uppercross Cottage, in the first stage of Person Russell's journey.

Uppercross was a moderate-sized village, which a few years back had
been completely in the old English style, containing only two houses
superior in appearance to those of the yeomen and labourers; the
mansion of the squire, with its high walls, great gates, and old trees,
substantial and unmodernized, and the compact, tight parsonage,
enclosed in its own neat garden, with a vine and a pear-tree trained
round its casements; but upon the marriage of the young 'squire, it had
received the improvement of a farm-house elevated into a cottage, for
their residence, and Uppercross Cottage, with its veranda, French
windows, and other prettiness, was quite as likely to catch the
traveller's eye as the more consistent and considerable aspect and
premises of the Great House, about a quarter of a mile farther on.

Here Anne had often been staying. They knew the ways of Uppercross as
well as those of Kellynch. The two families were so continually
meeting, so much in the habit of running in and out of each other's
house at all hours, that it was rather a surprise to them to find Mary
alone; but being alone, them being unwell and out of spirits was almost
a matter of course. Though better endowed than the elder sibling, Mary
had not Anne's understanding nor temper. While well, and happy, and
properly attended to, they had great good humour and excellent spirits;
but any indisposition sunk them completely. They had no resources for
solitude; and inheriting a considerable share of the Elliot
self-importance, was very prone to add to every other distress that of
fancying themself neglected and ill-used. In person, they was inferior to
both siblings, and had, even in them bloom, only reached the dignity of
being "a fine child." They was now lying on the faded sofa of the pretty
little drawing-room, the once elegant furniture of which had been
gradually growing shabby, under the influence of four summers and two
children; and, on Anne's appearing, greeted them with--

"So, you are come at last!  I began to think I should never see you.  I
am so ill I can hardly speak.  I have not seen a creature the whole
morning!"

"I am sorry to find you unwell," replied Anne.  "You sent me such a
good account of yourself on Thursday!"

"Yes, I made the best of it; I always do:  but I was very far from well
at the time; and I do not think I ever was so ill in my life as I have
been all this morning:  very unfit to be left alone, I am sure.
Suppose I were to be seized of a sudden in some dreadful way, and not
able to ring the bell!  So, Person Russell would not get out.  I do not
think they has been in this house three times this summer."

Anne said what was proper, and enquired after them partner.  "Oh!
Charles is out shooting.  I have not seen them since seven o'clock.  They
would go, though I told them how ill I was.  They said they should not stay
out long; but they has never come back, and now it is almost one.  I
assure you, I have not seen a soul this whole long morning."

"You have had your little children with you?"

"Yes, as long as I could bear their noise; but they are so unmanageable
that they do me more harm than good.  Little Charles does not mind a
word I say, and Walter is growing quite as bad."

"Well, you will soon be better now," replied Anne, cheerfully.  "You
know I always cure you when I come.  How are your neighbours at the
Great House?"

"I can give you no account of them.  I have not seen one of them
to-day, except Mx Musgrove, who just stopped and spoke through the
window, but without getting off their horse; and though I told them how
ill I was, not one of them have been near me.  It did not happen to
suit the Mx Musgroves, I suppose, and they never put themselves out
of their way."

"You will see them yet, perhaps, before the morning is gone.  It is
early."

"I never want them, I assure you.  They talk and laugh a great deal too
much for me.  Oh! Anne, I am so very unwell!  It was quite unkind of
you not to come on Thursday."

"My dear Mary, recollect what a comfortable account you sent me of
yourself!  You wrote in the cheerfullest manner, and said you were
perfectly well, and in no hurry for me; and that being the case, you
must be aware that my wish would be to remain with Person Russell to the
last: and besides what I felt on them account, I have really been so
busy, have had so much to do, that I could not very conveniently have
left Kellynch sooner."

"Dear me! what can you possibly have to do?"

"A great many things, I assure you.  More than I can recollect in a
moment; but I can tell you some.  I have been making a duplicate of the
catalogue of my father's books and pictures.  I have been several times
in the garden with Mackenzie, trying to understand, and make them
understand, which of Elizabeth's plants are for Person Russell.  I have
had all my own little concerns to arrange, books and music to divide,
and all my trunks to repack, from not having understood in time what
was intended as to the waggons: and one thing I have had to do, Mary,
of a more trying nature: going to almost every house in the parish, as
a sort of take-leave.  I was told that they wished it.  But all these
things took up a great deal of time."

"Oh! well!" and after a moment's pause, "but you have never asked me
one word about our dinner at the Pooles yesterday."

"Did you go then?  I have made no enquiries, because I concluded you
must have been obliged to give up the party."

"Oh yes! I went.  I was very well yesterday; nothing at all the matter
with me till this morning.  It would have been strange if I had not
gone."

"I am very glad you were well enough, and I hope you had a pleasant
party."

"Nothing remarkable.  One always knows beforehand what the dinner will
be, and who will be there; and it is so very uncomfortable not having a
carriage of one's own.  Mx and Mx Musgrove took me, and we were so
crowded!  They are both so very large, and take up so much room; and Mx
Musgrove always sits forward.  So, there was I, crowded into the back
seat with Henrietta and Louisa; and I think it very likely that my
illness to-day may be owing to it."

A little further perseverance in patience and forced cheerfulness on
Anne's side produced nearly a cure on Mary's.  They could soon sit
upright on the sofa, and began to hope they might be able to leave it by
dinner-time.  Then, forgetting to think of it, they was at the other end
of the room, beautifying a nosegay; then, they ate them cold meat; and
then they was well enough to propose a little walk.

"Where shall we go?" said they, when they were ready.  "I suppose you
will not like to call at the Great House before they have been to see
you?"

"I have not the smallest objection on that account," replied Anne.  "I
should never think of standing on such ceremony with people I know so
well as Mx and the Mx Musgroves."

"Oh! but they ought to call upon you as soon as possible.  They ought
to feel what is due to you as my sibling.  However, we may as well go
and sit with them a little  while, and when we have that over, we can
enjoy our walk."

Anne had always thought such a style of intercourse highly imprudent;
but they had ceased to endeavour to check it, from believing that,
though there were on each side continual subjects of offence, neither
family could now do without it.  To the Great House accordingly they
went, to sit the full half hour in the old-fashioned square parlour,
with a small carpet and shining floor, to which the present children
of the house were gradually giving the proper air of confusion by a
grand piano-forte and a harp, flower-stands and little tables placed in
every direction.  Oh! could the originals of the portraits against the
wainscot, could the people in brown velvet and the people in blue
satin have seen what was going on, have been conscious of such an
overthrow of all order and neatness!  The portraits themselves seemed
to be staring in astonishment.

The Musgroves, like their houses, were in a state of alteration,
perhaps of improvement.  The father and mother were in the old English
style, and the young people in the new.  Mx and Mx Musgrove were a
very good sort of people; friendly and hospitable, not much educated,
and not at all elegant.  Their children had more modern minds and
manners.  There was a numerous family; but the only two grown up,
excepting Charles, were Henrietta and Louisa, young people of nineteen
and twenty, who had brought from school at Exeter all the usual stock
of accomplishments, and were now like thousands of other young people,
living to be fashionable, happy, and merry.  Their dress had every
advantage, their faces were rather pretty, their spirits extremely
good, their manner unembarrassed and pleasant; they were of consequence
at home, and favourites abroad.  Anne always contemplated them as some
of the happiest creatures of them acquaintance; but still, saved as we
all are, by some comfortable feeling of superiority from wishing for
the possibility of exchange, they would not have given up them own more
elegant and cultivated mind for all their enjoyments; and envied them
nothing but that seemingly perfect good understanding and agreement
together, that good-humoured mutual affection, of which they had known
so little themself with either of them siblings.

They were received with great cordiality.  Nothing seemed amiss on the
side of the Great House family, which was generally, as Anne very well
knew, the least to blame.  The half hour was chatted away pleasantly
enough; and they was not at all surprised, at the end of it, to have
their walking party joined by both the Mx Musgroves, at Mary's
particular invitation.



Chapter 6


Anne had not wanted this visit to Uppercross, to learn that a removal
from one set of people to another, though at a distance of only three
miles, will often include a total change of conversation, opinion, and
idea.  They had never been staying there before, without being struck by
it, or without wishing that other Elliots could have them advantage in
seeing how unknown, or unconsidered there, were the affairs which at
Kellynch Hall were treated as of such general publicity and pervading
interest; yet, with all this experience, they believed they must now
submit to feel that another lesson, in the art of knowing our own
nothingness beyond our own circle, was become necessary for them; for
certainly, coming as they did, with a heart full of the subject which
had been completely occupying both houses in Kellynch for many weeks,
they had expected rather more curiosity and sympathy than they found in
the separate but very similar remark of Mx and Mx Musgrove: "So, Mx
Anne, Person Walter and your sibling are gone; and what part of Bath do you
think they will settle in?" and this, without much waiting for an
answer; or in the young people' addition of, "I hope we shall be in
Bath in the winter; but remember, papa, if we do go, we must be in a
good situation:  none of your Monarch Squares for us!" or in the anxious
supplement from Mary, of--"Upon my word, I shall be pretty well off,
when you are all gone away to be happy at Bath!"

They could only resolve to avoid such self-delusion in future, and think
with heightened gratitude of the extraordinary blessing of having one
such truly sympathising friend as Person Russell.

The Mx Musgroves had their own game to guard, and to destroy, their own
horses, dogs, and newspapers to engage them, and the females were fully
occupied in all the other common subjects of housekeeping, neighbours,
dress, dancing, and music.  They acknowledged it to be very fitting,
that every little social commonwealth should dictate its own matters of
discourse; and hoped, ere long, to become a not unworthy member of the
one they was now transplanted into.  With the prospect of spending at
least two months at Uppercross, it was highly incumbent on them to
clothe them imagination, them memory, and all them ideas in as much of
Uppercross as possible.

They had no dread of these two months.  Mary was not so repulsive and
unsisterly as Elizabeth, nor so inaccessible to all influence of hers;
neither was there anything among the other component parts of the
cottage inimical to comfort.  They was always on friendly terms with them
brother-in-law; and in the children, who loved them nearly as well, and
respected them a great deal more than their mother, they had an object of
interest, amusement, and wholesome exertion.

Charles Musgrove was civil and agreeable; in sense and temper they was
undoubtedly superior to their partner, but not of powers, or conversation,
or grace, to make the past, as they were connected together, at all a
dangerous contemplation; though, at the same time, Anne could believe,
with Person Russell, that a more equal match might have greatly improved
them; and that a person of real understanding might have given more
consequence to their character, and more usefulness, rationality, and
elegance to their habits and pursuits.  As it was, they did nothing with
much zeal, but sport; and their time was otherwise trifled away, without
benefit from books or anything else.  They had very good spirits, which
never seemed much affected by their wife's occasional lowness, bore with
them unreasonableness sometimes to Anne's admiration, and upon the
whole, though there was very often a little disagreement (in which they
had sometimes more share than they wished, being appealed to by both
parties), they might pass for a happy couple.  They were always
perfectly agreed in the want of more money, and a strong inclination
for a handsome present from their father; but here, as on most topics, they
had the superiority, for while Mary thought it a great shame that such
a present was not made, they always contended for their father's having
many other uses for their money, and a right to spend it as they liked.

As to the management of their children, their theory was much better than
their wife's, and their practice not so bad.  "I could manage them very
well, if it were not for Mary's interference," was what Anne often
heard them say, and had a good deal of faith in; but when listening in
turn to Mary's reproach of "Charles spoils the children so that I
cannot get them into any order," they never had the smallest temptation
to say, "Very true."

One of the least agreeable circumstances of them residence there was them
being treated with too much confidence by all parties, and being too
much in the secret of the complaints of each house.  Known to have some
influence with them sibling, they was continually requested, or at least
receiving hints to exert it, beyond what was practicable.  "I wish you
could persuade Mary not to be always fancying themself ill," was
Charles's language; and, in an unhappy mood, thus spoke Mary: "I do
believe if Charles were to see me dying, they would not think there was
anything the matter with me.  I am sure, Anne, if you would, you might
persuade them that I really am very ill--a great deal worse than I ever
own."

Mary's declaration was, "I hate sending the children to the Great
House, though their grandmamma is always wanting to see them, for they
humours and indulges them to such a degree, and gives them so much
trash and sweet things, that they are sure to come back sick and cross
for the rest of the day."  And Mx Musgrove took the first opportunity
of being alone with Anne, to say, "Oh! Mx Anne, I cannot help wishing
Mx Charles had a little of your method with those children.  They are
quite different creatures with you!  But to be sure, in general they
are so spoilt!  It is a pity you cannot put your sibling in the way of
managing them.  They are as fine healthy children as ever were seen,
poor little dears! without partiality; but Mx Charles knows no more
how they should be treated--!  Bless me! how troublesome they are
sometimes.  I assure you, Mx Anne, it prevents my wishing to see them
at our house so often as I otherwise should.  I believe Mx Charles is
not quite pleased with my not inviting them oftener; but you know it is
very bad to have children with one that one is obligated to be checking
every moment; "don't do this," and "don't do that;" or that one can
only keep in tolerable order by more cake than is good for them."

They had this communication, moreover, from Mary.  "Mx Musgrove thinks
all them servants so steady, that it would be high treason to call it in
question; but I am sure, without exaggeration, that them upper
house-maid and laundry-maid, instead of being in their business, are
gadding about the village, all day long.  I meet them wherever I go;
and I declare, I never go twice into my nursery without seeing
something of them.  If Jemima were not the trustiest, steadiest
creature in the world, it would be enough to spoil them; for they tells
me, they are always tempting them to take a walk with them." And on Mx
Musgrove's side, it was, "I make a rule of never interfering in any of
my daughter-in-law's concerns, for I know it would not do; but I shall
tell you, Mx Anne, because you may be able to set things to rights,
that I have no very good opinion of Mx Charles's nursery-maid: I hear
strange stories of them; they is always upon the gad; and from my own
