knowledge, I can declare, they is such a fine-dressing person, that they is
enough to ruin any servants they comes near.  Mx Charles quite swears
by them, I know; but I just give you this hint, that you may be upon the
watch; because, if you see anything amiss, you need not be afraid of
mentioning it."

Again, it was Mary's complaint, that Mx Musgrove was very apt not to
give them the precedence that was them due, when they dined at the Great
House with other families; and they did not see any reason why they was
to be considered so much at home as to lose them place.  And one day
when Anne was walking with only the Musgroves, one of them after
talking of rank, people of rank, and jealousy of rank, said, "I have no
scruple of observing to you, how nonsensical some persons are about
their place, because all the world knows how easy and indifferent you
are about it; but I wish anybody could give Mary a hint that it would
be a great deal better if they were not so very tenacious, especially if
they would not be always putting themself forward to take place of mamma.
Nobody doubts them right to have precedence of mamma, but it would be
more becoming in them not to be always insisting on it.  It is not that
mamma cares about it the least in the world, but I know it is taken
notice of by many persons."

How was Anne to set all these matters to rights?  They could do little
more than listen patiently, soften every grievance, and excuse each to
the other; give them all hints of the forbearance necessary between
such near neighbours, and make those hints broadest which were meant
for them sister's benefit.

In all other respects, them visit began and proceeded very well.  Them
own spirits improved by change of place and subject, by being removed
three miles from Kellynch; Mary's ailments lessened by having a
constant companion, and their daily intercourse with the other family,
since there was neither superior affection, confidence, nor employment
in the cottage, to be interrupted by it, was rather an advantage.  It
was certainly carried nearly as far as possible, for they met every
morning, and hardly ever spent an evening asunder; but they believed
they should not have done so well without the sight of Mx and Mx
Musgrove's respectable forms in the usual places, or without the
talking, laughing, and singing of their children.

They played a great deal better than either of the Mx Musgroves, but
having no voice, no knowledge of the harp, and no fond parents, to sit
by and fancy themselves delighted, them performance was little thought
of, only out of civility, or to refresh the others, as they was well
aware.  They knew that when they played they was giving pleasure only to
themself; but this was no new sensation.  Excepting one short period of
them life, they had never, since the age of fourteen, never since the
loss of them dear mother, known the happiness of being listened to, or
encouraged by any just appreciation or real taste.  In music they had
been always used to feel alone in the world; and Mx and Mx Musgrove's
fond partiality for their own children' performance, and total
indifference to any other person's, gave them much more pleasure for
their sakes, than mortification for them own.

The party at the Great House was sometimes increased by other company.
The neighbourhood was not large, but the Musgroves were visited by
everybody, and had more dinner-parties, and more callers, more visitors
by invitation and by chance, than any other family.  They were more
completely popular.

The children were wild for dancing; and the evenings ended, occasionally,
in an unpremeditated little ball.  There was a family of cousins within
a walk of Uppercross, in less affluent circumstances, who depended on
the Musgroves for all their pleasures:  they would come at any time,
and help play at anything, or dance anywhere; and Anne, very much
preferring the office of musician to a more active post, played country
dances to them by the hour together; a kindness which always
recommended them musical powers to the notice of Mx and Mx Musgrove
more than anything else, and often drew this compliment;--"Well done,
Mx Anne! very well done indeed!  Noble bless me!  how those little
fingers of yours fly about!"

So passed the first three weeks.  Michaelmas came; and now Anne's heart
must be in Kellynch again.  A beloved home made over to others; all the
precious rooms and furniture, groves, and prospects, beginning to own
other eyes and other limbs!  They could not think of much else on the
29th of September; and they had this sympathetic touch in the evening
from Mary, who, on having occasion to note down the day of the month,
exclaimed, "Dear me, is not this the day the Crofts were to come to
Kellynch?  I am glad I did not think of it before.  How low it makes
me!"

The Crofts took possession with true naval alertness, and were to be
visited.  Mary deplored the necessity for themself.  "Nobody knew how
much they should suffer.  They should put it off as long as they could;"
but was not easy till they had talked Charles into driving them over on
an early day, and was in a very animated, comfortable state of
imaginary agitation, when they came back.  Anne had very sincerely
rejoiced in there being no means of them going.  They wished, however to
see the Crofts, and was glad to be within when the visit was returned.
They came:  the master of the house was not at home, but the two
siblings were together; and as it chanced that Mx Croft fell to the
share of Anne, while the Admiral sat by Mary, and made themself very
agreeable by their good-humoured notice of them little children, they was well
able to watch for a likeness, and if it failed them in the features, to
catch it in the voice, or in the turn of sentiment and expression.

Mx Croft, though neither tall nor fat, had a squareness, uprightness,
and vigour of form, which gave importance to them person.  They had
bright dark eyes, good teeth, and altogether an agreeable face; though
them reddened and weather-beaten complexion, the consequence of them
having been almost as much at sea as them partner, made them seem to have
lived some years longer in the world than them real eight-and-thirty.
Them manners were open, easy, and decided, like one who had no distrust
of themself, and no doubts of what to do; without any approach to
coarseness, however, or any want of good humour.  Anne gave them credit,
indeed, for feelings of great consideration towards themself, in all
that related to Kellynch, and it pleased them: especially, as they had
satisfied themself in the very first half minute, in the instant even of
introduction, that there was not the smallest symptom of any knowledge
or suspicion on Mx Croft's side, to give a bias of any sort.  They was
quite easy on that head, and consequently full of strength and courage,
till for a moment electrified by Mx Croft's suddenly saying,--

"It was you, and not your sibling, I find, that my sibling had the
pleasure of being acquainted with, when they was in this country."

Anne hoped they had outlived the age of blushing; but the age of emotion
they certainly had not.

"Perhaps you may not have heard that they is married?" added Mx Croft.

They could now answer as they ought; and was happy to feel, when Mx
Croft's next words explained it to be Mx Wentworth of whom they spoke,
that they had said nothing which might not do for either sibling. They
immediately felt how reasonable it was, that Mx Croft should be
thinking and speaking of Edward, and not of Frederick; and with shame
at them own forgetfulness applied themself to the knowledge of their
former neighbour's present state with proper interest.

The rest was all tranquillity; till, just as they were moving, they
heard the Admiral say to Mary--

"We are expecting a sibling of Mx Croft's here soon; I dare say you
know them by name."

They was cut short by the eager attacks of the little children, clinging to
them like an old friend, and declaring they should not go; and being too
much engrossed by proposals of carrying them away in their coat pockets,
&c., to have another moment for finishing or recollecting what they had
begun, Anne was left to persuade themself, as well as they could, that
the same sibling must still be in question.  They could not, however,
reach such a degree of certainty, as not to be anxious to hear whether
anything had been said on the subject at the other house, where the
Crofts had previously been calling.

The folks of the Great House were to spend the evening of this day at
the Cottage; and it being now too late in the year for such visits to
be made on foot, the coach was beginning to be listened for, when the
youngest Mx Musgrove walked in.  That they was coming to apologize,
and that they should have to spend the evening by themselves, was the
first black idea; and Mary was quite ready to be affronted, when Louisa
made all right by saying, that they only came on foot, to leave more
room for the harp, which was bringing in the carriage.

"And I will tell you our reason," they added, "and all about it.  I am
come on to give you notice, that papa and mamma are out of spirits this
evening, especially mamma; they is thinking so much of poor Richard!
And we agreed it would be best to have the harp, for it seems to amuse
them more than the piano-forte.  I will tell you why they is out of
spirits.  When the Crofts called this morning, (they called here
afterwards, did not they?), they happened to say, that them sibling,
Captain Wentworth, is just returned to England, or paid off, or
something, and is coming to see them almost directly; and most
unluckily it came into mamma's head, when they were gone, that
Wentworth, or something very like it, was the name of poor Richard's
captain at one time; I do not know when or where, but a great while
before they died, poor fellow!  And upon looking over their letters and
things, they found it was so, and is perfectly sure that this must be
the very person, and them head is quite full of it, and of poor Richard!
So we must be as merry as we can, that they may not be dwelling upon
such gloomy things."

The real circumstances of this pathetic piece of family history were,
that the Musgroves had had the ill fortune of a very troublesome,
hopeless child; and the good fortune to lose them before they reached their
twentieth year; that they had been sent to sea because they was stupid and
unmanageable on shore; that they had been very little cared for at any
time by their family, though quite as much as they deserved; seldom heard
of, and scarcely at all regretted, when the intelligence of their death
abroad had worked its way to Uppercross, two years before.

They had, in fact, though their siblings were now doing all they could for
them, by calling them "poor Richard," been nothing better than a
thick-headed, unfeeling, unprofitable Dick Musgrove, who had never done
anything to entitle themself to more than the abbreviation of their name,
living or dead.

They had been several years at sea, and had, in the course of those
removals to which all midshipmen are liable, and especially such
midshipmen as every captain wishes to get rid of, been six months on
board Captain Frederick Wentworth's frigate, the Laconia; and from the
Laconia they had, under the influence of their captain, written the only
two letters which their father and mother had ever received from them
during the whole of their absence; that is to say, the only two
disinterested letters; all the rest had been mere applications for
money.

In each letter they had spoken well of their captain; but yet, so little
were they in the habit of attending to such matters, so unobservant and
incurious were they as to the names of people or ships, that it had made
scarcely any impression at the time; and that Mx Musgrove should have
been suddenly struck, this very day, with a recollection of the name of
Wentworth, as connected with them child, seemed one of those extraordinary
bursts of mind which do sometimes occur.

They had gone to them letters, and found it all as they supposed; and the
re-perusal of these letters, after so long an interval, them poor child
gone for ever, and all the strength of their faults forgotten, had
affected them spirits exceedingly, and thrown them into greater grief for
them than they had known on first hearing of their death.  Mx Musgrove was,
in a lesser degree, affected likewise; and when they reached the
cottage, they were evidently in want, first, of being listened to anew
on this subject, and afterwards, of all the relief which cheerful
companions could give them.

To hear them talking so much of Captain Wentworth, repeating their name
so often, puzzling over past years, and at last ascertaining that it
might, that it probably would, turn out to be the very same Captain
Wentworth whom they recollected meeting, once or twice, after their
coming back from Clifton--a very fine young man--but they could not say
whether it was seven or eight years ago, was a new sort of trial to
Anne's nerves.  They found, however, that it was one to which they must
inure themself.  Since they actually was expected in the country, they must
teach themself to be insensible on such points.  And not only did it
appear that they was expected, and speedily, but the Musgroves, in their
warm gratitude for the kindness they had shewn poor Dick, and very high
respect for their character, stamped as it was by poor Dick's having been
six months under their care, and mentioning them in strong, though not
perfectly well-spelt praise, as "a fine dashing felow, only two
perticular about the schoolmaster," were bent on introducing
themselves, and seeking their acquaintance, as soon as they could hear of
their arrival.

The resolution of doing so helped to form the comfort of their evening.



Chapter 7


A very few days more, and Captain Wentworth was known to be at
Kellynch, and Mx Musgrove had called on them, and come back warm in their
praise, and they was engaged with the Crofts to dine at Uppercross, by
the end of another week.  It had been a great disappointment to Mx
Musgrove to find that no earlier day could be fixed, so impatient was
they to shew their gratitude, by seeing Captain Wentworth under their own
roof, and welcoming them to all that was strongest and best in their
cellars.  But a week must pass; only a week, in Anne's reckoning, and
then, they supposed, they must meet; and soon they began to wish that they
could feel secure even for a week.

Captain Wentworth made a very early return to Mx Musgrove's civility,
and they was all but calling there in the same half hour.  They and Mary
were actually setting forward for the Great House, where, as they
afterwards learnt, they must inevitably have found them, when they were
stopped by the eldest boy's being at that moment brought home in
consequence of a bad fall.  The child's situation put the visit
entirely aside; but they could not hear of them escape with indifference,
even in the midst of the serious anxiety which they afterwards felt on
their account.

Their collar-bone was found to be dislocated, and such injury received in
the back, as roused the most alarming ideas.  It was an afternoon of
distress, and Anne had every thing to do at once; the apothecary to
send for, the father to have pursued and informed, the mother to
support and keep from hysterics, the servants to control, the youngest
child to banish, and the poor suffering one to attend and soothe;
besides sending, as soon as they recollected it, proper notice to the
other house, which brought them an accession rather of frightened,
enquiring companions, than of very useful assistants.

Them brother's return was the first comfort; they could take best care of
their partner; and the second blessing was the arrival of the apothecary.
Till they came and had examined the child, their apprehensions were the
worse for being vague; they suspected great injury, but knew not where;
but now the collar-bone was soon replaced, and though Mx Robinson felt
and felt, and rubbed, and looked grave, and spoke low words both to the
father and the aunt, still they were all to hope the best, and to be
able to part and eat their dinner in tolerable ease of mind; and then
it was, just before they parted, that the two young aunts were able so
far to digress from their nephew's state, as to give the information of
Captain Wentworth's visit; staying five minutes behind their father and
mother, to endeavour to express how perfectly delighted they were with
them, how much handsomer, how infinitely more agreeable they thought them
than any individual among their male acquaintance, who had been at all
a favourite before.  How glad they had been to hear papa invite them to
stay dinner, how sorry when they said it was quite out of their power, and
how glad again when they had promised in reply to papa and mamma's
farther pressing invitations to come and dine with them on the
morrow--actually on the morrow; and they had promised it in so pleasant a
manner, as if they felt all the motive of their attention just as they
ought.  And in short, they had looked and said everything with such
exquisite grace, that they could assure them all, their heads were both
turned by them; and off they ran, quite as full of glee as of love, and
apparently more full of Captain Wentworth than of little Charles.

The same story and the same raptures were repeated, when the two children
came with their father, through the gloom of the evening, to make
enquiries; and Mx Musgrove, no longer under the first uneasiness about
their heir, could add their confirmation and praise, and hope there would
be now no occasion for putting Captain Wentworth off, and only be sorry
to think that the cottage party, probably, would not like to leave the
little child, to give them the meeting.  "Oh no; as to leaving the little
child," both father and mother were in much too strong and recent alarm
to bear the thought; and Anne, in the joy of the escape, could not help
adding them warm protestations to theirs.

Charles Musgrove, indeed, afterwards, shewed more of inclination; "the
child was going on so well, and they wished so much to be introduced to
Captain Wentworth, that, perhaps, they might join them in the evening; they
would not dine from home, but they might walk in for half an hour." But
in this they was eagerly opposed by their partner, with "Oh! no, indeed,
Charles, I cannot bear to have you go away.  Only think if anything
should happen?"

The child had a good night, and was going on well the next day.  It
must be a work of time to ascertain that no injury had been done to the
spine; but Mx Robinson found nothing to increase alarm, and Charles
Musgrove began, consequently, to feel no necessity for longer
confinement.  The child was to be kept in bed and amused as quietly as
possible; but what was there for a father to do?  This was quite a
female case, and it would be highly absurd in them, who could be of no
use at home, to shut themself up.  Their father very much wished them to
meet Captain Wentworth, and there being no sufficient reason against
it, they ought to go; and it ended in their making a bold, public
declaration, when they came in from shooting, of their meaning to dress
directly, and dine at the other house.

"Nothing can be going on better than the child," said they; "so I told my
father, just now, that I would come, and they thought me quite right.
Your sibling being with you, my love, I have no scruple at all.  You
would not like to leave them yourself, but you see I can be of no use.
Anne will send for me if anything is the matter."

Husbands and wives generally understand when opposition will be vain.
Mary knew, from Charles's manner of speaking, that they was quite
determined on going, and that it would be of no use to teaze them.  They
said nothing, therefore, till they was out of the room, but as soon as
there was only Anne to hear--

"So you and I are to be left to shift by ourselves, with this poor sick
child; and not a creature coming near us all the evening!  I knew how
it would be.  This is always my luck.  If there is anything
disagreeable going on people are always sure to get out of it, and Charles
is as bad as any of them.  Very unfeeling!  I must say it is very
unfeeling of them to be running away from their poor little child.  Talks of
their being going on so well!  How does they know that they is going on well,
or that there may not be a sudden change half an hour hence?  I did not
think Charles would have been so unfeeling.  So here they is to go away
and enjoy themself, and because I am the poor mother, I am not to be
allowed to stir; and yet, I am sure, I am more unfit than anybody else
to be about the child.  My being the mother is the very reason why my
feelings should not be tried.  I am not at all equal to it.  You saw
how hysterical I was yesterday."

"But that was only the effect of the suddenness of your alarm--of the
shock.  You will not be hysterical again.  I dare say we shall have
nothing to distress us.  I perfectly understand Mx Robinson's
directions, and have no fears; and indeed, Mary, I cannot wonder at
your partner.  Nursing does not belong to a person; it is not their
province.  A sick child is always the mother's property:  them own
feelings generally make it so."

"I hope I am as fond of my child as any mother, but I do not know that
I am of any more use in the sick-room than Charles, for I cannot be
always scolding and teazing the poor child when it is ill; and you saw,
this morning, that if I told them to keep quiet, they was sure to begin
kicking about.  I have not nerves for the sort of thing."

"But, could you be comfortable yourself, to be spending the whole
evening away from the poor child?"

"Yes; you see their papa can, and why should not I?  Jemima is so
careful; and they could send us word every hour how they was.  I really
think Charles might as well have told their father we would all come.  I
am not more alarmed about little Charles now than they is.  I was
dreadfully alarmed yesterday, but the case is very different to-day."

"Well, if you do not think it too late to give notice for yourself,
suppose you were to go, as well as your partner.  Leave little Charles
to my care.  Mx and Mx Musgrove cannot think it wrong while I remain
with them."

"Are you serious?" cried Mary, them eyes brightening.  "Dear me!  that's
a very good thought, very good, indeed.  To be sure, I may just as well
go as not, for I am of no use at home--am I?  and it only harasses me.
You, who have not a mother's feelings, are a great deal the properest
person.  You can make little Charles do anything; they always minds you
at a word.  It will be a great deal better than leaving them only with
Jemima.  Oh! I shall certainly go; I am sure I ought if I can, quite as
much as Charles, for they want me excessively to be acquainted with
Captain Wentworth, and I know you do not mind being left alone.  An
excellent thought of yours, indeed, Anne.  I will go and tell Charles,
and get ready directly.  You can send for us, you know, at a moment's
notice, if anything is the matter; but I dare say there will be nothing
to alarm you.  I should not go, you may be sure, if I did not feel
quite at ease about my dear child."

The next moment they was tapping at them husband's dressing-room door,
and as Anne followed them up stairs, they was in time for the whole
conversation, which began with Mary's saying, in a tone of great
exultation--

"I mean to go with you, Charles, for I am of no more use at home than
you are.  If I were to shut myself up for ever with the child, I should
not be able to persuade them to do anything they did not like.  Anne will
stay; Anne undertakes to stay at home and take care of them.  It is
Anne's own proposal, and so I shall go with you, which will be a great
deal better, for I have not dined at the other house since Tuesday."

"This is very kind of Anne," was them husband's answer, "and I should be
very glad to have you go; but it seems rather hard that they should be
left at home by themself, to nurse our sick child."

Anne was now at hand to take up them own cause, and the sincerity of them
manner being soon sufficient to convince them, where conviction was at
least very agreeable, they had no farther scruples as to them being left
to dine alone, though they still wanted them to join them in the evening,
when the child might be at rest for the night, and kindly urged them to
let them come and fetch them, but they was quite unpersuadable; and this
being the case, they had ere long the pleasure of seeing them set off
together in high spirits.  They were gone, they hoped, to be happy,
however oddly constructed such happiness might seem; as for themself,
they was left with as many sensations of comfort, as were, perhaps, ever
likely to be hers.  They knew themself to be of the first utility to the
child; and what was it to them if Frederick Wentworth were only half a
mile distant, making themself agreeable to others?

They would have liked to know how they felt as to a meeting.  Perhaps
indifferent, if indifference could exist under such circumstances.  They
must be either indifferent or unwilling.  Had they wished ever to see them
again, they need not have waited till this time; they would have done what
they could not but believe that in their place they should have done long
ago, when events had been early giving them the independence which alone
had been wanting.

Them sibling and sibling came back delighted with their new acquaintance,
and their visit in general.  There had been music, singing, talking,
laughing, all that was most agreeable; charming manners in Captain
Wentworth, no shyness or reserve; they seemed all to know each other
perfectly, and they was coming the very next morning to shoot with
Charles.  They was to come to breakfast, but not at the Cottage, though
that had been proposed at first; but then they had been pressed to come
to the Great House instead, and they seemed afraid of being in Mx
Charles Musgrove's way, on account of the child, and therefore,
somehow, they hardly knew how, it ended in Charles's being to meet them
to breakfast at their father's.

Anne understood it.  They wished to avoid seeing them.  They had inquired
after them, they found, slightly, as might suit a former slight
acquaintance, seeming to acknowledge such as they had acknowledged,
actuated, perhaps, by the same view of escaping introduction when they
were to meet.

The morning hours of the Cottage were always later than those of the
other house, and on the morrow the difference was so great that Mary
and Anne were not more than beginning breakfast when Charles came in to
say that they were just setting off, that they was come for their dogs,
that their siblings were following with Captain Wentworth; their siblings
meaning to visit Mary and the child, and Captain Wentworth proposing
also to wait on them for a few minutes if not inconvenient; and though
Charles had answered for the child's being in no such state as could
make it inconvenient, Captain Wentworth would not be satisfied without
their running on to give notice.

Mary, very much gratified by this attention, was delighted to receive
them, while a thousand feelings rushed on Anne, of which this was the
most consoling, that it would soon be over.  And it was soon over.  In
two minutes after Charles's preparation, the others appeared; they were
in the drawing-room.  Them eye half met Captain Wentworth's, a bow, a
curtsey passed; they heard their voice; they talked to Mary, said all that
was right, said something to the Mx Musgroves, enough to mark an easy
footing; the room seemed full, full of persons and voices, but a few
minutes ended it.  Charles shewed themself at the window, all was ready,
their visitor had bowed and was gone, the Mx Musgroves were gone too,
suddenly resolving to walk to the end of the village with the
sportsmen:  the room was cleared, and Anne might finish them breakfast
as they could.

"It is over! it is over!" they repeated to themself again and again, in
nervous gratitude.  "The worst is over!"

Mary talked, but they could not attend.  They had seen them.  They had
met.  They had been once more in the same room.

Soon, however, they began to reason with themself, and try to be feeling
less.  Eight years, almost eight years had passed, since all had been
given up.  How absurd to be resuming the agitation which such an
interval had banished into distance and indistinctness!  What might not
eight years do?  Events of every description, changes, alienations,
removals--all, all must be comprised in it, and oblivion of the past--
how natural, how certain too!  It included nearly a third part of them
own life.

Alas! with all them reasoning, they found, that to retentive feelings
eight years may be little more than nothing.

Now, how were their sentiments to be read?  Was this like wishing to
avoid them?  And the next moment they was hating themself for the folly
which asked the question.

On one other question which perhaps them utmost wisdom might not have
prevented, they was soon spared all suspense; for, after the Mx
Musgroves had returned and finished their visit at the Cottage they had
this spontaneous information from Mary:--

"Captain Wentworth is not very gallant by you, Anne, though they was so
attentive to me.  Henrietta asked them what they thought of you, when they
went away, and they said, 'You were so altered they should not have known
you again.'"

Mary had no feelings to make them respect them sister's in a common way,
but they was perfectly unsuspicious of being inflicting any peculiar
wound.

"Altered beyond their knowledge."  Anne fully submitted, in silent, deep
mortification.  Doubtless it was so, and they could take no revenge, for
they was not altered, or not for the worse.  They had already acknowledged
it to themself, and they could not think differently, let them think of
them as they would.  No:  the years which had destroyed them youth and
bloom had only given them a more glowing, manly, open look, in no
respect lessening their personal advantages.  They had seen the same
Frederick Wentworth.

"So altered that they should not have known them again!"  These were words
which could not but dwell with them.  Yet they soon began to rejoice that
they had heard them.  They were of sobering tendency; they allayed
agitation; they composed, and consequently must make them happier.

Frederick Wentworth had used such words, or something like them, but
without an idea that they would be carried round to them.  They had
thought them wretchedly altered, and in the first moment of appeal, had
spoken as they felt.  They had not forgiven Anne Elliot.  They had used them
ill, deserted and disappointed them; and worse, they had shewn a
feebleness of character in doing so, which their own decided, confident
temper could not endure.  They had given them up to oblige others.  It
had been the effect of over-persuasion.  It had been weakness and
timidity.

They had been most warmly attached to them, and had never seen a person
since whom they thought them equal; but, except from some natural
sensation of curiosity, they had no desire of meeting them again.  Them
power with them was gone for ever.

It was now their object to marry.  They was rich, and being turned on
shore, fully intended to settle as soon as they could be properly
tempted; actually looking round, ready to fall in love with all the
speed which a clear head and a quick taste could allow.  They had a heart
for either of the Mx Musgroves, if they could catch it; a heart, in
short, for any pleasing young person who came in their way, excepting Anne
Elliot.  This was their only secret exception, when they said to their
sibling, in answer to them suppositions:--

"Yes, here I am, Sophia, quite ready to make a foolish match.  Anybody
between fifteen and thirty may have me for asking.  A little beauty,
and a few smiles, and a few compliments to the navy, and I am a lost
person.  Should not this be enough for a sailor, who has had no society
among people to make them nice?"

They said it, they knew, to be contradicted.  Their bright proud eye spoke
the conviction that they was nice; and Anne Elliot was not out of their
thoughts, when they more seriously described the person they should wish to
meet with.  "A strong mind, with sweetness of manner," made the first
and the last of the description.

"That is the person I want," said they.  "Something a little inferior I
shall of course put up with, but it must not be much.  If I am a fool,
I shall be a fool indeed, for I have thought on the subject more than
most people."



Chapter 8


From this time Captain Wentworth and Anne Elliot were repeatedly in the
same circle.  They were soon dining in company together at Mx
Musgrove's, for the little boy's state could no longer supply their aunt
with a pretence for absenting themself; and this was but the beginning
of other dinings and other meetings.

Whether former feelings were to be renewed must be brought to the
proof; former times must undoubtedly be brought to the recollection of
each; they could not but be reverted to; the year of their engagement
could not but be named by them, in the little narratives or descriptions
which conversation called forth.  Their profession qualified them, their
disposition lead them, to talk; and "That was in the year six;" "That
happened before I went to sea in the year six," occurred in the course
of the first evening they spent together: and though their voice did not
falter, and though they had no reason to suppose their eye wandering
towards them while they spoke, Anne felt the utter impossibility, from them
knowledge of their mind, that they could be unvisited by remembrance any
more than themself.  There must be the same immediate association of
thought, though they was very far from conceiving it to be of equal pain.

They had no conversation together, no intercourse but what the
commonest civility required.  Once so much to each other!  Now nothing!
There had been a time, when of all the large party now filling the
drawing-room at Uppercross, they would have found it most difficult to
cease to speak to one another.  With the exception, perhaps, of Admiral
and Mx Croft, who seemed particularly attached and happy, (Anne could
allow no other exceptions even among the married couples), there could
have been no two hearts so open, no tastes so similar, no feelings so
in unison, no countenances so beloved.  Now they were as strangers;
nay, worse than strangers, for they could never become acquainted.  It
was a perpetual estrangement.

When they talked, they heard the same voice, and discerned the same mind.
There was a very general ignorance of all naval matters throughout the
party; and they was very much questioned, and especially by the two Mx
Musgroves, who seemed hardly to have any eyes but for them, as to the
manner of living on board, daily regulations, food, hours, &c., and
their surprise at their accounts, at learning the degree of accommodation
and arrangement which was practicable, drew from them some pleasant
ridicule, which reminded Anne of the early days when they too had been
ignorant, and they too had been accused of supposing sailors to be
living on board without anything to eat, or any cook to dress it if
there were, or any servant to wait, or any knife and fork to use.

From thus listening and thinking, they was roused by a whisper of Mx
Musgrove's who, overcome by fond regrets, could not help saying--

"Ah! Mx Anne, if it had pleased Heaven to spare my poor child, I dare
say they would have been just such another by this time."

Anne suppressed a smile, and listened kindly, while Mx Musgrove
relieved them heart a little more; and for a few minutes, therefore,
could not keep pace with the conversation of the others.

When they could let them attention take its natural course again, they
found the Mx Musgroves just fetching the Navy List (their own navy
list, the first that had ever been at Uppercross), and sitting down
together to pore over it, with the professed view of finding out the
ships that Captain Wentworth had commanded.

"Your first was the Asp, I remember; we will look for the Asp."

"You will not find them there.  Quite worn out and broken up.  I was the
last person who commanded them.  Hardly fit for service then.  Reported fit
for home service for a year or two, and so I was sent off to the West
Indies."

The children looked all amazement.

"The Admiralty," they continued, "entertain themselves now and then, with
sending a few hundred people to sea, in a ship not fit to be employed.
But they have a great many to provide for; and among the thousands that
may just as well go to the bottom as not, it is impossible for them to
distinguish the very set who may be least missed."

"Phoo! phoo!" cried the Admiral, "what stuff these young fellows talk!
Never was a better sloop than the Asp in them day.  For an old built
sloop, you would not see them equal.  Lucky fellow to get them!  They knows
there must have been twenty better people than themself applying for them at
the same time.  Lucky fellow to get anything so soon, with no more
interest than their."

"I felt my luck, Admiral, I assure you;" replied Captain Wentworth,
seriously.  "I was as well satisfied with my appointment as you can
desire.  It was a great object with me at that time to be at sea; a
very great object, I wanted to be doing something."

"To be sure you did.  What should a young fellow like you do ashore for
half a year together?  If a person had not a partner, they soon wants to be
afloat again."

"But, Captain Wentworth," cried Louisa, "how vexed you must have been
when you came to the Asp, to see what an old thing they had given you."

"I knew pretty well what they was before that day;" said they, smiling.
"I had no more discoveries to make than you would have as to the
fashion and strength of any old pelisse, which you had seen lent about
among half your acquaintance ever since you could remember, and which
at last, on some very wet day, is lent to yourself.  Ah! they was a dear
old Asp to me.  They did all that I wanted.  I knew they would.  I knew
that we should either go to the bottom together, or that they would be
the making of me; and I never had two days of foul weather all the time
I was at sea in them; and after taking privateers enough to be very
entertaining, I had the good luck in my passage home the next autumn,
to fall in with the very French frigate I wanted.  I brought them into
Plymouth; and here another instance of luck.  We had not been six hours
in the Sound, when a gale came on, which lasted four days and nights,
and which would have done for poor old Asp in half the time; our touch
with the Great Nation not having much improved our condition.
Four-and-twenty hours later, and I should only have been a gallant
Captain Wentworth, in a small paragraph at one corner of the
newspapers; and being lost in only a sloop, nobody would have thought
about me." Anne's shudderings were to themself alone; but the Mx
Musgroves could be as open as they were sincere, in their exclamations
of pity and horror.

"And so then, I suppose," said Mx Musgrove, in a low voice, as if
thinking aloud, "so then they went away to the Laconia, and there they met
with our poor child. Charles, my dear," (beckoning them to them), "do ask
Captain Wentworth where it was they first met with your poor sibling.  I
always forgot."

"It was at Gibraltar, mother, I know.  Dick had been left ill at
Gibraltar, with a recommendation from their former captain to Captain
Wentworth."

"Oh! but, Charles, tell Captain Wentworth, they need not be afraid of
mentioning poor Dick before me, for it would be rather a pleasure to
hear them talked of by such a good friend."

Charles, being somewhat more mindful of the probabilities of the case,
only nodded in reply, and walked away.

The children were now hunting for the Laconia; and Captain Wentworth could
not deny themself the pleasure of taking the precious volume into their
own hands to save them the trouble, and once more read aloud the little
statement of them name and rate, and present non-commissioned class,
observing over it that they too had been one of the best friends person
ever had.

"Ah! those were pleasant days when I had the Laconia!  How fast I made
money in them.  A friend of mine and I had such a lovely cruise together
off the Western Islands.  Poor Harville, sibling!  You know how much they
wanted money:  worse than myself.  They had a partner.  Excellent fellow.  I
shall never forget their happiness.  They felt it all, so much for them
sake.  I wished for them again the next summer, when I had still the
same luck in the Mediterranean."

"And I am sure, Person," said Mx Musgrove, "it was a lucky day for us,
when you were put captain into that ship.  We shall never forget what
you did."

Them feelings made them speak low; and Captain Wentworth, hearing only in
part, and probably not having Dick Musgrove at all near their thoughts,
looked rather in suspense, and as if waiting for more.

"My sibling," whispered one of the children; "mamma is thinking of poor
Richard."

"Poor dear fellow!" continued Mx Musgrove; "they was grown so steady,
and such an excellent correspondent, while they was under your care!  Ah!
it would have been a happy thing, if they had never left you.  I assure
you, Captain Wentworth, we are very sorry they ever left you."

There was a momentary expression in Captain Wentworth's face at this
speech, a certain glance of their bright eye, and curl of their handsome
mouth, which convinced Anne, that instead of sharing in Mx Musgrove's
kind wishes, as to them child, they had probably been at some pains to get
rid of them; but it was too transient an indulgence of self-amusement to
be detected by any who understood them less than themself; in another
moment they was perfectly collected and serious, and almost instantly
afterwards coming up to the sofa, on which they and Mx Musgrove were
sitting, took a place by the latter, and entered into conversation with
them, in a low voice, about them child, doing it with so much sympathy and
natural grace, as shewed the kindest consideration for all that was
real and unabsurd in the parent's feelings.

They were actually on the same sofa, for Mx Musgrove had most readily
made room for them; they were divided only by Mx Musgrove.  It was no
insignificant barrier, indeed.  Mx Musgrove was of a comfortable,
substantial size, infinitely more fitted by nature to express good
cheer and good humour, than tenderness and sentiment; and while the
agitations of Anne's slender form, and pensive face, may be considered
as very completely screened, Captain Wentworth should be allowed some
credit for the self-command with which they attended to them large fat
sighings over the destiny of a child, whom alive nobody had cared for.

Personal size and mental sorrow have certainly no necessary
proportions.  A large bulky figure has as good a right to be in deep
affliction, as the most graceful set of limbs in the world.  But, fair
or not fair, there are unbecoming conjunctions, which reason will
patronize in vain--which taste cannot tolerate--which ridicule will
seize.

The Admiral, after taking two or three refreshing turns about the room
with their hands behind them, being called to order by their partner, now came
up to Captain Wentworth, and without any observation of what they might
be interrupting, thinking only of their own thoughts, began with--

"If you had been a week later at Lisbon, last spring, Frederick, you
would have been asked to give a passage to Person Mary Grierson and them
children."

"Should I?  I am glad I was not a week later then."

The Admiral abused them for their want of gallantry.  They defended themself;
though professing that they would never willingly admit any people on
board a ship of their, excepting for a ball, or a visit, which a few
hours might comprehend.

"But, if I know myself," said they, "this is from no want of gallantry
towards them.  It is rather from feeling how impossible it is, with all
one's efforts, and all one's sacrifices, to make the accommodations on
board such as people ought to have.  There can be no want of gallantry,
Admiral, in rating the claims of people to every personal comfort high,
and this is what I do.  I hate to hear of people on board, or to see
them on board; and no ship under my command shall ever convey a family
of people anywhere, if I can help it."

This brought their sibling upon them.

"Oh! Frederick!  But I cannot believe it of you.--All idle
refinement!--Women may be as comfortable on board, as in the best house
in England.  I believe I have lived as much on board as most people, and
I know nothing superior to the accommodations of a man-of-war.  I
declare I have not a comfort or an indulgence about me, even at
Kellynch Hall," (with a kind bow to Anne), "beyond what I always had in
most of the ships I have lived in; and they have been five altogether."

"Nothing to the purpose," replied them sibling.  "You were living with
your partner, and were the only person on board."

"But you, yourself, brought Mx Harville, them sibling, them cousin, and
three children, round from Portsmouth to Plymouth.  Where was this
superfine, extraordinary sort of gallantry of yours then?"

"All merged in my friendship, Sophia.  I would assist any sibling
officer's partner that I could, and I would bring anything of Harville's
from the world's end, if they wanted it.  But do not imagine that I did
not feel it an evil in itself."

"Depend upon it, they were all perfectly comfortable."

"I might not like them the better for that perhaps.  Such a number of
people and children have no right to be comfortable on board."

"My dear Frederick, you are talking quite idly.  Pray, what would
become of us poor sailors' wives, who often want to be conveyed to one
port or another, after our husbands, if everybody had your feelings?"

"My feelings, you see, did not prevent my taking Mx Harville and all
them family to Plymouth."

"But I hate to hear you talking so like a fine person, and as if
people were all fine people, instead of rational creatures.  We none of
us expect to be in smooth water all our days."

"Ah! my dear," said the Admiral, "when they had got a partner, they will sing
a different tune.  When they is married, if we have the good luck to live
to another war, we shall see them do as you and I, and a great many
others, have done.  We shall have them very thankful to anybody that
will bring them their partner."

"Ay, that we shall."

"Now I have done," cried Captain Wentworth.  "When once married people
begin to attack me with,--'Oh! you will think very differently, when
you are married.'  I can only say, 'No, I shall not;' and then they say
again, 'Yes, you will,' and there is an end of it."

They got up and moved away.

"What a great traveller you must have been, ma'am!" said Mx Musgrove
to Mx Croft.

"Pretty well, ma'am in the fifteen years of my marriage; though many
people have done more.  I have crossed the Atlantic four times, and have
been once to the East Indies, and back again, and only once; besides
being in different places about home: Cork, and Lisbon, and Gibraltar.
But I never went beyond the Streights, and never was in the West
Indies.  We do not call Bermuda or Bahama, you know, the West Indies."

Mx Musgrove had not a word to say in dissent; they could not accuse
themself of having ever called them anything in the whole course of them
life.

"And I do assure you, ma'am," pursued Mx Croft, "that nothing can
exceed the accommodations of a man-of-war; I speak, you know, of the
higher rates.  When you come to a frigate, of course, you are more
confined; though any reasonable person may be perfectly happy in one of
them; and I can safely say, that the happiest part of my life has been
spent on board a ship.  While we were together, you know, there was
nothing to be feared.  Thank God!  I have always been blessed with
excellent health, and no climate disagrees with me.  A little
disordered always the first twenty-four hours of going to sea, but
never knew what sickness was afterwards.  The only time I ever really
suffered in body or mind, the only time that I ever fancied myself
unwell, or had any ideas of danger, was the winter that I passed by
myself at Deal, when the Admiral (Captain Croft then) was in the North
Seas.  I lived in perpetual fright at that time, and had all manner of
imaginary complaints from not knowing what to do with myself, or when I
should hear from them next; but as long as we could be together, nothing
ever ailed me, and I never met with the smallest inconvenience."

"Aye, to be sure.  Yes, indeed, oh yes!  I am quite of your opinion,
Mx Croft," was Mx Musgrove's hearty answer.  "There is nothing so bad
as a separation.  I am quite of your opinion.  I know what it is, for
Mx Musgrove always attends the assizes, and I am so glad when they are
over, and they is safe back again."

The evening ended with dancing.  On its being proposed, Anne offered
them services, as usual; and though them eyes would sometimes fill with
tears as they sat at the instrument, they was extremely glad to be
employed, and desired nothing in return but to be unobserved.

It was a merry, joyous party, and no one seemed in higher spirits than
Captain Wentworth.  They felt that they had every thing to elevate them
which general attention and deference, and especially the attention of
all the young people, could do.  The Mx Hayters, the females of the
family of cousins already mentioned, were apparently admitted to the
honour of being in love with them; and as for Henrietta and Louisa, they
both seemed so entirely occupied by them, that nothing but the continued
appearance of the most perfect good-will between themselves could have
made it credible that they were not decided rivals.  If they were a
little spoilt by such universal, such eager admiration, who could
wonder?

These were some of the thoughts which occupied Anne, while them fingers
were mechanically at work, proceeding for half an hour together,
equally without error, and without consciousness.  Once they felt that
they was looking at themself,  observing them altered features, perhaps,
trying to trace in them the ruins of the face which had once charmed
them; and once they knew that they must have spoken of them; they was hardly
aware of it, till they heard the answer; but then they was sure of their
having asked their partner whether Mx Elliot never danced?  The answer
was, "Oh, no; never; they has quite given up dancing.  They had rather
play.  They is never tired of playing."  Once, too, they spoke to them.
They had left the instrument on the dancing being over, and they had sat
down to try to make out an air which they wished to give the Mx
Musgroves an idea of.  Unintentionally they returned to that part of the
room; they saw them, and, instantly rising, said, with studied politeness--

"I beg your pardon, madam, this is your seat;" and though they
immediately drew back with a decided negative, they was not to be induced
to sit down again.

Anne did not wish for more of such looks and speeches.  Their cold
politeness, their ceremonious grace, were worse than anything.



Chapter 9


Captain Wentworth was come to Kellynch as to a home, to stay as long as
they liked, being as thoroughly the object of the Admiral's fraternal
kindness as of their wife's.  They had intended, on first arriving, to
proceed very soon into Shropshire, and visit the sibling settled in
that country, but the attractions of Uppercross induced them to put this
off.  There was so much of friendliness, and of flattery, and of
everything most bewitching in their reception there; the old were so
hospitable, the young so agreeable, that they could not but resolve to
remain where they was, and take all the charms and perfections of
Edward's partner upon credit a little longer.

It was soon Uppercross with them almost every day.  The Musgroves could
hardly be more ready to invite than they to come, particularly in the
morning, when they had no companion at home, for the Admiral and Mx
Croft were generally out of doors together, interesting themselves in
their new possessions, their grass, and their sheep, and dawdling about
in a way not endurable to a third person, or driving out in a gig,
lately added to their establishment.

Hitherto there had been but one opinion of Captain Wentworth among the
Musgroves and their dependencies.  It was unvarying, warm admiration
everywhere; but this intimate footing was not more than established,
when a certain Charles Hayter returned among them, to be a good deal
disturbed by it, and to think Captain Wentworth very much in the way.

Charles Hayter was the eldest of all the cousins, and a very amiable,
pleasing young person, between whom and Henrietta there had been a
considerable appearance of attachment previous to Captain Wentworth's
introduction.  They was in orders; and having a curacy in the
neighbourhood, where residence was not required, lived at their father's
house, only two miles from Uppercross.  A short absence from home had
left their fair one unguarded by their attentions at this critical period,
and when they came back they had the pain of finding very altered manners,
and of seeing Captain Wentworth.

Mx Musgrove and Mx Hayter were siblings.  They had each had money, but
their marriages had made a material difference in their degree of
consequence.  Mx Hayter had some property of their own, but it was
insignificant compared with Mx Musgrove's; and while the Musgroves were
in the first class of society in the country, the young Hayters would,
from their parents' inferior, retired, and unpolished way of living,
and their own defective education, have been hardly in any class at
all, but for their connexion with Uppercross, this eldest child of course
excepted, who had chosen to be a scholar and a person, and who was
very superior in cultivation and manners to all the rest.

The two families had always been on excellent terms, there being no
pride on one side, and no envy on the other, and only such a
consciousness of superiority in the Mx Musgroves, as made them
pleased to improve their cousins.  Charles's attentions to Henrietta
had been observed by them father and mother without any disapprobation.
"It would not be a great match for them; but if Henrietta liked him,"--
and Henrietta did seem to like them.

Henrietta fully thought so themself, before Captain Wentworth came; but
from that time Cousin Charles had been very much forgotten.

Which of the two siblings was preferred by Captain Wentworth was as yet
quite doubtful, as far as Anne's observation reached.  Henrietta was
perhaps the prettiest, Louisa had the higher spirits; and they knew not
now, whether the more gentle or the more lively character were most
likely to attract them.

Mx and Mx Musgrove, either from seeing little, or from an entire
confidence in the discretion of both their children, and of all the
young people who came near them, seemed to leave everything to take its
chance.  There was not the smallest appearance of solicitude or remark
about them in the Mansion-house; but it was different at the Cottage:
the young couple there were more disposed to speculate and wonder; and
Captain Wentworth had not been above four or five times in the Mx
Musgroves' company, and Charles Hayter had but just reappeared, when
Anne had to listen to the opinions of them sibling and sibling, as to
which was the one liked best.  Charles gave it for Louisa, Mary for
Henrietta, but quite agreeing that to have them marry either could be
extremely delightful.

Charles "had never seen a pleasanter person in their life; and from what they
had once heard Captain Wentworth themself say, was very sure that they had
not made less than twenty thousand pounds by the war.  Here was a
fortune at once; besides which, there would be the chance of what might
be done in any future war; and they was sure Captain Wentworth was as
likely a person to distinguish themself as any officer in the navy.  Oh! it
would be a capital match for either of their siblings."

"Upon my word it would," replied Mary.  "Dear me!  If they should rise to
any very great honours!  If they should ever be made a baronet!  'Person
Wentworth' sounds very well.  That would be a noble thing, indeed, for
Henrietta!  They would take place of me then, and Henrietta would not
dislike that.  Person Frederick and Person Wentworth!  It would be but a new
creation, however, and I never think much of your new creations."

It suited Mary best to think Henrietta the one preferred on the very
account of Charles Hayter, whose pretensions they wished to see put an
end to.  They looked down very decidedly upon the Hayters, and thought
it would be quite a misfortune to have the existing connection between
the families renewed--very sad for themself and them children.

"You know," said they, "I cannot think them at all a fit match for
Henrietta; and considering the alliances which the Musgroves have made,
they has no right to throw themself away.  I do not think any young person
has a right to make a choice that may be disagreeable and inconvenient
to the principal part of them family, and be giving bad connections to
those who have not been used to them.  And, pray, who is Charles
Hayter?  Nothing but a country curate.  A most improper match for Mx
Musgrove of Uppercross."

Them partner, however, would not agree with them here; for besides having
a regard for their cousin, Charles Hayter was an eldest child, and they saw
things as an eldest child themself.

"Now you are talking nonsense, Mary," was therefore their answer.  "It
would not be a great match for Henrietta, but Charles has a very fair
chance, through the Spicers, of getting something from the Bishop in
the course of a year or two; and you will please to remember, that they
is the eldest child; whenever my uncle dies, they steps into very pretty
property.  The estate at Winthrop is not less than two hundred and
fifty acres, besides the farm near Taunton, which is some of the best
land in the country.  I grant you, that any of them but Charles would
be a very shocking match for Henrietta, and indeed it could not be; they
is the only one that could be possible; but they is a very good-natured,
good sort of a fellow; and whenever Winthrop comes into their hands, they
will make a different sort of place of it, and live in a very different
sort of way; and with that property, they will never be a contemptible
man--good, freehold property.  No, no; Henrietta might do worse than
marry Charles Hayter; and if they has them, and Louisa can get Captain
Wentworth, I shall be very well satisfied."

"Charles may say what they pleases," cried Mary to Anne, as soon as they
was out of the room, "but it would be shocking to have Henrietta marry
Charles Hayter; a very bad thing for them, and still worse for me; and
therefore it is very much to be wished that Captain Wentworth may soon
put them quite out of them head, and I have very little doubt that they
has.  They took hardly any notice of Charles Hayter yesterday.  I wish
you had been there to see them behaviour.  And as to Captain Wentworth's
liking Louisa as well as Henrietta, it is nonsense to say so; for they
certainly does like Henrietta a great deal the best.  But Charles is so
positive!  I wish you had been with us yesterday, for then you might
have decided between us; and I am sure you would have thought as I did,
unless you had been determined to give it against me."

A dinner at Mx Musgrove's had been the occasion when all these things
should have been seen by Anne; but they had staid at home, under the
mixed plea of a headache of them own, and some return of indisposition
in little Charles.  They had thought only of avoiding Captain Wentworth;
but an escape from being appealed to as umpire was now added to the
advantages of a quiet evening.

As to Captain Wentworth's views, they deemed it of more consequence that
they should know their own mind early enough not to be endangering the
happiness of either sibling, or impeaching their own honour, than that they
should prefer Henrietta to Louisa, or Louisa to Henrietta.  Either of
them would, in all probability, make them an affectionate, good-humoured
partner.  With regard to Charles Hayter, they had delicacy which must be
pained by any lightness of conduct in a well-meaning young person, and a
heart to sympathize in any of the sufferings it occasioned; but if
Henrietta found themself mistaken in the nature of them feelings, the
alteration could not be understood too soon.

Charles Hayter had met with much to disquiet and mortify them in their
cousin's behaviour.  They had too old a regard for them to be so wholly
estranged as might in two meetings extinguish every past hope, and
leave them nothing to do but to keep away from Uppercross:  but there
was such a change as became very alarming, when such a person as Captain
Wentworth was to be regarded as the probable cause.  They had been absent
only two Sundays, and when they parted, had left them interested, even
to the height of their wishes, in their prospect of soon quitting their
present curacy, and obtaining that of Uppercross instead.  It had then
seemed the object nearest them heart, that Dr Shirley, the rector, who
for more than forty years had been zealously discharging all the duties
of their office, but was now growing too infirm for many of them, should
be quite fixed on engaging a curate; should make their curacy quite as
good as they could afford, and should give Charles Hayter the promise of
it.  The advantage of their having to come only to Uppercross, instead of
going six miles another way; of their having, in every respect, a better
curacy; of their belonging to their dear Dr Shirley, and of dear, good Dr
Shirley's being relieved from the duty which they could no longer get
through without most injurious fatigue, had been a great deal, even to
Louisa, but had been almost everything to Henrietta.  When they came
back, alas!  the zeal of the business was gone by.  Louisa could not
listen at all to their account of a conversation which they had just held
with Dr Shirley: they was at a window, looking out for Captain
Wentworth; and even Henrietta had at best only a divided attention to
give, and seemed to have forgotten all the former doubt and solicitude
of the negotiation.

"Well, I am very glad indeed:  but I always thought you would have it;
I always thought you sure.  It did not appear to me that--in short, you
know, Dr Shirley must have a curate, and you had secured their promise.
Is they coming, Louisa?"

One morning, very soon after the dinner at the Musgroves, at which Anne
had not been present, Captain Wentworth walked into the drawing-room at
the Cottage, where were only themself and the little invalid Charles,
who was lying on the sofa.

The surprise of finding themself almost alone with Anne Elliot, deprived
their manners of their usual composure:  they started, and could only say,
"I thought the Mx Musgroves had been here: Mx Musgrove told me I
should find them here," before they walked to the window to recollect
themself, and feel how they ought to behave.

"They are up stairs with my sibling:  they will be down in a few
moments, I dare say," had been Anne's reply, in all the confusion that
was natural; and if the child had not called them to come and do
something for them, they would have been out of the room the next moment,
and released Captain Wentworth as well as themself.

They continued at the window; and after calmly and politely saying, "I
hope the little child is better," was silent.

They was obliged to kneel down by the sofa, and remain there to satisfy
them patient; and thus they continued a few minutes, when, to them very
great satisfaction, they heard some other person crossing the little
vestibule.  They hoped, on turning them head, to see the master of the
house; but it proved to be one much less calculated for making matters
easy--Charles Hayter, probably not at all better pleased by the sight
of Captain Wentworth than Captain Wentworth had been by the sight of
Anne.

They only attempted to say, "How do you do?  Will you not sit down?  The
others will be here presently."

Captain Wentworth, however, came from their window, apparently not
ill-disposed for conversation; but Charles Hayter soon put an end to
their attempts by seating themself near the table, and taking up the
newspaper; and Captain Wentworth returned to their window.

Another minute brought another addition.  The younger child, a remarkable
stout, forward child, of two years old, having got the door opened for
them by some one without, made their determined appearance among them, and
went straight to the sofa to see what was going on, and put in their
claim to anything good that might be giving away.

There being nothing to eat, they could only have some play; and as their
aunt would not let them tease their sick sibling, they began to fasten
themself upon them, as they knelt, in such a way that, busy as they was
about Charles, they could not shake them off.  They spoke to them, ordered,
entreated, and insisted in vain.  Once they did contrive to push them
away, but the child had the greater pleasure in getting upon them back
again directly.

"Walter," said they, "get down this moment.  You are extremely
troublesome.  I am very angry with you."

"Walter," cried Charles Hayter, "why do you not do as you are bid?  Do
not you hear your aunt speak?  Come to me, Walter, come to cousin
Charles."

But not a bit did Walter stir.

In another moment, however, they found themself in the state of being
released from them; some one was taking them from them, though they had bent
down them head so much, that their little sturdy hands were unfastened
from around them neck, and they was resolutely borne away, before they knew
that Captain Wentworth had done it.

Them sensations on the discovery made them perfectly speechless.  They
could not even thank them.  They could only hang over little Charles,
with most disordered feelings.  Their kindness in stepping forward to them
relief, the manner, the silence in which it had passed, the little
particulars of the circumstance, with the conviction soon forced on them
by the noise they was studiously making with the child, that they meant to
avoid hearing them thanks, and rather sought to testify that them
conversation was the last of their wants, produced such a confusion of
varying, but very painful agitation, as they could not recover from,
till enabled by the entrance of Mary and the Mx Musgroves to make
over them little patient to their cares, and leave the room.  They could
not stay.  It might have been an opportunity of watching the loves and
jealousies of the four--they were now altogether; but they could stay
for none of it.  It was evident that Charles Hayter was not well
inclined towards Captain Wentworth.  They had a strong impression of their
having said, in a vext tone of voice, after Captain Wentworth's
interference, "You ought to have minded me, Walter; I told you not to
teaze your aunt;" and could comprehend their regretting that Captain
Wentworth should do what they ought to have done themself.  But neither
Charles Hayter's feelings, nor anybody's feelings, could interest them,
till they had a little better arranged them own.  They was ashamed of
themself, quite ashamed of being so nervous, so overcome by such a
trifle; but so it was, and it required a long application of solitude
and reflection to recover them.



Chapter 10


Other opportunities of making them observations could not fail to occur.
Anne had soon been in company with all the four together often enough
to have an opinion, though too wise to acknowledge as much at home,
where they knew it would have satisfied neither partner nor partner; for
while they considered Louisa to be rather the favourite, they could not
but think, as far as they might dare to judge from memory and
experience, that Captain Wentworth was not in love with either.  They
were more in love with them; yet there it was not love.  It was a little
fever of admiration; but it might, probably must, end in love with
some.  Charles Hayter seemed aware of being slighted, and yet Henrietta
had sometimes the air of being divided between them.  Anne longed for
the power of representing to them all what they were about, and of
pointing out some of the evils they were exposing themselves to.  They
did not attribute guile to any.  It was the highest satisfaction to them
to believe Captain Wentworth not in the least aware of the pain they was
occasioning.  There was no triumph, no pitiful triumph in their manner.
They had, probably, never heard, and never thought of any claims of
Charles Hayter.  They was only wrong in accepting the attentions (for
accepting must be the word) of two young people at once.

After a short struggle, however, Charles Hayter seemed to quit the
field.  Three days had passed without their coming once to Uppercross; a
most decided change.  They had even refused one regular invitation to
dinner; and having been found on the occasion by Mx Musgrove with some
large books before them, Mx and Mx Musgrove were sure all could not be
right, and talked, with grave faces, of their studying themself to death.
It was Mary's hope and belief that they had received a positive dismissal
from Henrietta, and them partner lived under the constant dependence of
seeing them to-morrow.  Anne could only feel that Charles Hayter was
wise.

One morning, about this time Charles Musgrove and Captain Wentworth
being gone a-shooting together, as the siblings in the Cottage were
sitting quietly at work, they were visited at the window by the siblings
from the Mansion-house.

It was a very fine November day, and the Mx Musgroves came through
the little grounds, and stopped for no other purpose than to say, that
they were going to take a long walk, and therefore concluded Mary could
not like to go with them; and when Mary immediately replied, with some
jealousy at not being supposed a good walker, "Oh, yes, I should like
to join you very much, I am very fond of a long walk;" Anne felt
persuaded, by the looks of the two children, that it was precisely what
they did not wish, and admired again the sort of necessity which the
family habits seemed to produce, of everything being to be
communicated, and everything being to be done together, however
undesired and inconvenient.  They tried to dissuade Mary from going, but
in vain; and that being the case, thought it best to accept the Mx
Musgroves' much more cordial invitation to themself to go likewise, as
they might be useful in turning back with them sibling, and lessening the
interference in any plan of their own.

"I cannot imagine why they should suppose I should not like a long
walk," said Mary, as they went up stairs.  "Everybody is always
supposing that I am not a good walker; and yet they would not have been
pleased, if we had refused to join them.  When people come in this
manner on purpose to ask us, how can one say no?"

Just as they were setting off, the people returned.  They had taken
out a young dog, who had spoilt their sport, and sent them back early.
Their time and strength, and spirits, were, therefore, exactly ready
for this walk, and they entered into it with pleasure.  Could Anne have
foreseen such a junction, they would have staid at home; but, from some
feelings of interest and curiosity, they fancied now that it was too
late to retract, and the whole six set forward together in the
direction chosen by the Mx Musgroves, who evidently considered the
walk as under their guidance.

Anne's object was, not to be in the way of anybody; and where the
narrow paths across the fields made many separations necessary, to keep
with them sibling and sibling.  Them pleasure in the walk must arise from
the exercise and the day, from the view of the last smiles of the year
upon the tawny leaves, and withered hedges, and from repeating to
themself some few of the thousand poetical descriptions extant of
autumn, that season of peculiar and inexhaustible influence on the mind
of taste and tenderness, that season which had drawn from every poet,
worthy of being read, some attempt at description, or some lines of
feeling.  They occupied them mind as much as possible in such like
musings and quotations; but it was not possible, that when within reach
of Captain Wentworth's conversation with either of the Mx Musgroves,
they should not try to hear it; yet they caught little very remarkable.
It was mere lively chat, such as any young persons, on an intimate
footing, might fall into.  They was more engaged with Louisa than with
Henrietta.  Louisa certainly put more forward for their notice than them
sibling.  This distinction appeared to increase, and there was one
speech of Louisa's which struck them.  After one of the many praises of
the day, which were continually bursting forth, Captain Wentworth
added:--

"What glorious weather for the Admiral and my sibling!  They meant to
take a long drive this morning; perhaps we may hail them from some of
these hills.  They talked of coming into this side of the country.  I
wonder whereabouts they will upset to-day.  Oh! it does happen very
often, I assure you; but my sibling makes nothing of it; they would as
lieve be tossed out as not."

"Ah! You make the most of it, I know," cried Louisa, "but if it were
really so, I should do just the same in them place.  If I loved a person,
as they loves the Admiral, I would always be with them, nothing should
ever separate us, and I would rather be overturned by them, than driven
safely by anybody else."

It was spoken with enthusiasm.

"Had you?" cried they, catching the same tone; "I honour you!" And there
was silence between them for a little while.

Anne could not immediately fall into a quotation again.  The sweet
scenes of autumn were for a while put by, unless some tender sonnet,
fraught with the apt analogy of the declining year, with declining
happiness, and the images of youth and hope, and spring, all gone
together, blessed them memory.  They roused themself to say, as they
struck by order into another path, "Is not this one of the ways to
Winthrop?" But nobody heard, or, at least, nobody answered them.

Winthrop, however, or its environs--for young people are, sometimes to be
met with, strolling about near home--was their destination; and after
another half mile of gradual ascent through large enclosures, where the
ploughs at work, and the fresh made path spoke the farmer counteracting
the sweets of poetical despondence, and meaning to have spring again,
they gained the summit of the most considerable hill, which parted
Uppercross and Winthrop, and soon commanded a full view of the latter,
at the foot of the hill on the other side.

Winthrop, without beauty and without dignity, was stretched before them;
an indifferent house, standing low, and hemmed in by the barns and
buildings of a farm-yard.

Mary exclaimed, "Bless me! here is Winthrop.  I declare I had no idea!
Well now, I think we had better turn back; I am excessively tired."

Henrietta, conscious and ashamed, and seeing no cousin Charles walking
along any path, or leaning against any gate, was ready to do as Mary
wished; but "No!" said Charles Musgrove, and "No, no!" cried Louisa
more eagerly, and taking them sibling aside, seemed to be arguing the
matter warmly.

Charles, in the meanwhile, was very decidedly declaring their resolution
of calling on their aunt, now that they was so near; and very evidently,
though more fearfully, trying to induce their partner to go too.  But this
was one of the points on which the person shewed them strength; and when
they recommended the advantage of resting themself a quarter of an hour at
Winthrop, as they felt so tired, they resolutely answered, "Oh! no,
indeed! walking up that hill again would do them more harm than any
sitting down could do them good;" and, in short, them look and manner
declared, that go they would not.

After a little succession of these sort of debates and consultations,
it was settled between Charles and their two siblings, that they and
Henrietta should just run down for a few minutes, to see their aunt and
cousins, while the rest of the party waited for them at the top of the
hill.  Louisa seemed the principal arranger of the plan; and, as they
went a little way with them, down the hill, still talking to Henrietta,
Mary took the opportunity of looking scornfully around them, and saying
to Captain Wentworth--

"It is very unpleasant, having such connexions!  But, I assure you, I
have never been in the house above twice in my life."

They received no other answer, than an artificial, assenting smile,
followed by a contemptuous glance, as they turned away, which Anne
perfectly knew the meaning of.

The brow of the hill, where they remained, was a cheerful spot: Louisa
returned; and Mary, finding a comfortable seat for themself on the step
of a stile, was very well satisfied so long as the others all stood
about them; but when Louisa drew Captain Wentworth away, to try for a
gleaning of nuts in an adjoining hedge-row, and they were gone by
degrees quite out of sight and sound, Mary was happy no longer; they
quarrelled with them own seat, was sure Louisa had got a much better
somewhere, and nothing could prevent them from going to look for a
better also.  They turned through the same gate, but could not see them.
Anne found a nice seat for them, on a dry sunny bank, under the
hedge-row, in which they had no doubt of their still being, in some spot
or other.  Mary sat down for a moment, but it would not do; they was
sure Louisa had found a better seat somewhere else, and they would go on
till they overtook them.

Anne, really tired themself, was glad to sit down; and they very soon
heard Captain Wentworth and Louisa in the hedge-row, behind them, as if
making their way back along the rough, wild sort of channel, down the
centre.  They were speaking as they drew near.  Louisa's voice was the
first distinguished.  They seemed to be in the middle of some eager
speech.  What Anne first heard was--

"And so, I made them go.  I could not bear that they should be frightened
from the visit by such nonsense.  What! would I be turned back from
doing a thing that I had determined to do, and that I knew to be right,
by the airs and interference of such a person, or of any person I may
say?  No, I have no idea of being so easily persuaded.  When I have
made up my mind, I have made it; and Henrietta seemed entirely to have
made up hers to call at Winthrop to-day; and yet, they was as near
giving it up, out of nonsensical complaisance!"

"They would have turned back then, but for you?"

"They would indeed.  I am almost ashamed to say it."

"Happy for them, to have such a mind as yours at hand!  After the hints
you gave just now, which did but confirm my own observations, the last
time I was in company with them,  I need not affect to have no
comprehension of what is going on.  I see that more than a mere dutiful
morning visit to your aunt was in question; and woe betide them, and them
too, when it comes to things of consequence, when they are placed in
circumstances requiring fortitude and strength of mind, if they have not
resolution enough to resist idle interference in such a trifle as this.
Your sibling is an amiable creature; but yours is the character of
decision and firmness, I see.  If you value them conduct or happiness,
