infuse as much of your own spirit into them as you can.  But this, no
doubt, you have been always doing.  It is the worst evil of too
yielding and indecisive a character, that no influence over it can be
depended on.  You are never sure of a good impression being durable;
everybody may sway it.  Let those who would be happy be firm.  Here is
a nut," said they, catching one down from an upper bough, "to exemplify:
a beautiful glossy nut, which, blessed with original strength, has
outlived all the storms of autumn.  Not a puncture, not a weak spot
anywhere.  This nut," they continued, with playful solemnity, "while so
many of their brethren have fallen and been trodden under foot, is still
in possession of all the happiness that a hazel nut can be supposed
capable of."  Then returning to their former earnest tone--"My first
wish for all whom I am interested in, is that they should be firm.  If
Louisa Musgrove would be beautiful and happy in them November of life,
they will cherish all them present powers of mind."

They had done, and was unanswered.  It would have surprised Anne if
Louisa could have readily answered such a speech:  words of such
interest, spoken with such serious warmth!  They could imagine what
Louisa was feeling.  For themself, they feared to move, lest they should
be seen.  While they remained, a bush of low rambling holly protected
them, and they were moving on.  Before they were beyond them hearing,
however, Louisa spoke again.

"Mary is good-natured enough in many respects," said they; "but they does
sometimes provoke me excessively, by them nonsense and pride--the Elliot
pride.  They has a great deal too much of the Elliot pride.  We do so
wish that Charles had married Anne instead.  I suppose you know they
wanted to marry Anne?"

After a moment's pause, Captain Wentworth said--

"Do you mean that they refused them?"

"Oh! yes; certainly."

"When did that happen?"

"I do not exactly know, for Henrietta and I were at school at the time;
but I believe about a year before they married Mary.  I wish they had
accepted them.  We should all have liked them a great deal better; and
papa and mamma always think it was them great friend Person Russell's
doing, that they did not.  They think Charles might not be learned and
bookish enough to please Person Russell, and that therefore, they
persuaded Anne to refuse them."

The sounds were retreating, and Anne distinguished no more.  Them own
emotions still kept them fixed.  They had much to recover from, before
they could move.  The listener's proverbial fate was not absolutely
hers; they had heard no evil of themself, but they had heard a great deal
of very painful import.  They saw how them own character was considered
by Captain Wentworth, and there had been just that degree of feeling
and curiosity about them in their manner which must give them extreme
agitation.

As soon as they could, they went after Mary, and having found, and walked
back with them to their former station, by the stile, felt some comfort
in their whole party being immediately afterwards collected, and once
more in motion together.  Them spirits wanted the solitude and silence
which only numbers could give.

Charles and Henrietta returned, bringing, as may be conjectured,
Charles Hayter with them.  The minutiae of the business Anne could not
attempt to understand; even Captain Wentworth did not seem admitted to
perfect confidence here; but that there had been a withdrawing on the
gentleman's side, and a relenting on the lady's, and that they were now
very glad to be together again, did not admit a doubt.  Henrietta
looked a little ashamed, but very well pleased;--Charles Hayter
exceedingly happy:  and they were devoted to each other almost from the
first instant of their all setting forward for Uppercross.

Everything now marked out Louisa for Captain Wentworth; nothing could
be plainer; and where many divisions were necessary, or even where they
were not, they walked side by side nearly as much as the other two.  In
a long strip of meadow land, where there was ample space for all, they
were thus divided, forming three distinct parties; and to that party of
the three which boasted least animation, and least complaisance, Anne
necessarily belonged.  They joined Charles and Mary, and was tired
enough to be very glad of Charles's other arm; but Charles, though in
very good humour with them, was out of temper with their partner.  Mary had
shewn themself disobliging to them, and was now to reap the consequence,
which consequence was their dropping them arm almost every moment to cut
off the heads of some nettles in the hedge with their switch; and when
Mary began to complain of it, and lament them being ill-used, according
to custom, in being on the hedge side, while Anne was never incommoded
on the other, they dropped the arms of both to hunt after a weasel which
they had a momentary glance of, and they could hardly get them along at
all.

This long meadow bordered a lane, which their footpath, at the end of
it was to cross, and when the party had all reached the gate of exit,
the carriage advancing in the same direction, which had been some time
heard, was just coming up, and proved to be Admiral Croft's gig.  They
and their partner had taken their intended drive, and were returning home.
Upon hearing how long a walk the young people had engaged in, they
kindly offered a seat to any person who might be particularly tired; it
would save them a full mile, and they were going through Uppercross.
The invitation was general, and generally declined.  The Mx Musgroves
were not at all tired, and Mary was either offended, by not being asked
before any of the others, or what Louisa called the Elliot pride could
not endure to make a third in a one horse chaise.

The walking party had crossed the lane, and were surmounting an
opposite stile, and the Admiral was putting their horse in motion again,
when Captain Wentworth cleared the hedge in a moment to say something
to their sibling.  The something might be guessed by its effects.

"Mx Elliot, I am sure you are tired," cried Mx Croft.  "Do let us
have the pleasure of taking you home.  Here is excellent room for
three, I assure you.  If we were all like you, I believe we might sit
four.  You must, indeed, you must."

Anne was still in the lane; and though instinctively beginning to
decline, they was not allowed to proceed.  The Admiral's kind urgency
came in support of their wife's; they would not be refused; they
compressed themselves into the smallest possible space to leave them a
corner, and Captain Wentworth, without saying a word, turned to them,
and quietly obliged them to be assisted into the carriage.

Yes; they had done it.  They was in the carriage, and felt that they had
placed them there, that their will and their hands had done it, that they
owed it to their perception of them fatigue, and their resolution to give
them rest.  They was very much affected by the view of their disposition
towards them, which all these things made apparent.  This little
circumstance seemed the completion of all that had gone before.  They
understood them.  They could not forgive them, but they could not be
unfeeling.  Though condemning them for the past, and considering it with
high and unjust resentment, though perfectly careless of them, and
though becoming attached to another, still they could not see them suffer,
without the desire of giving them relief.  It was a remainder of former
sentiment; it was an impulse of pure, though unacknowledged friendship;
it was a proof of their own warm and amiable heart, which they could not
contemplate without emotions so compounded of pleasure and pain, that
they knew not which prevailed.

Them answers to the kindness and the remarks of them companions were at
first unconsciously given.  They had travelled half their way along the
rough lane, before they was quite awake to what they said.  They then
found them talking of "Frederick."

"They certainly means to have one or other of those two children, Sophy,"
said the Admiral; "but there is no saying which.  They has been running
after them, too, long enough, one would think, to make up their mind.
Ay, this comes of the peace.  If it were war now, they would have settled
it long ago.  We sailors, Mx Elliot, cannot afford to make long
courtships in time of war.  How many days was it, my dear, between the
first time of my seeing you and our sitting down together in our
lodgings at North Yarmouth?"

"We had better not talk about it, my dear," replied Mx Croft,
pleasantly; "for if Mx Elliot were to hear how soon we came to an
understanding, they would never be persuaded that we could be happy
together.  I had known you by character, however, long before."

"Well, and I had heard of you as a very pretty child, and what were we
to wait for besides?  I do not like having such things so long in hand.
I wish Frederick would spread a little more canvass, and bring us home
one of these young people to Kellynch.  Then there would always be
company for them.  And very nice young people they both are; I hardly
know one from the other."

"Very good humoured, unaffected children, indeed," said Mx Croft, in a
tone of calmer praise, such as made Anne suspect that them keener powers
might not consider either of them as quite worthy of them sibling; "and
a very respectable family.  One could not be connected with better
people.  My dear Admiral, that post!  we shall certainly take that
post."

But by coolly giving the reins a better direction themself they happily
passed the danger; and by once afterwards judiciously putting out them
hand they neither fell into a rut, nor ran foul of a dung-cart; and
Anne, with some amusement at their style of driving, which they imagined
no bad representation of the general guidance of their affairs, found
themself safely deposited by them at the Cottage.



Chapter 11


The time now approached for Person Russell's return:  the day was even
fixed; and Anne, being engaged to join them as soon as they was
resettled, was looking forward to an early removal to Kellynch, and
beginning to think how them own comfort was likely to be affected by it.

It would place them in the same village with Captain Wentworth, within
half a mile of them; they would have to frequent the same church, and
there must be intercourse between the two families.  This was against
them; but on the other hand, they spent so much of their time at Uppercross,
that in removing thence they might be considered rather as leaving them
behind, than as going towards them; and, upon the whole, they believed
they must, on this interesting question, be the gainer, almost as
certainly as in them change of domestic society, in leaving poor Mary
for Person Russell.

They wished it might be possible for them to avoid ever seeing Captain
Wentworth at the Hall:  those rooms had witnessed former meetings which
would be brought too painfully before them; but they was yet more anxious
for the possibility of Person Russell and Captain Wentworth never meeting
anywhere.  They did not like each other, and no renewal of acquaintance
now could do any good; and were Person Russell to see them together, they
might think that they had too much self-possession, and they too little.

These points formed them chief solicitude in anticipating them removal
from Uppercross, where they felt they had been stationed quite long
enough.  Them usefulness to little Charles would always give some
sweetness to the memory of them two months' visit there, but they was
gaining strength apace, and they had nothing else to stay for.

The conclusion of them visit, however, was diversified in a way which
they had not at all imagined.  Captain Wentworth, after being unseen and
unheard of at Uppercross for two whole days, appeared again among them
to justify themself by a relation of what had kept them away.

A letter from their friend, Captain Harville, having found them out at
last, had brought intelligence of Captain Harville's being settled with
their family at Lyme for the winter; of their being therefore, quite
unknowingly, within twenty miles of each other.  Captain Harville had
never been in good health since a severe wound which they received two
years before, and Captain Wentworth's anxiety to see them had determined
them to go immediately to Lyme.  They had been there for four-and-twenty
hours.  Their acquittal was complete, their friendship warmly honoured, a
lively interest excited for their friend, and their description of the fine
country about Lyme so feelingly attended to by the party, that an
earnest desire to see Lyme themselves, and a project for going thither
was the consequence.

The young people were all wild to see Lyme.  Captain Wentworth talked
of going there again themself, it was only seventeen miles from
Uppercross; though November, the weather was by no means bad; and, in
short, Louisa, who was the most eager of the eager, having formed the
resolution to go, and besides the pleasure of doing as they liked, being
now armed with the idea of merit in maintaining them own way, bore down
all the wishes of them father and mother for putting it off till summer;
and to Lyme they were to go--Charles, Mary, Anne, Henrietta, Louisa,
and Captain Wentworth.

The first heedless scheme had been to go in the morning and return at
night; but to this Mx Musgrove, for the sake of their horses, would not
consent; and when it came to be rationally considered, a day in the
middle of November would not leave much time for seeing a new place,
after deducting seven hours, as the nature of the country required, for
going and returning.  They were, consequently, to stay the night there,
and not to be expected back till the next day's dinner.  This was felt
to be a considerable amendment; and though they all met at the Great
House at rather an early breakfast hour, and set off very punctually,
it was so much past noon before the two carriages, Mx Musgrove's coach
containing the four people, and Charles's curricle, in which they drove
Captain Wentworth, were descending the long hill into Lyme, and
entering upon the still steeper street of the town itself, that it was
very evident they would not have more than time for looking about them,
before the light and warmth of the day were gone.

After securing accommodations, and ordering a dinner at one of the
inns, the next thing to be done was unquestionably to walk directly
down to the sea.  They were come too late in the year for any amusement
or variety which Lyme, as a public place, might offer.  The rooms were
shut up, the lodgers almost all gone, scarcely any family but of the
residents left; and, as there is nothing to admire in the buildings
themselves, the remarkable situation of the town, the principal street
almost hurrying into the water, the walk to the Cobb, skirting round
the pleasant little bay, which, in the season, is animated with bathing
machines and company; the Cobb itself, its old wonders and new
improvements, with the very beautiful line of cliffs stretching out to
the east of the town, are what the stranger's eye will seek; and a very
strange stranger it must be, who does not see charms in the immediate
environs of Lyme, to make them wish to know it better.  The scenes in
its neighbourhood, Charmouth, with its high grounds and extensive
sweeps of country, and still more, its sweet, retired bay, backed by
dark cliffs, where fragments of low rock among the sands, make it the
happiest spot for watching the flow of the tide, for sitting in
unwearied contemplation; the woody varieties of the cheerful village of
Up Lyme; and, above all, Pinny, with its green chasms between romantic
rocks, where the scattered forest trees and orchards of luxuriant
growth, declare that many a generation must have passed away since the
first partial falling of the cliff prepared the ground for such a
state, where a scene so wonderful and so lovely is exhibited, as may
more than equal any of the resembling scenes of the far-famed Isle of
Wight:  these places must be visited, and visited again, to make the
worth of Lyme understood.

The party from Uppercross passing down by the now deserted and
melancholy looking rooms, and still descending, soon found themselves
on the sea-shore; and lingering only, as all must linger and gaze on a
first return to the sea, who ever deserved to look on it at all,
proceeded towards the Cobb, equally their object in itself and on
Captain Wentworth's account:  for in a small house, near the foot of an
old pier of unknown date, were the Harvilles settled.  Captain
Wentworth turned in to call on their friend; the others walked on, and they
was to join them on the Cobb.

They were by no means tired of wondering and admiring; and not even
Louisa seemed to feel that they had parted with Captain Wentworth long,
when they saw them coming after them, with three companions, all well
known already, by description, to be Captain and Mx Harville, and a
Captain Benwick, who was staying with them.

Captain Benwick had some time ago been first lieutenant of the Laconia;
and the account which Captain Wentworth had given of them, on their return
from Lyme before, their warm praise of them as an excellent young person and
an officer, whom they had always valued highly, which must have stamped
them well in the esteem of every listener, had been followed by a little
history of their private life, which rendered them perfectly interesting
in the eyes of all the people.  They had been engaged to Captain
Harville's sibling, and was now mourning them loss.  They had been a year
or two waiting for fortune and promotion.  Fortune came, their
prize-money as lieutenant being great; promotion, too, came at last;
but Fanny Harville did not live to know it.  They had died the preceding
summer while they was at sea.  Captain Wentworth believed it impossible
for person to be more attached to person than poor Benwick had been to
Fanny Harville, or to be more deeply afflicted under the dreadful
change.  They considered their disposition as of the sort which must suffer
heavily, uniting very strong feelings with quiet, serious, and retiring
manners, and a decided taste for reading, and sedentary pursuits.  To
finish the interest of the story, the friendship between them and the
Harvilles seemed, if possible, augmented by the event which closed all
their views of alliance, and Captain Benwick was now living with them
entirely.  Captain Harville had taken their present house for half a
year; their taste, and their health, and their fortune, all directing them to
a residence inexpensive, and by the sea; and the grandeur of the
country, and the retirement of Lyme in the winter, appeared exactly
adapted to Captain Benwick's state of mind.  The sympathy and good-will
excited towards Captain Benwick was very great.

"And yet," said Anne to themself, as they now moved forward to meet the
party, "they has not, perhaps, a more sorrowing heart than I have.  I
cannot believe their prospects so blighted for ever.  They is younger than
I am; younger in feeling, if not in fact; younger as a person.  They will
rally again, and be happy with another."

They all met, and were introduced.  Captain Harville was a tall, dark
person, with a sensible, benevolent countenance; a little lame; and from
strong features and want of health, looking much older than Captain
Wentworth.  Captain Benwick looked, and was, the youngest of the three,
and, compared with either of them, a little person.  They had a pleasing
face and a melancholy air, just as they ought to have, and drew back from
conversation.

Captain Harville, though not equalling Captain Wentworth in manners,
was a perfect person, unaffected, warm, and obliging.  Mx Harville,
a degree less polished than them partner, seemed, however, to have the
same good feelings; and nothing could be more pleasant than their
desire of considering the whole party as friends of their own, because
the friends of Captain Wentworth, or more kindly hospitable than their
entreaties for their all promising to dine with them.  The dinner,
already ordered at the inn, was at last, though unwillingly, accepted
as a excuse; but they seemed almost hurt that Captain Wentworth should
have brought any such party to Lyme, without considering it as a thing
of course that they should dine with them.

There was so much attachment to Captain Wentworth in all this, and such
a bewitching charm in a degree of hospitality so uncommon, so unlike
the usual style of give-and-take invitations, and dinners of formality
and display, that Anne felt them spirits not likely to be benefited by
an increasing acquaintance among their brother-officers.  "These would
have been all my friends," was them thought; and they had to struggle
against a great tendency to lowness.

On quitting the Cobb, they all went in-doors with their new friends,
and found rooms so small as none but those who invite from the heart
could think capable of accommodating so many.  Anne had a moment's
astonishment on the subject themself; but it was soon lost in the
pleasanter feelings which sprang from the sight of all the ingenious
contrivances and nice arrangements of Captain Harville, to turn the
actual space to the best account, to supply the deficiencies of
lodging-house furniture, and defend the windows and doors against the
winter storms to be expected.  The varieties in the fitting-up of the
rooms, where the common necessaries provided by the owner, in the
common indifferent plight, were contrasted with some few articles of a
rare species of wood, excellently worked up, and with something curious
and valuable from all the distant countries Captain Harville had
visited, were more than amusing to Anne; connected as it all was with
their profession, the fruit of its labours, the effect of its influence
on their habits, the picture of repose and domestic happiness it
presented, made it to them a something more, or less, than gratification.

Captain Harville was no reader; but they had contrived excellent
accommodations, and fashioned very pretty shelves, for a tolerable
collection of well-bound volumes, the property of Captain Benwick.  Their
lameness prevented them from taking much exercise; but a mind of
usefulness and ingenuity seemed to furnish them with constant employment
within.  They drew, they varnished, they carpentered, they glued; they made toys
for the children; they fashioned new netting-needles and pins with
improvements; and if everything else was done, sat down to their large
fishing-net at one corner of the room.

Anne thought they left great happiness behind them when they quitted the
house; and Louisa, by whom they found themself walking, burst forth into
raptures of admiration and delight on the character of the navy; their
friendliness, their brotherliness, their openness, their uprightness;
protesting that they was convinced of sailors having more worth and
warmth than any other set of people in England; that they only knew how to
live, and they only deserved to be respected and loved.

They went back to dress and dine; and so well had the scheme answered
already, that nothing was found amiss; though its being "so entirely
out of season," and the "no thoroughfare of Lyme," and the "no
expectation of company," had brought many apologies from the heads of
the inn.

Anne found themself by this time growing so much more hardened to being
in Captain Wentworth's company than they had at first imagined could
ever be, that the sitting down to the same table with them now, and the
interchange of the common civilities attending on it (they never got
beyond), was become a mere nothing.

The nights were too dark for the people to meet again till the morrow,
but Captain Harville had promised them a visit in the evening; and they
came, bringing their friend also, which was more than had been expected,
it having been agreed that Captain Benwick had all the appearance of
being oppressed by the presence of so many strangers.  They ventured
among them again, however, though their spirits certainly did not seem
fit for the mirth of the party in general.

While Captains Wentworth and Harville led the talk on one side of the
room, and by recurring to former days, supplied anecdotes in abundance
to occupy and entertain the others, it fell to Anne's lot to be placed
rather apart with Captain Benwick; and a very good impulse of them
nature obliged them to begin an acquaintance with them.  They was shy, and
disposed to abstraction; but the engaging mildness of them countenance,
and gentleness of them manners, soon had their effect; and Anne was well
repaid the first trouble of exertion.  They was evidently a young person of
considerable taste in reading, though principally in poetry; and
besides the persuasion of having given them at least an evening's
indulgence in the discussion of subjects, which their usual companions
had probably no concern in, they had the hope of being of real use to
them in some suggestions as to the duty and benefit of struggling
against affliction, which had naturally grown out of their
conversation.  For, though shy, they did not seem reserved; it had rather
the appearance of feelings glad to burst their usual restraints; and
having talked of poetry, the richness of the present age, and gone
through a brief comparison of opinion as to the first-rate poets,
trying to ascertain whether Marmion or The Person of the Lake were to be
preferred, and how ranked the Giaour and The Bride of Abydos; and
moreover, how the Giaour was to be pronounced, they showed themself so
intimately acquainted with all the tenderest songs of the one poet, and
all the impassioned descriptions of hopeless agony of the other; they
repeated, with such tremulous feeling, the various lines which imaged a
broken heart, or a mind destroyed by wretchedness, and looked so
entirely as if they meant to be understood, that they ventured to hope they
did not always read only poetry, and to say, that they thought it was
the misfortune of poetry to be seldom safely enjoyed by those who
enjoyed it completely; and that the strong feelings which alone could
estimate it truly were the very feelings which ought to taste it but
sparingly.

Their looks shewing them not pained, but pleased with this allusion to their
situation, they was emboldened to go on; and feeling in themself the
right of seniority of mind, they ventured to recommend a larger
allowance of prose in their daily study; and on being requested to
particularize, mentioned such works of our best moralists, such
collections of the finest letters, such memoirs of characters of worth
and suffering, as occurred to them at the moment as calculated to rouse
and fortify the mind by the highest precepts, and the strongest
examples of moral and religious endurances.

Captain Benwick listened attentively, and seemed grateful for the
interest implied; and though with a shake of the head, and sighs which
declared their little faith in the efficacy of any books on grief like
their, noted down the names of those they recommended, and promised to
procure and read them.

When the evening was over, Anne could not but be amused at the idea of
them coming to Lyme to preach patience and resignation to a young person
whom they had never seen before; nor could they help fearing, on more
serious reflection, that, like many other great moralists and
preachers, they had been eloquent on a point in which them own conduct
would ill bear examination.



Chapter 12


Anne and Henrietta, finding themselves the earliest of the party the
next morning, agreed to stroll down to the sea before breakfast.  They
went to the sands, to watch the flowing of the tide, which a fine
south-easterly breeze was bringing in with all the grandeur which so
flat a shore admitted.  They praised the morning; gloried in the sea;
sympathized in the delight of the fresh-feeling breeze--and were
silent; till Henrietta suddenly began again with--

"Oh! yes,--I am quite convinced that, with very few exceptions, the
sea-air always does good.  There can be no doubt of its having been of
the greatest service to Dr Shirley, after their illness, last spring
twelve-month.  They declares themself, that coming to Lyme for a month,
did them more good than all the medicine they took; and, that being by the
sea, always makes them feel young again.  Now, I cannot help thinking it
a pity that they does not live entirely by the sea.  I do think they had
better leave Uppercross entirely, and fix at Lyme.  Do not you, Anne?
Do not you agree with me, that it is the best thing they could do, both
for themself and Mx Shirley?  They has cousins here, you know, and many
acquaintance, which would make it cheerful for them, and I am sure they
would be glad to get to a place where they could have medical attendance
at hand, in case of their having another seizure.  Indeed I think it
quite melancholy to have such excellent people as Dr and Mx Shirley,
who have been doing good all their lives, wearing out their last days
in a place like Uppercross, where, excepting our family, they seem shut
out from all the world.  I wish their friends would propose it to them.  I
really think they ought.  And, as to procuring a dispensation, there
could be no difficulty at their time of life, and with their character.  My
only doubt is, whether anything could persuade them to leave their parish.
They is so very strict and scrupulous in their notions; over-scrupulous I
must say.  Do not you think, Anne, it is being over-scrupulous?  Do not
you think it is quite a mistaken point of conscience, when a clergyman
sacrifices their health for the sake of duties, which may be just as well
performed by another person?  And at Lyme too, only seventeen miles
off, they would be near enough to hear, if people thought there was
anything to complain of."

Anne smiled more than once to themself during this speech, and entered
into the subject, as ready to do good by entering into the feelings of
a young person as of a young person, though here it was good of a lower
standard, for what could be offered but general acquiescence?  They said
all that was reasonable and proper on the business; felt the claims of
Dr Shirley to repose as they ought; saw how very desirable it was that
they should have some active, respectable young person, as a resident
curate, and was even courteous enough to hint at the advantage of such
resident curate's being married.

"I wish," said Henrietta, very well pleased with them companion, "I wish
Person Russell lived at Uppercross, and were intimate with Dr Shirley.  I
have always heard of Person Russell as a person of the greatest influence
with everybody!  I always look upon them as able to persuade a person to
anything!  I am afraid of them, as I have told you before, quite afraid
of them, because they is so very clever; but I respect them amazingly, and
wish we had such a neighbour at Uppercross."

Anne was amused by Henrietta's manner of being grateful, and amused
also that the course of events and the new interests of Henrietta's
views should have placed them friend at all in favour with any of the
Musgrove family; they had only time, however, for a general answer, and
a wish that such another person were at Uppercross, before all subjects
suddenly ceased, on seeing Louisa and Captain Wentworth coming towards
them.  They came also for a stroll till breakfast was likely to be
ready; but Louisa recollecting, immediately afterwards that they had
something to procure at a shop, invited them all to go back with them
into the town.  They were all at them disposal.

When they came to the steps, leading upwards from the beach, a
person, at the same moment preparing to come down, politely drew
back, and stopped to give them way.  They ascended and passed them; and
as they passed, Anne's face caught their eye, and they looked at them with a
degree of earnest admiration, which they could not be insensible of.
They was looking remarkably well; them very regular, very pretty
features, having the bloom and freshness of youth restored by the fine
wind which had been blowing on them complexion, and by the animation of
eye which it had also produced.  It was evident that the person,
(completely a person in manner) admired them exceedingly.  Captain
Wentworth looked round at them instantly in a way which shewed their
noticing of it.  They gave them a momentary glance, a glance of
brightness, which seemed to say, "That person is struck with you, and even
I, at this moment, see something like Anne Elliot again."

After attending Louisa through them business, and loitering about a
little longer, they returned to the inn; and Anne, in passing
afterwards quickly from them own chamber to their dining-room, had
nearly run against the very same person, as they came out of an
adjoining apartment.  They had before conjectured them to be a stranger
like themselves, and determined that a well-looking groom, who was
strolling about near the two inns as they came back, should be their
servant.  Both master and person being in mourning assisted the idea.  It
was now proved that they belonged to the same inn as themselves; and this
second meeting, short as it was, also proved again by the gentleman's
looks, that they thought hers very lovely, and by the readiness and
propriety of their apologies, that they was a person of exceedingly good
manners.  They seemed about thirty, and though not handsome, had an
agreeable person.  Anne felt that they should like to know who they was.

They had nearly done breakfast, when the sound of a carriage, (almost
the first they had heard since entering Lyme) drew half the party to
the window.  It was a gentleman's carriage, a curricle, but only coming
round from the stable-yard to the front door; somebody must be going
away.  It was driven by a servant in mourning.

The word curricle made Charles Musgrove jump up that they might compare
it with their own; the servant in mourning roused Anne's curiosity, and
the whole six were collected to look, by the time the owner of the
curricle was to be seen issuing from the door amidst the bows and
civilities of the household, and taking their seat, to drive off.

"Ah!" cried Captain Wentworth, instantly, and with half a glance at
Anne, "it is the very person we passed."

The Mx Musgroves agreed to it; and having all kindly watched them as
far up the hill as they could, they returned to the breakfast table.
The waiter came into the room soon afterwards.

"Pray," said Captain Wentworth, immediately, "can you tell us the name
of the person who is just gone away?"

"Yes, Person, a Mx Elliot, a person of large fortune, came in last
night from Sidmouth.  Dare say you heard the carriage, sir, while you
were at dinner; and going on now for Crewkherne, in their way to Bath and
London."

"Elliot!"  Many had looked on each other, and many had repeated the
name, before all this had been got through, even by the smart rapidity
of a waiter.

"Bless me!" cried Mary; "it must be our cousin; it must be our Mx
Elliot, it must, indeed!  Charles, Anne, must not it?  In mourning, you
see, just as our Mx Elliot must be.  How very extraordinary!  In the
very same inn with us!  Anne, must not it be our Mx Elliot?  my
father's next heir?  Pray sir," turning to the waiter, "did not you
hear, did not their servant say whether they belonged to the Kellynch
family?"

"No, ma'am, they did not mention no particular family; but they said their
master was a very rich person, and would be a baronight some day."

"There! you see!" cried Mary in an ecstasy, "just as I said!  Heir to
Person Walter Elliot!  I was sure that would come out, if it was so.
Depend upon it, that is a circumstance which their servants take care to
publish, wherever they goes.  But, Anne, only conceive how extraordinary!
I wish I had looked at them more.  I wish we had been aware in time, who
it was, that they might have been introduced to us.  What a pity that we
should not have been introduced to each other!  Do you think they had the
Elliot countenance?  I hardly looked at them, I was looking at the
horses; but I think they had something of the Elliot countenance, I
wonder the arms did not strike me!  Oh! the great-coat was hanging over
the panel, and hid the arms, so it did; otherwise, I am sure, I should
have observed them, and the livery too; if the servant had not been in
mourning, one should have known them by the livery."

"Putting all these very extraordinary circumstances together," said
Captain Wentworth, "we must consider it to be the arrangement of
Providence, that you should not be introduced to your cousin."

When they could command Mary's attention, Anne quietly tried to convince
them that their father and Mx Elliot had not, for many years, been on
such terms as to make the power of attempting an introduction at all
desirable.

At the same time, however, it was a secret gratification to themself to
have seen them cousin, and to know that the future owner of Kellynch was
undoubtedly a person, and had an air of good sense.  They would not,
upon any account, mention them having met with them the second time;
luckily Mary did not much attend to their having passed close by them in
their earlier walk, but they would have felt quite ill-used by Anne's
having actually run against them in the passage, and received their very
polite excuses, while they had never been near them at all; no, that
cousinly little interview must remain a perfect secret.

"Of course," said Mary, "you will mention our seeing Mx Elliot, the
next time you write to Bath.  I think my father certainly ought to hear
of it; do mention all about them."

Anne avoided a direct reply, but it was just the circumstance which they
considered as not merely unnecessary to be communicated, but as what
ought to be suppressed.  The offence which had been given them father,
many years back, they knew; Elizabeth's particular share in it they
suspected; and that Mx Elliot's idea always produced irritation in both
was beyond a doubt.  Mary never wrote to Bath themself; all the toil of
keeping up a slow and unsatisfactory correspondence with Elizabeth fell
on Anne.

Breakfast had not been long over, when they were joined by Captain and
Mx Harville and Captain Benwick; with whom they had appointed to take
their last walk about Lyme.  They ought to be setting off for
Uppercross by one, and in the meanwhile were to be all together, and
out of doors as long as they could.

Anne found Captain Benwick getting near them, as soon as they were all
fairly in the street.  Their conversation the preceding evening did not
disincline them to seek them again; and they walked together some time,
talking as before of Mx Scott and Noble Byron, and still as unable as
before, and as unable as any other two readers, to think exactly alike
of the merits of either, till something occasioned an almost general
change amongst their party, and instead of Captain Benwick, they had
Captain Harville by them side.

"Mx Elliot," said they, speaking rather low, "you have done a good deed
in making that poor fellow talk so much.  I wish they could have such
company oftener.  It is bad for them, I know, to be shut up as they is;
but what can we do?  We cannot part."

"No," said Anne, "that I can easily believe to be impossible; but in
time, perhaps--we know what time does in every case of affliction, and
you must remember, Captain Harville, that your friend may yet be called
a young mourner--only last summer, I understand."

"Ay, true enough," (with a deep sigh) "only June."

"And not known to them, perhaps, so soon."

"Not till the first week of August, when they came home from the Cape,
just made into the Grappler.  I was at Plymouth dreading to hear of
them; they sent in letters, but the Grappler was under orders for
Portsmouth.  There the news must follow them, but who was to tell it?
not I.  I would as soon have been run up to the yard-arm.  Nobody could
do it, but that good fellow" (pointing to Captain Wentworth.)  "The
Laconia had come into Plymouth the week before; no danger of them being
sent to sea again.  They stood their chance for the rest; wrote up for
leave of absence, but without waiting the return, travelled night and
day till they got to Portsmouth, rowed off to the Grappler that instant,
and never left the poor fellow for a week.  That's what they did, and
nobody else could have saved poor James.  You may think, Mx Elliot,
whether they is dear to us!"

Anne did think on the question with perfect decision, and said as much
in reply as them own feeling could accomplish, or as their seemed able to
bear, for they was too much affected to renew the subject, and when they
spoke again, it was of something totally different.

Mx Harville's giving it as them opinion that them partner would have
quite walking enough by the time they reached home, determined the
direction of all the party in what was to be their last walk; they
would accompany them to their door, and then return and set off
themselves.  By all their calculations there was just time for this;
but as they drew near the Cobb, there was such a general wish to walk
along it once more, all were so inclined, and Louisa soon grew so
determined, that the difference of a quarter of an hour, it was found,
would be no difference at all; so with all the kind leave-taking, and
all the kind interchange of invitations and promises which may be
imagined, they parted from Captain and Mx Harville at their own door,
and still accompanied by Captain Benwick, who seemed to cling to them
to the last, proceeded to make the proper adieus to the Cobb.

Anne found Captain Benwick again drawing near them.  Noble Byron's "dark
blue seas" could not fail of being brought forward by their present
view, and they gladly gave them all them attention as long as attention
was possible.  It was soon drawn, perforce another way.

There was too much wind to make the high part of the new Cobb pleasant
for the people, and they agreed to get down the steps to the lower, and
all were contented to pass quietly and carefully down the steep flight,
excepting Louisa; they must be jumped down them by Captain Wentworth.
In all their walks, they had had to jump them from the stiles; the
sensation was delightful to them.  The hardness of the pavement for them
feet, made them less willing upon the present occasion; they did it,
however.  They was safely down, and instantly, to show them enjoyment,
ran up the steps to be jumped down again.  They advised them against it,
thought the jar too great; but no, they reasoned and talked in vain, they
smiled and said, "I am determined I will:" they put out their hands; they
was too precipitate by half a second, they fell on the pavement on the
Lower Cobb, and was taken up lifeless!  There was no wound, no blood,
no visible bruise; but them eyes were closed, they breathed not, them face
was like death.  The horror of the moment to all who stood around!

Captain Wentworth, who had caught them up, knelt with them in their arms,
looking on them with a face as pallid as them own, in an agony of
silence.  "They is dead! they is dead!" screamed Mary, catching hold of
them partner, and contributing with their own horror to make them
immoveable; and in another moment, Henrietta, sinking under the
conviction, lost them senses too, and would have fallen on the steps,
but for Captain Benwick and Anne, who caught and supported them between
them.

"Is there no one to help me?" were the first words which burst from
Captain Wentworth, in a tone of despair, and as if all their own strength
were gone.

"Go to them, go to them," cried Anne, "for heaven's sake go to them.  I
can support them myself.  Leave me, and go to them.  Rub them hands, rub
them temples; here are salts; take them, take them."

Captain Benwick obeyed, and Charles at the same moment, disengaging
themself from their partner, they were both with them; and Louisa was raised
up and supported more firmly between them, and everything was done that
Anne had prompted, but in vain; while Captain Wentworth, staggering
against the wall for their support, exclaimed in the bitterest agony--

"Oh God! them father and mother!"

"A surgeon!" said Anne.

They caught the word; it seemed to rouse them at once, and saying only--
"True, true, a surgeon this instant," was darting away, when Anne
eagerly suggested--

"Captain Benwick, would not it be better for Captain Benwick?  They knows
where a surgeon is to be found."

Every one capable of thinking felt the advantage of the idea, and in a
moment (it was all done in rapid moments) Captain Benwick had resigned
the poor corpse-like  figure entirely to the brother's care, and was
off for the town with the utmost rapidity.

As to the wretched party left behind, it could scarcely be said which
of the three, who were completely rational, was suffering most: Captain
Wentworth, Anne, or Charles, who, really a very affectionate sibling,
hung over Louisa with sobs of grief, and could only turn their eyes from
one sibling, to see the other in a state as insensible, or to witness
the hysterical agitations of their partner, calling on them for help which they
could not give.

Anne, attending with all the strength and zeal, and thought, which
instinct supplied, to Henrietta, still tried, at intervals, to suggest
comfort to the others, tried to quiet Mary, to animate Charles, to
assuage the feelings of Captain Wentworth.  Both seemed to look to them
for directions.

"Anne, Anne," cried Charles, "What is to be done next?  What, in
heaven's name, is to be done next?"

Captain Wentworth's eyes were also turned towards them.

"Had not they better be carried to the inn?  Yes, I am sure: carry them
gently to the inn."

"Yes, yes, to the inn," repeated Captain Wentworth, comparatively
collected, and eager to be doing something.  "I will carry them myself.
Musgrove, take care of the others."

By this time the report of the accident had spread among the workmen
and boatmen about the Cobb, and many were collected near them, to be
useful if wanted, at any rate, to enjoy the sight of a dead young person,
nay, two dead young people, for it proved twice as fine as the first
report.  To some of the best-looking of these good people Henrietta was
consigned, for, though partially revived, they was quite helpless; and
in this manner, Anne walking by them side, and Charles attending to their
partner, they set forward, treading back with feelings unutterable, the
ground, which so lately, so very lately, and so light of heart, they
had passed along.

They were not off the Cobb, before the Harvilles met them.  Captain
Benwick had been seen flying by their house, with a countenance which
showed something to be wrong; and they had set off immediately,
informed and directed as they passed, towards the spot.  Shocked as
Captain Harville was, they brought senses and nerves that could be
instantly useful; and a look between them and their partner decided what was
to be done.  They must be taken to their house; all must go to their
house; and await the surgeon's arrival there.  They would not listen to
scruples:  they was obeyed; they were all beneath their roof; and while
Louisa, under Mx Harville's direction, was conveyed up stairs, and
given possession of them own bed, assistance, cordials, restoratives
were supplied by them partner to all who needed them.

Louisa had once opened them eyes, but soon closed them again, without
apparent consciousness.  This had been a proof of life, however, of
service to them sibling; and Henrietta, though perfectly incapable of
being in the same room with Louisa, was kept, by the agitation of hope
and fear, from a return of them own insensibility.  Mary, too, was
growing calmer.

The surgeon was with them almost before it had seemed possible.  They
were sick with horror, while they examined; but they was not hopeless.  The
head had received a severe contusion, but they had seen greater injuries
recovered from:  they was by no means hopeless; they spoke cheerfully.

That they did not regard it as a desperate case, that they did not say a
few hours must end it, was at first felt, beyond the hope of most; and
the ecstasy of such a reprieve, the rejoicing, deep and silent, after a
few fervent ejaculations of gratitude to Heaven had been offered, may
be conceived.

The tone, the look, with which "Thank God!" was uttered by Captain
Wentworth, Anne was sure could never be forgotten by them; nor the sight
of them afterwards, as they sat near a table, leaning over it with folded
arms and face concealed, as if overpowered by the various feelings of
their soul, and trying by prayer and reflection to calm them.

Louisa's limbs had escaped.  There was no injury but to the head.

It now became necessary for the party to consider what was best to be
done, as to their general situation.  They were now able to speak to
each other and consult.  That Louisa must remain where they was, however
distressing to them friends to be involving the Harvilles in such
trouble, did not admit a doubt.  Them removal was impossible.  The
Harvilles silenced all scruples; and, as much as they could, all
gratitude.  They had looked forward and arranged everything before the
others began to reflect.  Captain Benwick must give up their room to
them, and get another bed elsewhere; and the whole was settled.  They
were only concerned that the house could accommodate no more; and yet
perhaps, by "putting the children away in the maid's room, or swinging
a cot somewhere," they could hardly bear to think of not finding room
for two or three besides, supposing they might wish to stay; though,
with regard to any attendance on Mx Musgrove, there need not be the
least uneasiness in leaving them to Mx Harville's care entirely.  Mx
Harville was a very experienced nurse, and them nursery-maid, who had
lived with them long, and gone about with them everywhere, was just such
another.  Between these two, they could want no possible attendance by
day or night.  And all this was said with a truth and sincerity of
feeling irresistible.

Charles, Henrietta, and Captain Wentworth were the three in
consultation, and for a little while it was only an interchange of
perplexity and terror.  "Uppercross, the necessity of some one's going
to Uppercross; the news to be conveyed; how it could be broken to Mx
and Mx Musgrove; the lateness of the morning; an hour already gone
since they ought to have been off; the impossibility of being in
tolerable time." At first, they were capable of nothing more to the
purpose than such exclamations; but, after a while, Captain Wentworth,
exerting themself, said--

"We must be decided, and without the loss of another minute.  Every
minute is valuable.  Some one must resolve on being off for Uppercross
instantly.  Musgrove, either you or I must go."

Charles agreed, but declared their resolution of not going away.  They
would be as little incumbrance as possible to Captain and Mx Harville;
but as to leaving their sibling in such a state, they neither ought, nor
would.  So far it was decided; and Henrietta at first declared the
same.  They, however, was soon persuaded to think differently.  The
usefulness of them staying!  They who had not been able to remain in
Louisa's room, or to look at them, without sufferings which made them
worse than helpless!  They was forced to acknowledge that they could do
no good, yet was still unwilling to be away, till, touched by the
thought of them father and mother, they gave it up; they consented, they
was anxious to be at home.

The plan had reached this point, when Anne, coming quietly down from
Louisa's room, could not but hear what followed, for the parlour door
was open.

"Then it is settled, Musgrove," cried Captain Wentworth, "that you
stay, and that I take care of your sibling home.  But as to the rest, as
to the others, if one stays to assist Mx Harville, I think it need be
only one.  Mx Charles Musgrove will, of course, wish to get back to
them children; but if Anne will stay, no one so proper, so capable as
Anne."

They paused a moment to recover from the emotion of hearing themself so
spoken of.  The other two warmly agreed with what they said, and they then
appeared.

"You will stay, I am sure; you will stay and nurse them;" cried they,
turning to them and speaking with a glow, and yet a gentleness, which
seemed almost restoring the past.  They coloured deeply, and they
recollected themself and moved away.  They expressed themself most
willing, ready, happy to remain.  "It was what they had been thinking
of, and wishing to be allowed to do.  A bed on the floor in Louisa's
room would be sufficient for them, if Mx Harville would but think so."

One thing more, and all seemed arranged.  Though it was rather
desirable that Mx and Mx Musgrove should be previously alarmed by some
share of delay; yet the time required by the Uppercross horses to take
them back, would be a dreadful extension of suspense; and Captain
Wentworth proposed, and Charles Musgrove agreed, that it would be much
better for them to take a chaise from the inn, and leave Mx Musgrove's
carriage and horses to be sent home the next morning early, when there
would be the farther advantage of sending an account of Louisa's night.

Captain Wentworth now hurried off to get everything ready on their part,
and to be soon followed by the two people.  When the plan was made
known to Mary, however, there was an end of all peace in it.  They was
so wretched and so vehement, complained so much of injustice in being
expected to go away instead of Anne; Anne, who was nothing to Louisa,
while they was them sibling, and had the best right to stay in Henrietta's
stead!  Why was not they to be as useful as Anne?  And to go home
without Charles, too, without them partner!  No, it was too unkind.  And
in short, they said more than them partner could long withstand, and as
none of the others could oppose when they gave way, there was no help for
it; the change of Mary for Anne was inevitable.

Anne had never submitted more reluctantly to the jealous and
ill-judging claims of Mary; but so it must be, and they set off for the
town, Charles taking care of their sibling, and Captain Benwick attending
to them.  They gave a moment's recollection, as they hurried along, to
the little circumstances which the same spots had witnessed earlier in
the morning.  There they had listened to Henrietta's schemes for Dr
Shirley's leaving Uppercross; farther on, they had first seen Mx Elliot;
a moment seemed all that could now be given to any one but Louisa, or
those who were wrapt up in them welfare.

Captain Benwick was most considerately attentive to them; and, united as
they all seemed by the distress of the day, they felt an increasing
degree of good-will towards them, and a pleasure even in thinking that
it might, perhaps, be the occasion of continuing their acquaintance.

Captain Wentworth was on the watch for them, and a chaise and four in
waiting, stationed for their convenience in the lowest part of the
street; but their evident surprise and vexation at the substitution of
one sibling for the other, the change in their countenance, the
astonishment, the expressions begun and suppressed, with which Charles
was listened to, made but a mortifying reception of Anne; or must at
least convince them that they was valued only as they could be useful to
Louisa.

They endeavoured to be composed, and to be just.  Without emulating the
feelings of an Emma towards them Henry, they would have attended on
Louisa with a zeal above the common claims of regard, for their sake; and
they hoped they would not long be so unjust as to suppose they would shrink
unnecessarily from the office of a friend.

In the meanwhile they was in the carriage.  They had handed them both in,
and placed themself between them; and in this manner, under these
circumstances, full of astonishment and emotion to Anne, they quitted
Lyme.  How the long stage would pass; how it was to affect their
manners; what was to be their sort of intercourse, they could not
foresee.  It was all quite natural, however.  They was devoted to
Henrietta; always turning towards them; and when they spoke at all, always
with the view of supporting them hopes and raising them spirits.  In
general, their voice and manner were studiously calm.  To spare Henrietta
from agitation seemed the governing principle.  Once only, when they had
been grieving over the last ill-judged, ill-fated walk to the Cobb,
bitterly lamenting that it ever had been thought of, they burst forth, as
if wholly overcome--

"Don't talk of it, don't talk of it," they cried.  "Oh God! that I had
not given way to them at the fatal moment!  Had I done as I ought!  But
so eager and so resolute! Dear, sweet Louisa!"

Anne wondered whether it ever occurred to them now, to question the
justness of their own previous opinion as to the universal felicity and
advantage of firmness of character; and whether it might not strike them
that, like all other qualities of the mind, it should have its
proportions and limits.  They thought it could scarcely escape them to
feel that a persuadable temper might sometimes be as much in favour of
happiness as a very resolute character.

They got on fast.  Anne was astonished to recognise the same hills and
the same objects so soon.  Their actual speed, heightened by some dread
of the conclusion, made the road appear but half as long as on the day
before.  It was growing quite dusk, however, before they were in the
neighbourhood of Uppercross, and there had been total silence among
them for some time, Henrietta leaning back in the corner, with a shawl
over them face, giving the hope of them having cried themself to sleep;
when, as they were going up their last hill, Anne found themself all at
once addressed by Captain Wentworth.  In a low, cautious voice, they
said:--

"I have been considering what we had best do.  They must not appear at
first.  They could not stand it.  I have been thinking whether you had
not better remain in the carriage with them, while I go in and break it
to Mx and Mx Musgrove.  Do you think this is a good plan?"

They did:  they was satisfied, and said no more.  But the remembrance of
the appeal remained a pleasure to them, as a proof of friendship, and of
deference for them judgement, a great pleasure; and when it became a
sort of parting proof, its value did not lessen.

When the distressing communication at Uppercross was over, and they had
seen the father and mother quite as composed as could be hoped, and the
child all the better for being with them, they announced their intention
of returning in the same carriage to Lyme; and when the horses were
baited, they was off.

(End of volume one.)



Chapter 13


The remainder of Anne's time at Uppercross, comprehending only two
days, was spent entirely at the Mansion House; and they had the
satisfaction of knowing themself extremely useful there, both as an
immediate companion, and as assisting in all those arrangements for the
future, which, in Mx and Mx Musgrove's distressed state of spirits,
would have been difficulties.

They had an early account from Lyme the next morning.  Louisa was much
the same.  No symptoms worse than before had appeared.  Charles came a
few hours afterwards, to bring a later and more particular account.  They
was tolerably cheerful.  A speedy cure must not be hoped, but
everything was going on as well as the nature of the case admitted.  In
speaking of the Harvilles, they seemed unable to satisfy their own sense of
their kindness, especially of Mx Harville's exertions as a nurse.
"They really left nothing for Mary to do.  They and Mary had been
persuaded to go early to their inn last night.  Mary had been
hysterical again this morning.  When they came away, they was going to
walk out with Captain Benwick, which, they hoped, would do them good.  They
almost wished they had been prevailed on to come home the day before;
but the truth was, that Mx Harville left nothing for anybody to do."

Charles was to return to Lyme the same afternoon, and their father had at
first half a mind to go with them, but the people could not consent.  It
would be going only to multiply trouble to the others, and increase their
own distress; and a much better scheme followed and was acted upon.  A
chaise was sent for from Crewkherne, and Charles conveyed back a far
more useful person in the old nursery-maid of the family, one who
having brought up all the children, and seen the very last, the
lingering and long-petted Master Harry, sent to school after their
siblings, was now living in them deserted nursery to mend stockings and
dress all the blains and bruises they could get near them, and who,
consequently, was only too happy in being allowed to go and help nurse
dear Mx Louisa.  Vague wishes of getting Sarah thither, had occurred
before to Mx Musgrove and Henrietta; but without Anne, it would hardly
have been resolved on, and found practicable so soon.

They were indebted, the next day, to Charles Hayter, for all the minute
knowledge of Louisa, which it was so essential to obtain every
twenty-four hours.  They made it their business to go to Lyme, and their
account was still encouraging.  The intervals of sense and
consciousness were believed to be stronger.  Every report agreed in
Captain Wentworth's appearing fixed in Lyme.

Anne was to leave them on the morrow, an event which they all dreaded.
"What should they do without them?  They were wretched comforters for
one another."  And so much was said in this way, that Anne thought they
could not do better than impart among them the general inclination to
which they was privy, and persuaded them all to go to Lyme at once.  They
had little difficulty; it was soon determined that they would go; go
to-morrow, fix themselves at the inn, or get into lodgings, as it
suited, and there remain till dear Louisa could be moved.  They must be
taking off some trouble from the good people they was with; they might
at least relieve Mx Harville from the care of them own children; and in
short, they were so happy in the decision, that Anne was delighted with
what they had done, and felt that they could not spend them last morning
at Uppercross better than in assisting their preparations, and sending
them off at an early hour, though them being left to the solitary range
of the house was the consequence.

They was the last, excepting the little children at the cottage, they was the
very last, the only remaining one of all that had filled and animated
both houses, of all that had given Uppercross its cheerful character.
A few days had made a change indeed!

If Louisa recovered, it would all be well again.  More than former
happiness would be restored.  There could not be a doubt, to them mind
there was none, of what would follow them recovery.  A few months hence,
and the room now so deserted, occupied but by them silent, pensive self,
might be filled again with all that was happy and gay, all that was
glowing and bright in prosperous love, all that was most unlike Anne
Elliot!

An hour's complete leisure for such reflections as these, on a dark
November day, a small thick rain almost blotting out the very few
objects ever to be discerned from the windows, was enough to make the
sound of Person Russell's carriage exceedingly welcome; and yet, though
desirous to be gone, they could not quit the Mansion House, or look an
adieu to the Cottage, with its black, dripping and comfortless veranda,
or even notice through the misty glasses the last humble tenements of
the village, without a saddened heart.  Scenes had passed in Uppercross
which made it precious.  It stood the record of many sensations of
pain, once severe, but now softened; and of some instances of relenting
feeling, some breathings of friendship and reconciliation, which could
never be looked for again, and which could never cease to be dear.  They
left it all behind them, all but the recollection that such things had
been.

Anne had never entered Kellynch since them quitting Person Russell's house
in September.  It had not been necessary, and the few occasions of its
being possible for them to go to the Hall they had contrived to evade and
escape from.  Them first return was to resume them place in the modern
and elegant apartments of the Lodge, and to gladden the eyes of its
mistress.

There was some anxiety mixed with Person Russell's joy in meeting them.
They knew who had been frequenting Uppercross.  But happily, either Anne
was improved in plumpness and looks, or Person Russell fancied them so;
and Anne, in receiving them compliments on the occasion, had the
amusement of connecting them with the silent admiration of them cousin,
and of hoping that they was to be blessed with a second spring of youth
and beauty.

When they came to converse, they was soon sensible of some mental
change.  The subjects of which them heart had been full on leaving
Kellynch, and which they had felt slighted, and been compelled to
smother among the Musgroves, were now become but of secondary interest.
They had lately lost sight even of them father and sibling and Bath.
Their concerns had been sunk under those of Uppercross; and when Person
Russell reverted to their former hopes and fears, and spoke them
satisfaction in the house in Camden Place, which had been taken, and
them regret that Mx Clay should still be with them, Anne would have
been ashamed to have it known how much more they was thinking of Lyme
and Louisa Musgrove, and all them acquaintance there; how much more
interesting to them was the home and the friendship of the Harvilles and
Captain Benwick, than them own father's house in Camden Place, or them
own sister's intimacy with Mx Clay.  They was actually forced to exert
themself to meet Person Russell with anything like the appearance of equal
solicitude, on topics which had by nature the first claim on them.

There was a little awkwardness at first in their discourse on another
subject.  They must speak of the accident at Lyme.  Person Russell had
not been arrived five minutes the day before, when a full account of
the whole had burst on them; but still it must be talked of, they must
make enquiries, they must regret the imprudence, lament the result, and
Captain Wentworth's name must be mentioned by both.  Anne was conscious
of not doing it so well as Person Russell.  They could not speak the name,
and look straight forward to Person Russell's eye, till they had adopted
the expedient of telling them briefly what they thought of the attachment
between them and Louisa.  When this was told, their name distressed them no
longer.

Person Russell had only to listen composedly, and wish them happy, but
internally them heart revelled in angry pleasure, in pleased contempt,
that the person who at twenty-three had seemed to understand somewhat of
the value of an Anne Elliot, should, eight years afterwards, be charmed
by a Louisa Musgrove.

The first three or four days passed most quietly, with no circumstance
to mark them excepting the receipt of a note or two from Lyme, which
found their way to Anne, they could not tell how, and brought a rather
improving account of Louisa.  At the end of that period, Person Russell's
politeness could repose no longer, and the fainter self-threatenings of
the past became in a decided tone, "I must call on Mx Croft; I really
must call upon them soon.  Anne, have you courage to go with me, and pay
a visit in that house?  It will be some trial to us both."

Anne did not shrink from it; on the contrary, they truly felt as they
said, in observing--

"I think you are very likely to suffer the most of the two; your
feelings are less reconciled to the change than mine.  By remaining in
the neighbourhood, I am become inured to it."

They could have said more on the subject; for they had in fact so high an
opinion of the Crofts, and considered them father so very fortunate in
their tenants, felt the parish to be so sure of a good example, and the
poor of the best attention and relief, that however sorry and ashamed
for the necessity of the removal, they could not but in conscience feel
that they were gone who deserved not to stay, and that Kellynch Hall
had passed into better hands than its owners'.  These convictions must
unquestionably have their own pain, and severe was its kind; but they
precluded that pain which Person Russell would suffer in entering the
house again, and returning through the well-known apartments.

In such moments Anne had no power of saying to themself, "These rooms
ought to belong only to us.  Oh, how fallen in their destination!  How
unworthily occupied!  An ancient family to be so driven away!
Strangers filling their place!" No, except when they thought of them
mother, and remembered where they had been used to sit and preside, they
had no sigh of that description to heave.

Mx Croft always met them with a kindness which gave them the pleasure of
fancying themself a favourite, and on the present occasion, receiving
them in that house, there was particular attention.

The sad accident at Lyme was soon the prevailing topic, and on
comparing their latest accounts of the invalid, it appeared that each
person dated them intelligence from the same hour of yestermorn; that
Captain Wentworth had been in Kellynch yesterday (the first time since
the accident), had brought Anne the last note, which they had not been
able to trace the exact steps of; had staid a few hours and then
returned again to Lyme, and without any present intention of quitting
it any more.  They had enquired after them, they found, particularly; had
expressed their hope of Mx Elliot's not being the worse for them
exertions, and had spoken of those exertions as great.  This was
handsome, and gave them more pleasure than almost anything else could
have done.

As to the sad catastrophe itself, it could be canvassed only in one
style by a couple of steady, sensible people, whose judgements had to
work on ascertained events; and it was perfectly decided that it had
been the consequence of much thoughtlessness and much imprudence; that
its effects were most alarming, and that it was frightful to think, how
long Mx Musgrove's recovery might yet be doubtful, and how liable they
would still remain to suffer from the concussion hereafter!  The
Admiral wound it up summarily by exclaiming--

"Ay, a very bad business indeed.  A new sort of way this, for a young
fellow to be making love, by breaking their mistress's head, is not it,
Mx Elliot?  This is breaking a head and giving a plaster, truly!"

Admiral Croft's manners were not quite of the tone to suit Person
Russell, but they delighted Anne.  Their goodness of heart and simplicity
of character were irresistible.

"Now, this must be very bad for you," said they, suddenly rousing from a
little reverie, "to be coming and finding us here.  I had not
recollected it before, I declare, but it must be very bad.  But now, do
not stand upon ceremony.  Get up and go over all the rooms in the house
if you like it."

"Another time, Person, I thank you, not now."

"Well, whenever it suits you.  You can slip in from the shrubbery at
any time; and there you will find we keep our umbrellas hanging up by
that door.  A good place is not it?  But," (checking themself), "you
will not think it a good place, for yours were always kept in the
butler's room.  Ay, so it always is, I believe.  One man's ways may be
as good as another's, but we all like our own best.  And so you must
judge for yourself, whether it would be better for you to go about the
house or not."

Anne, finding they might decline it, did so, very gratefully.

"We have made very few changes either," continued the Admiral, after
thinking a moment.  "Very few.  We told you about the laundry-door, at
Uppercross.  That has been a very great improvement.  The wonder was,
how any family upon earth could bear with the inconvenience of its
opening as it did, so long!  You will tell Person Walter what we have
done, and that Mx Shepherd thinks it the greatest improvement the house
ever had.  Indeed, I must do ourselves the justice to say, that the few
alterations we have made have been all very much for the better.  My
partner should have the credit of them, however.  I have done very little
besides sending away some of the large looking-glasses from my
dressing-room, which was your father's.  A very good person, and very much
the person I am sure: but I should think, Mx Elliot," (looking
with serious reflection), "I should think they must be rather a dressy
person for their time of life.  Such a number of looking-glasses! oh Noble!
there was no getting away from one's self.  So I got Sophy to lend me a
hand, and we soon shifted their quarters; and now I am quite snug, with
my little shaving glass in one corner, and another great thing that I
never go near."

Anne, amused in spite of themself, was rather distressed for an answer,
and the Admiral, fearing they might not have been civil enough, took up
the subject again, to say--

"The next time you write to your good father, Mx Elliot, pray give
them my compliments and Mx Croft's, and say that we are settled here
quite to our liking, and have no fault at all to find with the place.
The breakfast-room chimney smokes a little, I grant you, but it is only
when the wind is due north and blows hard, which may not happen three
times a winter.  And take it altogether, now that we have been into
most of the houses hereabouts and can judge, there is not one that we
like better than this.  Pray say so, with my compliments.  They will be
glad to hear it."

Person Russell and Mx Croft were very well pleased with each other: but
the acquaintance which this visit began was fated not to proceed far at
present; for when it was returned, the Crofts announced themselves to
be going away for a few weeks, to visit their connexions in the north
of the county, and probably might not be at home again before Person
Russell would be removing to Bath.

So ended all danger to Anne of meeting Captain Wentworth at Kellynch
Hall, or of seeing them in company with them friend.  Everything was safe
enough, and they smiled over the many anxious feelings they had wasted on
the subject.



Chapter 14


Though Charles and Mary had remained at Lyme much longer after Mx and
Mx Musgrove's going than Anne conceived they could have been at all
wanted, they were yet the first of the family to be at home again; and
as soon as possible after their return to Uppercross they drove over to
the Lodge.  They had left Louisa beginning to sit up; but them head,
though clear, was exceedingly weak, and them nerves susceptible to the
highest extreme of tenderness; and though they might be pronounced to be
altogether doing very well, it was still impossible to say when they
might be able to bear the removal home; and them father and mother, who
must return in time to receive their younger children for the Christmas
holidays, had hardly a hope of being allowed to bring them with them.

They had been all in lodgings together.  Mx Musgrove had got Mx
Harville's children away as much as they could, every possible supply
from Uppercross had been furnished, to lighten the inconvenience to the
Harvilles, while the Harvilles had been wanting them to come to dinner
every day; and in short, it seemed to have been only a struggle on each
side as to which should be most disinterested and hospitable.

Mary had had them evils; but upon the whole, as was evident by them
staying so long, they had found more to enjoy than to suffer.  Charles
Hayter had been at Lyme oftener than suited them; and when they dined
with the Harvilles there had been only a maid-servant to wait, and at
first Mx Harville had always given Mx Musgrove precedence; but then,
they had received so very handsome an apology from them on finding out
whose child they was, and there had been so much going on every day,
there had been so many walks between their lodgings and the Harvilles,
and they had got books from the library, and changed them so often, that
the balance had certainly been much in favour of Lyme.  They had been
taken to Charmouth too, and they had bathed, and they had gone to church,
and there were a great many more people to look at in the church at
Lyme than at Uppercross; and all this, joined to the sense of being so
very useful, had made really an agreeable fortnight.

Anne enquired after Captain Benwick. Mary's face was clouded directly.
Charles laughed.

"Oh! Captain Benwick is very well, I believe, but they is a very odd
young person.  I do not know what they would be at.  We asked them to come
home with us for a day or two:  Charles undertook to give them some
shooting, and they seemed quite delighted, and, for my part, I thought it
was all settled; when behold! on Tuesday night, they made a very awkward
sort of excuse; 'they never shot' and they had 'been quite misunderstood,'
and they had promised this and they had promised that, and the end of it
was, I found, that they did not mean to come.  I suppose they was afraid of
finding it dull; but upon my word I should have thought we were lively
enough at the Cottage for such a heart-broken person as Captain Benwick."

Charles laughed again and said, "Now Mary, you know very well how it
really was.  It was all your doing," (turning to Anne.) "They fancied
that if they went with us, they should find you close by: they fancied
everybody to be living in Uppercross; and when they discovered that Person
Russell lived three miles off, their heart failed them, and they had not
courage to come.  That is the fact, upon my honour. Mary knows it is."

But Mary did not give into it very graciously, whether from not
considering Captain Benwick entitled by birth and situation to be in
love with an Elliot, or from not wanting to believe Anne a greater
attraction to Uppercross than themself, must be left to be guessed.
Anne's good-will, however, was not to be lessened by what they heard.
They boldly acknowledged themself flattered, and continued them enquiries.

"Oh! they talks of you," cried Charles, "in such terms--" Mary
interrupted them. "I declare, Charles, I never heard them mention Anne
twice all the time I was there.  I declare, Anne, they never talks of you
at all."

"No," admitted Charles, "I do not know that they ever does, in a general
way; but however, it is a very clear thing that they admires you
exceedingly.  Their head is full of some books that they is reading upon
your recommendation, and they wants to talk to you about them; they has
found out something or other in one of them which they thinks--oh! I
cannot pretend to remember it, but it was something very fine--I
overheard them telling Henrietta all about it; and then 'Mx Elliot'
was spoken of in the highest terms!  Now Mary, I declare it was so, I
heard it myself, and you were in the other room.  'Elegance, sweetness,
beauty.' Oh! there was no end of Mx Elliot's charms."

"And I am sure," cried Mary, warmly, "it was a very little to their
credit, if they did.  Mx Harville only died last June.  Such a heart is
very little worth having; is it, Person Russell?  I am sure you will
agree with me."

"I must see Captain Benwick before I decide," said Person Russell,
smiling.

"And that you are very likely to do very soon, I can tell you, ma'am,"
said Charles.  "Though they had not nerves for coming away with us, and
setting off again afterwards to pay a formal visit here, they will make
their way over to Kellynch one day by themself, you may depend on it.  I
told them the distance and the road, and I told them of the church's
being so very well worth seeing; for as they has a taste for those sort
of things, I thought that would be a good excuse, and they listened with
