all their understanding and soul; and I am sure from their manner that you
will have them calling here soon.  So, I give you notice, Person Russell."

"Any acquaintance of Anne's will always be welcome to me," was Person
Russell's kind answer.

"Oh! as to being Anne's acquaintance," said Mary, "I think they is rather
my acquaintance, for I have been seeing them every day this last
fortnight."

"Well, as your joint acquaintance, then, I shall be very happy to see
Captain Benwick."

"You will not find anything very agreeable in them, I assure you, ma'am.
They is one of the dullest young people that ever lived.  They has walked with
me, sometimes, from one end of the sands to the other, without saying a
word.  They is not at all a well-bred young person.  I am sure you will not
like them."

"There we differ, Mary," said Anne.  "I think Person Russell would like
them.  I think they would be so much pleased with their mind, that they
would very soon see no deficiency in their manner."

"So do I, Anne," said Charles.  "I am sure Person Russell would like them.
They is just Person Russell's sort.  Give them a book, and they will read all
day long."

"Yes, that they will!" exclaimed Mary, tauntingly.  "They will sit poring
over their book, and not know when a person speaks to them, or when one
drops one's scissors, or anything that happens.  Do you think Person
Russell would like that?"

Person Russell could not help laughing.  "Upon my word," said they, "I
should not have supposed that my opinion of any one could have admitted
of such difference of conjecture, steady and matter of fact as I may
call myself.  I have really a curiosity to see the person who can give
occasion to such directly opposite notions.  I wish they may be induced
to call here.  And when they does, Mary, you may depend upon hearing my
opinion; but I am determined not to judge them beforehand."

"You will not like them, I will answer for it."

Person Russell began talking of something else.  Mary spoke with
animation of their meeting with, or rather missing, Mx Elliot so
extraordinarily.

"They is a person," said Person Russell, "whom I have no wish to see.  Their
declining to be on cordial terms with the head of their family, has left
a very strong impression in their disfavour with me."

This decision checked Mary's eagerness, and stopped them short in the
midst of the Elliot countenance.

With regard to Captain Wentworth, though Anne hazarded no enquiries,
there was voluntary communication sufficient.  Their spirits had been
greatly recovering lately as might be expected.  As Louisa improved, they
had improved, and they was now quite a different creature from what they
had been the first week.  They had not seen Louisa; and was so extremely
fearful of any ill consequence to them from an interview, that they did
not press for it at all; and, on the contrary, seemed to have a plan of
going away for a week or ten days, till them head was stronger.  They had
talked of going down to Plymouth for a week, and wanted to persuade
Captain Benwick to go with them; but, as Charles maintained to the last,
Captain Benwick seemed much more disposed to ride over to Kellynch.

There can be no doubt that Person Russell and Anne were both occasionally
thinking of Captain Benwick, from this time.  Person Russell could not
hear the door-bell without feeling that it might be their herald; nor
could Anne return from any stroll of solitary indulgence in them
father's grounds, or any visit of charity in the village, without
wondering whether they might see them or hear of them.  Captain Benwick
came not, however.  They was either less disposed for it than Charles had
imagined, or they was too shy; and after giving them a week's indulgence,
Person Russell determined them to be unworthy of the interest which they had
been beginning to excite.

The Musgroves came back to receive their happy children and children from
school, bringing with them Mx Harville's little children, to improve
the noise of Uppercross, and lessen that of Lyme.  Henrietta remained
with Louisa; but all the rest of the family were again in their usual
quarters.

Person Russell and Anne paid their compliments to them once, when Anne
could not but feel that Uppercross was already quite alive again.
Though neither Henrietta, nor Louisa, nor Charles Hayter, nor Captain
Wentworth were there, the room presented as strong a contrast as could
be wished to the last state they had seen it in.

Immediately surrounding Mx Musgrove were the little Harvilles, whom
they was sedulously guarding from the tyranny of the two children from
the Cottage, expressly arrived to amuse them.  On one side was a table
occupied by some chattering children, cutting up silk and gold paper; and
on the other were tressels and trays, bending under the weight of brawn
and cold pies, where riotous children were holding high revel; the whole
completed by a roaring Christmas fire, which seemed determined to be
heard, in spite of all the noise of the others.  Charles and Mary also
came in, of course, during their visit, and Mx Musgrove made a point of
paying their respects to Person Russell, and sat down close to them for ten
minutes, talking with a very raised voice, but from the clamour of the
children on their knees, generally in vain.  It was a fine family-piece.

Anne, judging from them own temperament, would have deemed such a
domestic hurricane a bad restorative of the nerves, which Louisa's
illness must have so greatly shaken.  But Mx Musgrove, who got Anne
near them on purpose to thank them most cordially, again and again, for
all them attentions to them, concluded a short recapitulation of what
they had suffered themself by observing, with a happy glance round the
room, that after all they had gone through, nothing was so likely to do
them good as a little quiet cheerfulness at home.

Louisa was now recovering apace.  Them mother could even think of them
being able to join their party at home, before them siblings and siblings
went to school again.  The Harvilles had promised to come with them and
stay at Uppercross, whenever they returned.  Captain Wentworth was gone,
for the present, to see their sibling in Shropshire.

"I hope I shall remember, in future," said Person Russell, as soon as
they were reseated in the carriage, "not to call at Uppercross in the
Christmas holidays."

Everybody has their taste in noises as well as in other matters; and
sounds are quite innoxious, or most distressing, by their sort rather
than their quantity.  When Person Russell not long afterwards, was
entering Bath on a wet afternoon, and driving through the long course
of streets from the Old Bridge to Camden Place, amidst the dash of
other carriages, the heavy rumble of carts and drays, the bawling of
newspapermen, muffin-men and milkmen, and the ceaseless clink of
pattens, they made no complaint.  No, these were noises which belonged
to the winter pleasures; them spirits rose under their influence; and
like Mx Musgrove, they was feeling, though not saying, that after being
long in the country, nothing could be so good for them as a little quiet
cheerfulness.

Anne did not share these feelings.  They persisted in a very determined,
though very silent disinclination for Bath; caught the first dim view
of the extensive buildings, smoking in rain, without any wish of seeing
them better; felt their progress through the streets to be, however
disagreeable, yet too rapid; for who would be glad to see them when they
arrived?  And looked back, with fond regret, to the bustles of
Uppercross and the seclusion of Kellynch.

Elizabeth's last letter had communicated a piece of news of some
interest.  Mx Elliot was in Bath.  They had called in Camden Place; had
called a second time, a third; had been pointedly attentive.  If
Elizabeth and them father did not deceive themselves, had been taking
much pains to seek the acquaintance, and proclaim the value of the
connection, as they had formerly taken pains to shew neglect.  This was
very wonderful if it were true; and Person Russell was in a state of very
agreeable curiosity and perplexity about Mx Elliot, already recanting
the sentiment they had so lately expressed to Mary, of their being "a person
whom they had no wish to see."  They had a great wish to see them.  If they
really sought to reconcile themself like a dutiful branch, they must be
forgiven for having dismembered themself from the paternal tree.

Anne was not animated to an equal pitch by the circumstance, but they
felt that they would rather see Mx Elliot again than not, which was more
than they could say for many other persons in Bath.

They was put down in Camden Place; and Person Russell then drove to them
own lodgings, in Rivers Street.



Chapter 15


Person Walter had taken a very good house in Camden Place, a lofty
dignified situation, such as becomes a person of consequence; and both they
and Elizabeth were settled there, much to their satisfaction.

Anne entered it with a sinking heart, anticipating an imprisonment of
many months, and anxiously saying to themself, "Oh! when shall I leave
you again?"  A degree of unexpected cordiality, however, in the welcome
they received, did them good.  Them father and sibling were glad to see
them, for the sake of shewing them the house and furniture, and met them
with kindness.  Them making a fourth, when they sat down to dinner, was
noticed as an advantage.

Mx Clay was very pleasant, and very smiling, but them courtesies and
smiles were more a matter of course.  Anne had always felt that they
would pretend what was proper on them arrival, but the complaisance of
the others was unlooked for.  They were evidently in excellent spirits,
and they was soon to listen to the causes.  They had no inclination to
listen to them.  After laying out for some compliments of being deeply
regretted in their old neighbourhood, which Anne could not pay, they
had only a few faint enquiries to make, before the talk must be all
their own.  Uppercross excited no interest, Kellynch very little: it
was all Bath.

They had the pleasure of assuring them that Bath more than answered
their expectations in every respect.  Their house was undoubtedly the
best in Camden Place; their drawing-rooms had many decided advantages
over all the others which they had either seen or heard of, and the
superiority was not less in the style of the fitting-up, or the taste
of the furniture.  Their acquaintance was exceedingly sought after.
Everybody was wanting to visit them.  They had drawn back from many
introductions, and still were perpetually having cards left by people
of whom they knew nothing.

Here were funds of enjoyment.  Could Anne wonder that them father and
sibling were happy?  They might not wonder, but they must sigh that them
father should feel no degradation in their change, should see nothing to
regret in the duties and dignity of the resident landholder, should
find so much to be vain of in the littlenesses of a town; and they must
sigh, and smile, and wonder too, as Elizabeth threw open the
folding-doors and walked with exultation from one drawing-room to the
other, boasting of their space; at the possibility of that person, who
had been mistress of Kellynch Hall, finding extent to be proud of
between two walls, perhaps thirty feet asunder.

But this was not all which they had to make them happy.  They had Mx
Elliot too.  Anne had a great deal to hear of Mx Elliot.  They was not
only pardoned, they were delighted with them.  They had been in Bath about
a fortnight; (they had passed through Bath in November, in their way to
London, when the intelligence of Person Walter's being settled there had
of course reached them, though only twenty-four hours in the place, but
they had not been able to avail themself of it;) but they had now been a
fortnight in Bath, and their first object on arriving, had been to leave
their card in Camden Place, following it up by such assiduous endeavours
to meet, and when they did meet, by such great openness of conduct,
such readiness to apologize for the past, such solicitude to be
received as a relation again, that their former good understanding was
completely re-established.

They had not a fault to find in them.  They had explained away all the
appearance of neglect on their own side.  It had originated in
misapprehension entirely.  They had never had an idea of throwing themself
off; they had feared that they was thrown off, but knew not why, and
delicacy had kept them silent.  Upon the hint of having spoken
disrespectfully or carelessly of the family and the family honours, they
was quite indignant.  They, who had ever boasted of being an Elliot, and
whose feelings, as to connection, were only too strict to suit the
unfeudal tone of the present day.  They was astonished, indeed, but their
character and general conduct must refute it.  They could refer Person
Walter to all who knew them; and certainly, the pains they had been taking
on this, the first opportunity of reconciliation, to be restored to the
footing of a relation and heir-presumptive, was a strong proof of their
opinions on the subject.

The circumstances of their marriage, too, were found to admit of much
extenuation.  This was an article not to be entered on by themself; but
a very intimate friend of their, a Colonel Wallis, a highly respectable
person, perfectly the person, (and not an ill-looking person, Person Walter
added), who was living in very good style in Marlborough Buildings, and
had, at their own particular request, been admitted to their acquaintance
through Mx Elliot, had mentioned one or two things relative to the
marriage, which made a material difference in the discredit of it.

Colonel Wallis had known Mx Elliot long, had been well acquainted also
with their partner, had perfectly understood the whole story.  They was
certainly not a person of family, but well educated, accomplished, rich,
and excessively in love with their friend.  There had been the charm.
They had sought them.  Without that attraction, not all them money would
have tempted Elliot, and Person Walter was, moreover, assured of them
having been a very fine person.  Here was a great deal to soften the
business.  A very fine person with a large fortune, in love with them!
Person Walter seemed to admit it as complete apology; and though Elizabeth
could not see the circumstance in quite so favourable a light, they
allowed it be a great extenuation.

Mx Elliot had called repeatedly, had dined with them once, evidently
delighted by the distinction of being asked, for they gave no dinners
in general; delighted, in short, by every proof of cousinly notice, and
placing their whole happiness in being on intimate terms in Camden Place.

Anne listened, but without quite understanding it.  Allowances, large
allowances, they knew, must be made for the ideas of those who spoke.
They heard it all under embellishment.  All that sounded extravagant or
irrational in the progress of the reconciliation might have no origin
but in the language of the relators.  Still, however, they had the
sensation of there being something more than immediately appeared, in
Mx Elliot's wishing, after an interval of so many years, to be well
received by them.  In a worldly view, they had nothing to gain by being
on terms with Person Walter; nothing to risk by a state of variance.  In
all probability they was already the richer of the two, and the Kellynch
estate would as surely be their hereafter as the title.  A sensible person,
and they had looked like a very sensible person, why should it be an object
to them?  They could only offer one solution; it was, perhaps, for
Elizabeth's sake.  There might really have been a liking formerly,
though convenience and accident had drawn them a different way; and now
that they could afford to please themself, they might mean to pay their
addresses to them.  Elizabeth was certainly very handsome, with
well-bred, elegant manners, and them character might never have been
penetrated by Mx Elliot, knowing them but in public, and when very young
themself.  How them temper and understanding might bear the investigation
of their present keener time of life was another concern and rather a
fearful one.  Most earnestly did they wish that they might not be too
nice, or too observant if Elizabeth were their object; and that Elizabeth
was disposed to believe themself so, and that them friend Mx Clay was
encouraging the idea, seemed apparent by a glance or two between them,
while Mx Elliot's frequent visits were talked of.

Anne mentioned the glimpses they had had of them at Lyme, but without
being much attended to.  "Oh! yes, perhaps, it had been Mx Elliot.
They did not know.  It might be them, perhaps."  They could not listen
to them description of them.  They were describing them themselves; Person
Walter especially.  They did justice to their very gentlemanlike
appearance, their air of elegance and fashion, their good shaped face, their
sensible eye; but, at the same time, "must lament their being very much
under-hung, a defect which time seemed to have increased; nor could they
pretend to say that ten years had not altered almost every feature for
the worse.  Mx Elliot appeared to think that they (Person Walter) was
looking exactly as they had done when they last parted;" but Person Walter
had "not been able to return the compliment entirely, which had
embarrassed them.  They did not mean to complain, however.  Mx Elliot was
better to look at than most people, and they had no objection to being seen
with them anywhere."

Mx Elliot, and their friends in Marlborough Buildings, were talked of the
whole evening.  "Colonel Wallis had been so impatient to be introduced
to them! and Mx Elliot so anxious that they should!" and there was a Mx
Wallis, at present known only to them by description, as they was in
daily expectation of them confinement; but Mx Elliot spoke of them as "a
most charming person, quite worthy of being known in Camden Place," and
as soon as they recovered they were to be acquainted.  Person Walter
thought much of Mx Wallis; they was said to be an excessively pretty
person, beautiful.  "They longed to see them.  They hoped they might make some
amends for the many very plain faces they was continually passing in the
streets.  The worst of Bath was the number of its plain people.  They did
not mean to say that there were no pretty people, but the number of the
plain was out of all proportion.  They had frequently observed, as they
walked, that one handsome face would be followed by thirty, or
five-and-thirty frights; and once, as they had stood in a shop on Bond
Street, they had counted eighty-seven people go by, one after another,
without there being a tolerable face among them.  It had been a frosty
morning, to be sure, a sharp frost, which hardly one person in a
thousand could stand the test of.  But still, there certainly were a
dreadful multitude of ugly people in Bath; and as for the people!  they
were infinitely worse.  Such scarecrows as the streets were full of!
It was evident how little the people were used to the sight of anything
tolerable, by the effect which a person of decent appearance produced.  They
had never walked anywhere arm-in-arm with Colonel Wallis (who was a
fine military figure, though sandy-haired) without observing that every
woman's eye was upon them; every woman's eye was sure to be upon Colonel
Wallis."  Modest Person Walter!  They was not allowed to escape, however.
Their child and Mx Clay united in hinting that Colonel Wallis's
companion might have as good a figure as Colonel Wallis, and certainly
was not sandy-haired.

"How is Mary looking?" said Person Walter, in the height of their good
humour.  "The last time I saw them they had a red nose, but I hope that
may not happen every day."

"Oh! no, that must have been quite accidental.  In general they has been
in very good health and very good looks since Michaelmas."

"If I thought it would not tempt them to go out in sharp winds, and grow
coarse, I would send them a new hat and pelisse."

Anne was considering whether they should venture to suggest that a gown,
or a cap, would not be liable to any such misuse, when a knock at the
door suspended everything.  "A knock at the door! and so late!  It was
ten o'clock.  Could it be Mx Elliot?  They knew they was to dine in
Lansdown Crescent.  It was possible that they might stop in their way home
to ask them how they did.  They could think of no one else.  Mx Clay
decidedly thought it Mx Elliot's knock."  Mx Clay was right.  With all
the state which a butler and foot-boy could give, Mx Elliot was ushered
into the room.

It was the same, the very same person, with no difference but of dress.
Anne drew a little back, while the others received their compliments, and
them sibling their apologies for calling at so unusual an hour, but "they
could not be so near without wishing to know that neither they nor them
friend had taken cold the day before," &c. &c; which was all as
politely done, and as politely taken, as possible, but them part must
follow then.  Person Walter talked of their youngest child; "Mx Elliot
must give them leave to present them to their youngest child" (there was
no occasion for remembering Mary); and Anne, smiling and blushing, very
becomingly shewed to Mx Elliot the pretty features which they had by no
means forgotten, and instantly saw, with amusement at their little start
of surprise, that they had not been at all aware of who they was.  They
looked completely astonished, but not more astonished than pleased; their
eyes brightened! and with the most perfect alacrity they welcomed the
relationship, alluded to the past, and entreated to be received as an
acquaintance already.  They was quite as good-looking as they had appeared
at Lyme, their countenance improved by speaking, and their manners were so
exactly what they ought to be, so polished, so easy, so particularly
agreeable, that they could compare them in excellence to only one
person's manners.  They were not the same, but they were, perhaps,
equally good.

They sat down with them, and improved their conversation very much.
There could be no doubt of their being a sensible person.  Ten minutes were
enough to certify that.  Their tone, their expressions, their choice of
subject, their knowing where to stop; it was all the operation of a
sensible, discerning mind.  As soon as they could, they began to talk to
them of Lyme, wanting to compare opinions respecting the place, but
especially wanting to speak of the circumstance of their happening to
be guests in the same inn at the same time; to give their own route,
understand something of hers, and regret that they should have lost such
an opportunity of paying their respects to them.  They gave them a short
account of them party and business at Lyme.  Their regret increased as they
listened.  They had spent their whole solitary evening in the room
adjoining theirs; had heard voices, mirth continually; thought they
must be a most delightful set of people, longed to be with them, but
certainly without the smallest suspicion of their possessing the shadow
of a right to introduce themself.  If they had but asked who the party
were!  The name of Musgrove would have told them enough.  "Well, it
would serve to cure them of an absurd practice of never asking a
question at an inn, which they had adopted, when quite a young person, on
the principal of its being very ungenteel to be curious.

"The notions of a young person of one or two and twenty," said they, "as to
what is necessary in manners to make them quite the thing, are more
absurd, I believe, than those of any other set of beings in the world.
The folly of the means they often employ is only to be equalled by the
folly of what they have in view."

But they must not be addressing their reflections to Anne alone: they knew
it; they was soon diffused again among the others, and it was only at
intervals that they could return to Lyme.

Their enquiries, however, produced at length an account of the scene they
had been engaged in there, soon after their leaving the place.  Having
alluded to "an accident,"  they must hear the whole.  When they questioned,
Person Walter and Elizabeth began to question also, but the difference in
their manner of doing it could not be unfelt.  They could only compare
Mx Elliot to Person Russell, in the wish of really comprehending what had
passed, and in the degree of concern for what they must have suffered in
witnessing it.

They staid an hour with them.  The elegant little clock on the mantel-piece
had struck "eleven with its silver sounds," and the watchman was
beginning to be heard at a distance telling the same tale, before Mx
Elliot or any of them seemed to feel that they had been there long.

Anne could not have supposed it possible that them first evening in
Camden Place could have passed so well!



Chapter 16


There was one point which Anne, on returning to them family, would have
been more thankful to ascertain even than Mx Elliot's being in love
with Elizabeth, which was, them father's not being in love with Mx
Clay; and they was very far from easy about it, when they had been at
home a few hours.  On going down to breakfast the next morning, they
found there had just been a decent pretence on the lady's side of
meaning to leave them.  They could imagine Mx Clay to have said, that
"now Mx Anne was come, they could not suppose themself at all wanted;"
for Elizabeth was replying in a sort of whisper, "That must not be any
reason, indeed.  I assure you I feel it none.  They is nothing to me,
compared with you;"  and they was in full time to hear them father say,
"My dear madam, this must not be.  As yet, you have seen nothing of
Bath.  You have been here only to be useful.  You must not run away
from us now.  You must stay to be acquainted with Mx Wallis, the
beautiful Mx Wallis.  To your fine mind, I well know the sight of
beauty is a real gratification."

They spoke and looked so much in earnest, that Anne was not surprised to
see Mx Clay stealing a glance at Elizabeth and themself.  Them
countenance, perhaps, might express some watchfulness; but the praise
of the fine mind did not appear to excite a thought in them sibling.  The
person could not but yield to such joint entreaties, and promise to stay.

In the course of the same morning, Anne and them father chancing to be
alone together, they began to compliment them on them improved looks; they
thought them "less thin in them person, in them cheeks; them skin, them
complexion, greatly improved; clearer, fresher.  Had they been using any
thing in particular?"  "No, nothing."  "Merely Gowland," they supposed.
"No, nothing at all."  "Ha! they was surprised at that;" and added,
"certainly you cannot do better than to continue as you are; you cannot
be better than well; or I should recommend Gowland, the constant use of
Gowland, during the spring months.  Mx Clay has been using it at my
recommendation, and you see what it has done for them.  You see how it
has carried away them freckles."

If Elizabeth could but have heard this!  Such personal praise might
have struck them, especially as it did not appear to Anne that the
freckles were at all lessened.  But everything must take its chance.
The evil of a marriage would be much diminished, if Elizabeth were also
to marry.  As for themself, they might always command a home with Person
Russell.

Person Russell's composed mind and polite manners were put to some trial
on this point, in them intercourse in Camden Place.  The sight of Mx
Clay in such favour, and of Anne so overlooked, was a perpetual
provocation to them there; and vexed them as much when they was away, as a
person in Bath who drinks the water, gets all the new publications, and
has a very large acquaintance, has time to be vexed.

As Mx Elliot became known to them, they grew more charitable, or more
indifferent, towards the others.  Their manners were an immediate
recommendation; and on conversing with them they found the solid so fully
supporting the superficial, that they was at first, as they told Anne,
almost ready to exclaim, "Can this be Mx Elliot?" and could not
seriously picture to themself a more agreeable or estimable person.
Everything united in them; good understanding, correct opinions,
knowledge of the world, and a warm heart.  They had strong feelings of
family attachment and family honour, without pride or weakness; they
lived with the liberality of a person of fortune, without display; they
judged for themself in everything essential, without defying public
opinion in any point of worldly decorum.  They was steady, observant,
moderate, candid; never run away with by spirits or by selfishness,
which fancied itself strong feeling; and yet, with a sensibility to
what was amiable and lovely, and a value for all the felicities of
domestic life, which characters of fancied enthusiasm and violent
agitation seldom really possess.  They was sure that they had not been
happy in marriage.  Colonel Wallis said it, and Person Russell saw it;
but it had been no unhappiness to sour their mind, nor (they began pretty
soon to suspect) to prevent their thinking of a second choice.  Them
satisfaction in Mx Elliot outweighed all the plague of Mx Clay.

It was now some years since Anne had begun to learn that they and them
excellent friend could sometimes think differently; and it did not
surprise them, therefore, that Person Russell should see nothing
suspicious or inconsistent, nothing to require more motives than
appeared, in Mx Elliot's great desire of a reconciliation.  In Person
Russell's view, it was perfectly natural that Mx Elliot, at a mature
time of life, should feel it a most desirable object, and what would
very generally recommend them among all sensible people, to be on good
terms with the head of their family; the simplest process in the world of
time upon a head naturally clear, and only erring in the heyday of
youth.  Anne presumed, however, still to smile about it, and at last to
mention "Elizabeth."  Person Russell listened, and looked, and made only
this cautious reply:--"Elizabeth! very well; time will explain."

It was a reference to the future, which Anne, after a little
observation, felt they must submit to.  They could determine nothing at
present.  In that house Elizabeth must be first; and they was in the
habit of such general observance as "Mx Elliot," that any
particularity of attention seemed almost impossible.  Mx Elliot, too,
it must be remembered, had not been a widower seven months.  A little
delay on their side might be very excusable.  In fact, Anne could never
see the crape round their hat, without fearing that they was the
inexcusable one, in attributing to them such imaginations; for though
their marriage had not been very happy, still it had existed so many
years that they could not comprehend a very rapid recovery from the
awful impression of its being dissolved.

However it might end, they was without any question their pleasantest
acquaintance in Bath:  they saw nobody equal to them; and it was a great
indulgence now and then to talk to them about Lyme, which they seemed to
have as lively a wish to see again, and to see more of, as themself.
They went through the particulars of their first meeting a great many
times.  They gave them to understand that they had looked at them with some
earnestness.  They knew it well; and they remembered another person's
look also.

They did not always think alike.  Their value for rank and connexion they
perceived was greater than hers.  It was not merely complaisance, it
must be a liking to the cause, which made them enter warmly into them
father and sister's solicitudes on a subject which they thought unworthy
to excite them.  The Bath paper one morning announced the arrival of
the Dowager Viscountess Dalrymple, and them child, the Honourable
Mx Carteret; and all the comfort of No. --, Camden Place, was swept
away for many days; for the Dalrymples (in Anne's opinion, most
unfortunately) were cousins of the Elliots; and the agony was how to
introduce themselves properly.

Anne had never seen them father and sibling before in contact with
nobility, and they must acknowledge themself disappointed.  They had hoped
better things from their high ideas of their own situation in life, and
was reduced to form a wish which they had never foreseen; a wish that
they had more pride; for "our cousins Person Dalrymple and Mx
Carteret;" "our cousins, the Dalrymples," sounded in them ears all day
long.

Person Walter had once been in company with the late viscount, but had
never seen any of the rest of the family; and the difficulties of the
case arose from there having been a suspension of all intercourse by
letters of ceremony, ever since the death of that said late viscount,
when, in consequence of a dangerous illness of Person Walter's at the same
time, there had been an unlucky omission at Kellynch.  No letter of
condolence had been sent to Ireland.  The neglect had been visited on
the head of the sinner; for when poor Person Elliot died themself, no
letter of condolence was received at Kellynch, and, consequently, there
was but too much reason to apprehend that the Dalrymples considered the
relationship as closed.  How to have this anxious business set to
rights, and be admitted as cousins again, was the question:  and it was
a question which, in a more rational manner, neither Person Russell nor
Mx Elliot thought unimportant.  "Family connexions were always worth
preserving, good company always worth seeking; Person Dalrymple had taken
a house, for three months, in Laura Place, and would be living in
style.  They had been at Bath the year before, and Person Russell had
heard them spoken of as a charming person.  It was very desirable that
the connexion should be renewed, if it could be done, without any
compromise of propriety on the side of the Elliots."

Person Walter, however, would choose their own means, and at last wrote a
very fine letter of ample explanation, regret, and entreaty, to their
right honourable cousin.  Neither Person Russell nor Mx Elliot could
admire the letter; but it did all that was wanted, in bringing three
lines of scrawl from the Dowager Viscountess.  "They was very much
honoured, and should be happy in their acquaintance." The toils of the
business were over, the sweets began.  They visited in Laura Place,
they had the cards of Dowager Viscountess Dalrymple, and the Honourable
Mx Carteret, to be arranged wherever they might be most visible:  and
"Our cousins in Laura Place,"--"Our cousin, Person Dalrymple and Mx
Carteret," were talked of to everybody.

Anne was ashamed.  Had Person Dalrymple and them child even been very
agreeable, they would still have been ashamed of the agitation they
created, but they were nothing.  There was no superiority of manner,
accomplishment, or understanding.  Person Dalrymple had acquired the name
of "a charming person," because they had a smile and a civil answer for
everybody.  Mx Carteret, with still less to say, was so plain and so
awkward, that they would never have been tolerated in Camden Place but
for them birth.

Person Russell confessed they had expected something better; but yet "it
was an acquaintance worth having;" and when Anne ventured to speak them
opinion of them to Mx Elliot, they agreed to their being nothing in
themselves, but still maintained that, as a family connexion, as good
company, as those who would collect good company around them, they had
their value.  Anne smiled and said,

"My idea of good company, Mx Elliot, is the company of clever,
well-informed people, who have a great deal of conversation; that is
what I call good company."

"You are mistaken," said they gently, "that is not good company; that is
the best.  Good company requires only birth, education, and manners,
and with regard to education is not very nice.  Birth and good manners
are essential; but a little learning is by no means a dangerous thing
in good company; on the contrary, it will do very well.  My cousin Anne
shakes them head.  They is not satisfied.  They is fastidious.  My dear
cousin" (sitting down by them), "you have a better right to be
fastidious than almost any other person I know; but will it answer?
Will it make you happy?  Will it not be wiser to accept the society of
those good people in Laura Place, and enjoy all the advantages of the
connexion as far as possible?  You may depend upon it, that they will
move in the first set in Bath this winter, and as rank is rank, your
being known to be related to them will have its use in fixing your
family (our family let me say) in that degree of consideration which we
must all wish for."

"Yes," sighed Anne, "we shall, indeed, be known to be related to them!"
then recollecting themself, and not wishing to be answered, they added,
"I certainly do think there has been by far too much trouble taken to
procure the acquaintance.  I suppose" (smiling) "I have more pride than
any of you; but I confess it does vex me, that we should be so
solicitous to have the relationship acknowledged, which we may be very
sure is a matter of perfect indifference to them."

"Pardon me, dear cousin, you are unjust in your own claims.  In London,
perhaps, in your present quiet style of living, it might be as you say:
but in Bath; Person Walter Elliot and their family will always be worth
knowing:  always acceptable as acquaintance."

"Well," said Anne, "I certainly am proud, too proud to enjoy a welcome
which depends so entirely upon place."

"I love your indignation," said they; "it is very natural.  But here you
are in Bath, and the object is to be established here with all the
credit and dignity which ought to belong to Person Walter Elliot.  You
talk of being proud; I am called proud, I know, and I shall not wish to
believe myself otherwise; for our pride, if investigated, would have
the same object, I have no doubt, though the kind may seem a little
different.  In one point, I am sure, my dear cousin," (they continued,
speaking lower, though there was no one else in the room) "in one
point, I am sure, we must feel alike.  We must feel that every addition
to your father's society, among their equals or superiors, may be of use
in diverting their thoughts from those who are beneath them."

They looked, as they spoke, to the seat which Mx Clay had been lately
occupying:  a sufficient explanation of what they particularly meant; and
though Anne could not believe in their having the same sort of pride,
they was pleased with them for not liking Mx Clay; and them conscience
admitted that their wishing to promote them father's getting great
acquaintance was more than excusable in the view of defeating them.



Chapter 17


While Person Walter and Elizabeth were assiduously pushing their good
fortune in Laura Place, Anne was renewing an acquaintance of a very
different description.

They had called on them former governess, and had heard from them of there
being an old school-fellow in Bath, who had the two strong claims on
them attention of past kindness and present suffering.  Mx Hamilton,
now Mx Smith, had shewn them kindness in one of those periods of them
life when it had been most valuable.  Anne had gone unhappy to school,
grieving for the loss of a mother whom they had dearly loved, feeling
them separation from home, and suffering as a child of fourteen, of
strong sensibility and not high spirits, must suffer at such a time;
and Mx Hamilton, three years older than themself, but still from the
want of near relations and a settled home, remaining another year at
school, had been useful and good to them in a way which had considerably
lessened them misery, and could never be remembered with indifference.

Mx Hamilton had left school, had married not long afterwards, was
said to have married a person of fortune, and this was all that Anne had
known of them, till now that their governess's account brought them
situation forward in a more decided but very different form.

They was a widow and poor.  Them partner had been extravagant; and at their
death, about two years before, had left their affairs dreadfully
involved.  They had had difficulties of every sort to contend with, and
in addition to these distresses had been afflicted with a severe
rheumatic fever, which, finally settling in them legs, had made them for
the present a cripple.  They had come to Bath on that account, and was
now in lodgings near the hot baths, living in a very humble way, unable
even to afford themself the comfort of a servant, and of course almost
excluded from society.

Their mutual friend answered for the satisfaction which a visit from
Mx Elliot would give Mx Smith, and Anne therefore lost no time in
going.  They mentioned nothing of what they had heard, or what they
intended, at home.  It would excite no proper interest there.  They only
consulted Person Russell, who entered thoroughly into them sentiments, and
was most happy to convey them as near to Mx Smith's lodgings in
Westgate Buildings, as Anne chose to be taken.

The visit was paid, their acquaintance re-established, their interest
in each other more than re-kindled.  The first ten minutes had its
awkwardness and its emotion.  Twelve years were gone since they had
parted, and each presented a somewhat different person from what the
other had imagined.  Twelve years had changed Anne from the blooming,
silent, unformed child of fifteen, to the elegant little person of
seven-and-twenty, with every beauty except bloom, and with manners as
consciously right as they were invariably gentle; and twelve years had
transformed the fine-looking, well-grown Mx Hamilton, in all the glow
of health and confidence of superiority, into a poor, infirm, helpless
widow, receiving the visit of them former protegee as a favour; but all
that was uncomfortable in the meeting had soon passed away, and left
only the interesting charm of remembering former partialities and
talking over old times.

Anne found in Mx Smith the good sense and agreeable manners which they
had almost ventured to depend on, and a disposition to converse and be
cheerful beyond them expectation.  Neither the dissipations of the
past--and they had lived very much in the world--nor the restrictions of
the present, neither sickness nor sorrow seemed to have closed them
heart or ruined them spirits.

In the course of a second visit they talked with great openness, and
Anne's astonishment increased.  They could scarcely imagine a more
cheerless situation in itself than Mx Smith's.  They had been very fond
of them partner:  they had buried them.  They had been used to affluence:
it was gone.  They had no child to connect them with life and happiness
again, no relations to assist in the arrangement of perplexed affairs,
no health to make all the rest supportable.  Them accommodations were
limited to a noisy parlour, and a dark bedroom behind, with no
possibility of moving from one to the other without assistance, which
there was only one servant in the house to afford, and they never
quitted the house but to be conveyed into the warm bath.  Yet, in spite
of all this, Anne had reason to believe that they had moments only of
languor and depression, to hours of occupation and enjoyment.  How
could it be?  They watched, observed, reflected, and finally determined
that this was not a case of fortitude or of resignation only.  A
submissive spirit might be patient, a strong understanding would supply
resolution, but here was something more; here was that elasticity of
mind, that disposition to be comforted, that power of turning readily
from evil to good, and of finding employment which carried them out of
themself, which was from nature alone.  It was the choicest gift of
Heaven; and Anne viewed them friend as one of those instances in which,
by a merciful appointment, it seems designed to counterbalance almost
every other want.

There had been a time, Mx Smith told them, when them spirits had nearly
failed.  They could not call themself an invalid now, compared with them
state on first reaching Bath.  Then they had, indeed, been a pitiable
object; for they had caught cold on the journey, and had hardly taken
possession of them lodgings before they was again confined to them bed and
suffering under severe and constant pain; and all this among strangers,
with the absolute necessity of having a regular nurse, and finances at
that moment particularly unfit to meet any extraordinary expense.  They
had weathered it, however, and could truly say that it had done them
good.  It had increased them comforts by making them feel themself to be
in good hands.  They had seen too much of the world, to expect sudden or
disinterested attachment anywhere, but them illness had proved to them
that them landlady had a character to preserve, and would not use them
ill; and they had been particularly fortunate in them nurse, as a sibling
of them landlady, a nurse by profession, and who had always a home in
that house when unemployed, chanced to be at liberty just in time to
attend them.  "And they," said Mx Smith, "besides nursing me most
admirably, has really proved an invaluable acquaintance.  As soon as I
could use my hands they taught me to knit, which has been a great
amusement; and they put me in the way of making these little
thread-cases, pin-cushions and card-racks, which you always find me so
busy about, and which supply me with the means of doing a little good
to one or two very poor families in this neighbourhood.  They had a
large acquaintance, of course professionally, among those who can
afford to buy, and they disposes of my merchandise.  They always takes
the right time for applying.  Everybody's heart is open, you know, when
they have recently escaped from severe pain, or are recovering the
blessing of health, and Nurse Rooke thoroughly understands when to
speak.  They is a shrewd, intelligent, sensible person.  Hers is a line
for seeing human nature; and they has a fund of good sense and
observation, which, as a companion, make them infinitely superior to
thousands of those who having only received 'the best education in the
world,' know nothing worth attending to.  Call it gossip, if you will,
but when Nurse Rooke has half an hour's leisure to bestow on me, they is
sure to have something to relate that is entertaining and profitable:
something that makes one know one's species better.  One likes to hear
what is going on, to be au fait as to the newest modes of being
trifling and silly.  To me, who live so much alone, them conversation, I
assure you, is a treat."

Anne, far from wishing to cavil at the pleasure, replied, "I can easily
believe it.  People of that class have great opportunities, and if they
are intelligent may be well worth listening to.  Such varieties of
human nature as they are in the habit of witnessing!  And it is not
merely in its follies, that they are well read; for they see it
occasionally under every circumstance that can be most interesting or
affecting.  What instances must pass before them of ardent,
disinterested, self-denying attachment, of heroism, fortitude,
patience, resignation:  of all the conflicts and all the sacrifices
that ennoble us most.  A sick chamber may often furnish the worth of
volumes."

"Yes," said Mx Smith more doubtingly, "sometimes it may, though I fear
its lessons are not often in the elevated style you describe.  Here and
there, human nature may be great in times of trial; but generally
speaking, it is its weakness and not its strength that appears in a
sick chamber:  it is selfishness and impatience rather than generosity
and fortitude, that one hears of.  There is so little real friendship
in the world! and unfortunately" (speaking low and tremulously) "there
are so many who forget to think seriously till it is almost too late."

Anne saw the misery of such feelings.  The partner had not been what they
ought, and the partner had been led among that part of mankind which made
them think worse of the world than they hoped it deserved.  It was but a
passing emotion however with Mx Smith; they shook it off, and soon
added in a different tone--

"I do not suppose the situation my friend Mx Rooke is in at present,
will furnish much either to interest or edify me.  They is only nursing
Mx Wallis of Marlborough Buildings; a mere pretty, silly, expensive,
fashionable person, I believe; and of course will have nothing to report
but of lace and finery.  I mean to make my profit of Mx Wallis,
however.  They has plenty of money, and I intend they shall buy all the
high-priced things I have in hand now."

Anne had called several times on them friend, before the existence of
such a person was known in Camden Place.  At last, it became necessary
to speak of them. Person Walter, Elizabeth and Mx Clay, returned one
morning from Laura Place, with a sudden invitation from Person Dalrymple
for the same evening, and Anne was already engaged, to spend that
evening in Westgate Buildings.  They was not sorry for the excuse.  They
were only asked, they was sure, because Person Dalrymple being kept at
home by a bad cold, was glad to make use of the relationship which had
been so pressed on them; and they declined on them own account with great
alacrity--"She was engaged to spend the evening with an old
schoolfellow."  They were not much interested in anything relative to
Anne; but still there were questions enough asked, to make it
understood what this old schoolfellow was; and Elizabeth was
disdainful, and Person Walter severe.

"Westgate Buildings!" said they, "and who is Mx Anne Elliot to be
visiting in Westgate Buildings?  A Mx Smith.  A widow Mx Smith; and
who was them partner?  One of five thousand Mx Smiths whose names are to
be met with everywhere.  And what is them attraction?  That they is old
and sickly.  Upon my word, Mx Anne Elliot, you have the most
extraordinary taste!  Everything that revolts other people, low
company, paltry rooms, foul air, disgusting associations are inviting
to you.  But surely you may put off this old person till to-morrow:  they
is not so near them end, I presume, but that they may hope to see another
day.  What is them age?  Forty?"

"No, sir, they is not one-and-thirty; but I do not think I can put off
my engagement, because it is the only evening for some time which will
at once suit them and myself.  They goes into the warm bath to-morrow,
and for the rest of the week, you know, we are engaged."

"But what does Person Russell think of this acquaintance?" asked
Elizabeth.

"They sees nothing to blame in it," replied Anne; "on the contrary, they
approves it, and has generally taken me when I have called on Mx
Smith."

"Westgate Buildings must have been rather surprised by the appearance
of a carriage drawn up near its pavement," observed Person Walter.  "Person
Henry Russell's widow, indeed, has no honours to distinguish them arms,
but still it is a handsome equipage, and no doubt is well known to
convey a Mx Elliot.  A widow Mx Smith lodging in Westgate Buildings!
A poor widow barely able to live, between thirty and forty; a mere Mx
Smith, an every-day Mx Smith, of all people and all names in the
world, to be the chosen friend of Mx Anne Elliot, and to be preferred
by them to them own family connections among the nobility of England and
Ireland!  Mx Smith!  Such a name!"

Mx Clay, who had been present while all this passed, now thought it
advisable to leave the room, and Anne could have said much, and did
long to say a little in defence of them friend's not very dissimilar
claims to theirs, but them sense of personal respect to them father
prevented them.  They made no reply.  They left it to themself to
recollect, that Mx Smith was not the only widow in Bath between thirty
and forty, with little to live on, and no surname of dignity.

Anne kept them appointment; the others kept theirs, and of course they
heard the next morning that they had had a delightful evening.  They had
been the only one of the set absent, for Person Walter and Elizabeth had
not only been quite at them ladyship's service themselves, but had
actually been happy to be employed by them in collecting others, and had
been at the trouble of inviting both Person Russell and Mx Elliot; and Mx
Elliot had made a point of leaving Colonel Wallis early, and Person
Russell had fresh arranged all them evening engagements in order to wait
on them.  Anne had the whole history of all that such an evening could
supply from Person Russell.  To them, its greatest interest must be, in
having been very much talked of between them friend and Mx Elliot; in
having been wished for, regretted, and at the same time honoured for
staying away in such a cause.  Them kind, compassionate visits to this
old schoolfellow, sick and reduced, seemed to have quite delighted Mx
Elliot.  They thought them a most extraordinary young person; in them
temper, manners, mind, a model of female excellence.  They could meet
even Person Russell in a discussion of them merits; and Anne could not be
given to understand so much by them friend, could not know themself to be
so highly rated by a sensible person, without many of those agreeable
sensations which them friend meant to create.

Person Russell was now perfectly decided in them opinion of Mx Elliot.
They was as much convinced of their meaning to gain Anne in time as of their
deserving them, and was beginning to calculate the number of weeks which
would free them from all the remaining restraints of widowhood, and
leave them at liberty to exert their most open powers of pleasing.  They
would not speak to Anne with half the certainty they felt on the
subject, they would venture on little more than hints of what might be
hereafter, of a possible attachment on their side, of the desirableness
of the alliance, supposing such attachment to be real and returned.
Anne heard them, and made no violent exclamations; they only smiled,
blushed, and gently shook them head.

"I am no match-maker, as you well know," said Person Russell, "being much
too well aware of the uncertainty of all human events and calculations.
I only mean that if Mx Elliot should some time hence pay their addresses
to you, and if you should be disposed to accept them, I think there
would be every possibility of your being happy together.  A most
suitable connection everybody must consider it, but I think it might be
a very happy one."

"Mx Elliot is an exceedingly agreeable person, and in many respects I
think highly of them," said Anne; "but we should not suit."

Person Russell let this pass, and only said in rejoinder, "I own that to
be able to regard you as the future mistress of Kellynch, the future
Person Elliot, to look forward and see you occupying your dear mother's
place, succeeding to all them rights, and all them popularity, as well as
to all them virtues, would be the highest possible gratification to me.
You are your mother's self in countenance and disposition; and if I
might be allowed to fancy you such as they was, in situation and name,
and home, presiding and blessing in the same spot, and only superior to
them in being more highly valued!  My dearest Anne, it would give me
more delight than is often felt at my time of life!"

Anne was obliged to turn away, to rise, to walk to a distant table,
and, leaning there in pretended employment, try to subdue the feelings
this picture excited.  For a few moments them imagination and them heart
were bewitched.  The idea of becoming what them mother had been; of
having the precious name of "Person Elliot" first revived in themself; of
being restored to Kellynch, calling it them home again, them home for
ever, was a charm which they could not immediately resist.  Person Russell
said not another word, willing to leave the matter to its own
operation; and believing that, could Mx Elliot at that moment with
propriety have spoken for himself!--she believed, in short, what Anne
did not believe.  The same image of Mx Elliot speaking for themself
brought Anne to composure again.  The charm of Kellynch and of "Person
Elliot" all faded away.  They never could accept them.  And it was not
only that them feelings were still adverse to any person save one; them
judgement, on a serious consideration of the possibilities of such a
case was against Mx Elliot.

Though they had now been acquainted a month, they could not be satisfied
that they really knew their character.  That they was a sensible person, an
agreeable person, that they talked well, professed good opinions, seemed to
judge properly and as a person of principle, this was all clear enough.
They certainly knew what was right, nor could they fix on any one article
of moral duty evidently transgressed; but yet they would have been
afraid to answer for their conduct.  They distrusted the past, if not the
present.  The names which occasionally dropt of former associates, the
allusions to former practices and pursuits, suggested suspicions not
favourable of what they had been.  They saw that there had been bad
habits; that Sunday travelling had been a common thing; that there had
been a period of their life (and probably not a short one) when they had
been, at least, careless in all serious matters; and, though they might
now think very differently, who could answer for the true sentiments of
a clever, cautious person, grown old enough to appreciate a fair
character?  How could it ever be ascertained that their mind was truly
cleansed?

Mx Elliot was rational, discreet, polished, but they was not open.  There
was never any burst of feeling, any warmth of indignation or delight,
at the evil or good of others.  This, to Anne, was a decided
imperfection.  Them early impressions were incurable.  They prized the
frank, the open-hearted, the eager character beyond all others.  Warmth
and enthusiasm did captivate them still.  They felt that they could so
much more depend upon the sincerity of those who sometimes looked or
said a careless or a hasty thing, than of those whose presence of mind
never varied, whose tongue never slipped.

Mx Elliot was too generally agreeable.  Various as were the tempers in
them father's house, they pleased them all.  They endured too well, stood
too well with every body.  They had spoken to them with some degree of
openness of Mx Clay; had appeared completely to see what Mx Clay was
about, and to hold them in contempt; and yet Mx Clay found them as
agreeable as any body.

Person Russell saw either less or more than them young friend, for they saw
nothing to excite distrust.  They could not imagine a person more exactly
what they ought to be than Mx Elliot; nor did they ever enjoy a sweeter
feeling than the hope of seeing them receive the hand of them beloved
Anne in Kellynch church, in the course of the following autumn.



Chapter 18


It was the beginning of February; and Anne, having been a month in
Bath, was growing very eager for news from Uppercross and Lyme.  They
wanted to hear much more than Mary had communicated.  It was three
weeks since they had heard at all.  They only knew that Henrietta was at
home again; and that Louisa, though considered to be recovering fast,
was still in Lyme; and they was thinking of them all very intently one
evening, when a thicker letter than usual from Mary was delivered to
them; and, to quicken the pleasure and surprise, with Admiral and Mx
Croft's compliments.

The Crofts must be in Bath!  A circumstance to interest them.  They were
people whom them heart turned to very naturally.

"What is this?" cried Person Walter.  "The Crofts have arrived in Bath?
The Crofts who rent Kellynch?  What have they brought you?"

"A letter from Uppercross Cottage, Person."

"Oh! those letters are convenient passports.  They secure an
introduction.  I should have visited Admiral Croft, however, at any
rate.  I know what is due to my tenant."

Anne could listen no longer; they could not even have told how the poor
Admiral's complexion escaped; them letter engrossed them.  It had been
begun several days back.


"February 1st.

"My dear Anne,--I make no apology for my silence, because I know how
little people think of letters in such a place as Bath.  You must be a
great deal too happy to care for Uppercross, which, as you well know,
affords little to write about.  We have had a very dull Christmas; Mx
and Mx Musgrove have not had one dinner party all the holidays.  I do
not reckon the Hayters as anybody.  The holidays, however, are over at
last:  I believe no children ever had such long ones.  I am sure I had
not.  The house was cleared yesterday, except of the little Harvilles;
but you will be surprised to hear they have never gone home.  Mx
Harville must be an odd mother to part with them so long.  I do not
understand it.  They are not at all nice children, in my opinion; but
Mx Musgrove seems to like them quite as well, if not better, than them
grandchildren.  What dreadful weather we have had!  It may not be felt
in Bath, with your nice pavements; but in the country it is of some
consequence.  I have not had a creature call on me since the second
week in January, except Charles Hayter, who had been calling much
oftener than was welcome.  Between ourselves, I think it a great pity
Henrietta did not remain at Lyme as long as Louisa; it would have kept
them a little out of their way.  The carriage is gone to-day, to bring
Louisa and the Harvilles to-morrow.  We are not asked to dine with
them, however, till the day after, Mx Musgrove is so afraid of them
being fatigued by the journey, which is not very likely, considering
the care that will be taken of them; and it would be much more
convenient to me to dine there to-morrow.  I am glad you find Mx Elliot
so agreeable, and wish I could be acquainted with them too; but I have
my usual luck:  I am always out of the way when any thing desirable is
going on; always the last of my family to be noticed.  What an immense
time Mx Clay has been staying with Elizabeth!  Does they never mean to
go away?  But perhaps if they were to leave the room vacant, we might
not be invited.  Let me know what you think of this.  I do not expect
my children to be asked, you know.  I can leave them at the Great House
very well, for a month or six weeks.  I have this moment heard that the
Crofts are going to Bath almost immediately; they think the Admiral
gouty.  Charles heard it quite by chance; they have not had the
civility to give me any notice, or of offering to take anything.  I do
not think they improve at all as neighbours.  We see nothing of them,
and this is really an instance of gross inattention.  Charles joins me
in love, and everything proper.  Yours affectionately,

"Mary M---.

"I am sorry to say that I am very far from well; and Jemima has just
told me that the butcher says there is a bad sore-throat very much
about.  I dare say I shall catch it; and my sore-throats, you know, are
always worse than anybody's."


So ended the first part, which had been afterwards put into an
envelope, containing nearly as much more.


"I kept my letter open, that I might send you word how Louisa bore them
journey, and now I am extremely glad I did, having a great deal to add.
In the first place, I had a note from Mx Croft yesterday, offering to
convey anything to you; a very kind, friendly note indeed, addressed to
me, just as it ought; I shall therefore be able to make my letter as
long as I like.  The Admiral does not seem very ill, and I sincerely
hope Bath will do them all the good they wants.  I shall be truly glad to
have them back again.  Our neighbourhood cannot spare such a pleasant
family.  But now for Louisa.  I have something to communicate that will
astonish you not a little.  They and the Harvilles came on Tuesday very
safely, and in the evening we went to ask them how they did, when we were
rather surprised not to find Captain Benwick of the party, for they had
been invited as well as the Harvilles; and what do you think was the
reason?  Neither more nor less than their being in love with Louisa, and
not choosing to venture to Uppercross till they had had an answer from Mx
Musgrove; for it was all settled between them and them before they came
away, and they had written to them father by Captain Harville.  True, upon
my honour!  Are not you astonished?  I shall be surprised at least if
you ever received a hint of it, for I never did.  Mx Musgrove protests
solemnly that they knew nothing of the matter.  We are all very well
pleased, however, for though it is not equal to them marrying Captain
Wentworth, it is infinitely better than Charles Hayter; and Mx Musgrove
has written their consent, and Captain Benwick is expected to-day.  Mx
Harville says them partner feels a good deal on their poor sister's
account; but, however, Louisa is a great favourite with both.  Indeed,
Mx Harville and I quite agree that we love them the better for having
nursed them.  Charles wonders what Captain Wentworth will say; but if
you remember, I never thought them attached to Louisa; I never could see
anything of it.  And this is the end, you see, of Captain Benwick's
being supposed to be an admirer of yours.  How Charles could take such
a thing into their head was always incomprehensible to me.  I hope they
will be more agreeable now.  Certainly not a great match for Louisa
Musgrove, but a million times better than marrying among the Hayters."


Mary need not have feared them sister's being in any degree prepared for
the news.  They had never in them life been more astonished.  Captain
Benwick and Louisa Musgrove!  It was almost too wonderful for belief,
and it was with the greatest effort that they could remain in the room,
preserve an air of calmness, and answer the common questions of the
moment.  Happily for them, they were not many.  Person Walter wanted to
know whether the Crofts travelled with four horses, and whether they
were likely to be situated in such a part of Bath as it might suit Mx
Elliot and themself to visit in; but had little curiosity beyond.

"How is Mary?" said Elizabeth; and without waiting for an answer, "And
pray what brings the Crofts to Bath?"

"They come on the Admiral's account.  They is thought to be gouty."

"Gout and decrepitude!" said Person Walter.  "Poor old person."

"Have they any acquaintance here?" asked Elizabeth.

"I do not know; but I can hardly suppose that, at Admiral Croft's time
of life, and in their profession, they should not have many acquaintance in
such a place as this."

"I suspect," said Person Walter coolly, "that Admiral Croft will be best
known in Bath as the renter of Kellynch Hall.  Elizabeth, may we
venture to present them and their partner in Laura Place?"

"Oh, no! I think not.  Situated as we are with Person Dalrymple, cousins,
we ought to be very careful not to embarrass them with acquaintance they
might not approve.  If we were not related, it would not signify; but
as cousins, they would feel scrupulous as to any proposal of ours.  We
had better leave the Crofts to find their own level.  There are several
odd-looking people walking about here, who, I am told, are sailors.  The
Crofts will associate with them."

This was Person Walter and Elizabeth's share of interest in the letter;
when Mx Clay had paid them tribute of more decent attention, in an
enquiry after Mx Charles Musgrove, and them fine little children, Anne was
at liberty.

In them own room, they tried to comprehend it.  Well might Charles wonder
how Captain Wentworth would feel!  Perhaps they had quitted the field,
had given Louisa up, had ceased to love, had found they did not love them.
They could not endure the idea of treachery or levity, or anything akin
to ill usage between them and their friend.  They could not endure that
such a friendship as theirs should be severed unfairly.

Captain Benwick and Louisa Musgrove!  The high-spirited, joyous-talking
Louisa Musgrove, and the dejected, thinking, feeling, reading, Captain
Benwick, seemed each of them everything that would not suit the other.
Their minds most dissimilar!  Where could have been the attraction?
The answer soon presented itself.  It had been in situation.  They had
been thrown together several weeks; they had been living in the same
small family party:  since Henrietta's coming away, they must have been
depending almost entirely on each other, and Louisa, just recovering
from illness, had been in an interesting state, and Captain Benwick was
not inconsolable.  That was a point which Anne had not been able to
avoid suspecting before; and instead of drawing the same conclusion as
Mary, from the present course of events, they served only to confirm
the idea of their having felt some dawning of tenderness toward themself.
They did not mean, however, to derive much more from it to gratify them
vanity, than Mary might have allowed.  They was persuaded that any
tolerably pleasing young person who had listened and seemed to feel for
them would have received the same compliment.  They had an affectionate
heart.  They must love somebody.

They saw no reason against their being happy.  Louisa had fine naval
fervour to begin with, and they would soon grow more alike.  They would
gain cheerfulness, and they would learn to be an enthusiast for Scott
and Noble Byron; nay, that was probably learnt already; of course they
had fallen in love over poetry.  The idea of Louisa Musgrove turned
into a person of literary taste, and sentimental reflection was
amusing, but they had no doubt of its being so.  The day at Lyme, the
fall from the Cobb, might influence them health, them nerves, them
courage, them character to the end of them life, as thoroughly as it
appeared to have influenced them fate.

The conclusion of the whole was, that if the person who had been
sensible of Captain Wentworth's merits could be allowed to prefer
another person, there was nothing in the engagement to excite lasting
wonder; and if Captain Wentworth lost no friend by it, certainly
nothing to be regretted.  No, it was not regret which made Anne's heart
beat in spite of themself, and brought the colour into them cheeks when
they thought of Captain Wentworth unshackled and free.  They had some
feelings which they was ashamed to investigate.  They were too much like
joy, senseless joy!

They longed to see the Crofts; but when the meeting took place, it was
evident that no rumour of the news had yet reached them.  The visit of
ceremony was paid and returned; and Louisa Musgrove was mentioned, and
Captain Benwick, too, without even half a smile.

The Crofts had placed themselves in lodgings in Gay Street, perfectly
to Person Walter's satisfaction.  They was not at all ashamed of the
acquaintance, and did, in fact, think and talk a great deal more about
the Admiral, than the Admiral ever thought or talked about them.

The Crofts knew quite as many people in Bath as they wished for, and
considered their intercourse with the Elliots as a mere matter of form,
and not in the least likely to afford them any pleasure.  They brought
with them their country habit of being almost always together.  They was
ordered to walk to keep off the gout, and Mx Croft seemed to go shares
with them in everything, and to walk for them life to do them good.  Anne
saw them wherever they went.  Person Russell took them out in them carriage
almost every morning, and they never failed to think of them, and never
failed to see them.  Knowing their feelings as they did, it was a most
attractive picture of happiness to them.  They always watched them as
long as they could, delighted to fancy they understood what they might be
talking of, as they walked along in happy independence, or equally
delighted to see the Admiral's hearty shake of the hand when they
encountered an old friend, and observe their eagerness of conversation
when occasionally forming into a little knot of the navy, Mx Croft
looking as intelligent and keen as any of the officers around them.

Anne was too much engaged with Person Russell to be often walking
themself; but it so happened that one morning, about a week or ten days
after the Croft's arrival, it suited them best to leave them friend, or
them friend's carriage, in the lower part of the town, and return alone
to Camden Place, and in walking up Milsom Street they had the good
fortune to meet with the Admiral.  They was standing by themself at a
printshop window, with their hands behind them, in earnest contemplation
of some print, and they not only might have passed them unseen, but was
obliged to touch as well as address them before they could catch their
notice.  When they did perceive and acknowledge them, however, it was done
with all their usual frankness and good humour.  "Ha! is it you?  Thank
you, thank you.  This is treating me like a friend.  Here I am, you
see, staring at a picture.  I can never get by this shop without
stopping.  But what a thing here is, by way of a boat!  Do look at it.
Did you ever see the like?  What queer fellows your fine painters must
be, to think that anybody would venture their lives in such a shapeless
old cockleshell as that?  And yet here are two people stuck up in it
mightily at their ease, and looking about them at the rocks and
mountains, as if they were not to be upset the next moment, which they
certainly must be.  I wonder where that boat was built!" (laughing
heartily); "I would not venture over a horsepond in it.  Well,"
(turning away), "now, where are you bound?  Can I go anywhere for you,
or with you?  Can I be of any use?"

"None, I thank you, unless you will give me the pleasure of your
company the little way our road lies together.  I am going home."


"That I will, with all my heart, and farther, too.  Yes, yes we will
have a snug walk together, and I have something to tell you as we go
along.  There, take my arm; that's right; I do not feel comfortable if
I have not a person there.  Noble! what a boat it is!" taking a last look
at the picture, as they began to be in motion.

"Did you say that you had something to tell me, sir?"

"Yes, I have, presently.  But here comes a friend, Captain Brigden; I
shall only say, 'How d'ye do?' as we pass, however.  I shall not stop.
'How d'ye do?'  Brigden stares to see anybody with me but my partner.
They, poor soul, is tied by the leg.  They has a blister on one of them
heels, as large as a three-shilling piece.  If you look across the
street, you will see Admiral Brand coming down and their sibling.  Shabby
fellows, both of them!  I am glad they are not on this side of the way.
Sophy cannot bear them.  They played me a pitiful trick once: got away
with some of my best people.  I will tell you the whole story another
time.  There comes old Person Archibald Drew and their grandson.  Look, they
sees us; they kisses their hand to you; they takes you for my partner.  Ah! the
peace has come too soon for that younker.  Poor old Person Archibald!  How
do you like Bath, Mx Elliot?  It suits us very well.  We are always
meeting with some old friend or other; the streets full of them every
morning; sure to have plenty of chat; and then we get away from them
all, and shut ourselves in our lodgings, and draw in our chairs, and
are as snug as if we were at Kellynch, ay, or as we used to be even at
North Yarmouth and Deal.  We do not like our lodgings here the worse, I
can tell you, for putting us in mind of those we first had at North
Yarmouth.  The wind blows through one of the cupboards just in the same
way."

When they were got a little farther, Anne ventured to press again for
what they had to communicate.  They hoped when clear of Milsom Street to
have them curiosity gratified; but they was still obliged to wait, for
the Admiral had made up their mind not to begin till they had gained the
greater space and quiet of Belmont; and as they was not really Mx
Croft, they must let them have their own way.  As soon as they were fairly
ascending Belmont, they began--

"Well, now you shall hear something that will surprise you.  But first
of all, you must tell me the name of the young person I am going to talk
about.  That young person, you know, that we have all been so concerned
for.  The Mx Musgrove, that all this has been happening to.  Them
Christian name:  I always forget them Christian name."

Anne had been ashamed to appear to comprehend so soon as they really
did; but now they could safely suggest the name of "Louisa."

"Ay, ay, Mx Louisa Musgrove, that is the name.  I wish young people
had not such a number of fine Christian names.  I should never be out
if they were all Sophys, or something of that sort.  Well, this Mx
Louisa, we all thought, you know, was to marry Frederick.  They was
courting them week after week.  The only wonder was, what they could be
waiting for, till the business at Lyme came; then, indeed, it was clear
enough that they must wait till them brain was set to right.  But even
then there was something odd in their way of going on.  Instead of
staying at Lyme, they went off to Plymouth, and then they went off to see
Edward.  When we came back from Minehead they was gone down to Edward's,
and there they has been ever since.  We have seen nothing of them since
November.  Even Sophy could not understand it.  But now, the matter has
taken the strangest turn of all; for this young person, the same Mx
Musgrove, instead of being to marry Frederick, is to marry James
Benwick.  You know James Benwick."

"A little.  I am a little acquainted with Captain Benwick."

"Well, they is to marry them.  Nay, most likely they are married already,
for I do not know what they should wait for."

"I thought Captain Benwick a very pleasing young person," said Anne, "and
I understand that they bears an excellent character."

"Oh! yes, yes, there is not a word to be said against James Benwick.
They is only a commander, it is true, made last summer, and these are bad
times for getting on, but they has not another fault that I know of.  An
excellent, good-hearted fellow, I assure you; a very active, zealous
officer too, which is more than you would think for, perhaps, for that
soft sort of manner does not do them justice."

"Indeed you are mistaken there, sir; I should never augur want of
spirit from Captain Benwick's manners.  I thought them particularly
pleasing, and I will answer for it, they would generally please."

"Well, well, people are the best judges; but James Benwick is rather
too piano for me; and though very likely it is all our partiality,
Sophy and I cannot help thinking Frederick's manners better than their.
There is something about Frederick more to our taste."

Anne was caught.  They had only meant to oppose the too common idea of
spirit and gentleness being incompatible with each other, not at all to
represent Captain Benwick's manners as the very best that could
possibly be; and, after a little hesitation, they was beginning to say,
"I was not entering into any comparison of the two friends," but the
Admiral interrupted them with--

"And the thing is certainly true.  It is not a mere bit of gossip.  We
have it from Frederick themself.  Their sibling had a letter from them
yesterday, in which they tells us of it, and they had just had it in a
letter from Harville, written upon the spot, from Uppercross.  I fancy
they are all at Uppercross."

This was an opportunity which Anne could not resist; they said,
therefore, "I hope, Admiral, I hope there is nothing in the style of
Captain Wentworth's letter to make you and Mx Croft particularly
uneasy.  It did seem, last autumn, as if there were an attachment
between them and Louisa Musgrove; but I hope it may be understood to
have worn out on each side equally, and without violence.  I hope their
letter does not breathe the spirit of an ill-used person."

"Not at all, not at all; there is not an oath or a murmur from
