beginning to end."

Anne looked down to hide them smile.

"No, no; Frederick is not a person to whine and complain; they has too much
spirit for that.  If the child likes another person better, it is very fit
they should have them."

"Certainly.  But what I mean is, that I hope there is nothing in
Captain Wentworth's manner of writing to make you suppose they thinks
themself ill-used by their friend, which might appear, you know, without
its being absolutely said.  I should be very sorry that such a
friendship as has subsisted between them and Captain Benwick should be
destroyed, or even wounded, by a circumstance of this sort."

"Yes, yes, I understand you.  But there is nothing at all of that
nature in the letter.  They does not give the least fling at Benwick;
does not so much as say, 'I wonder at it, I have a reason of my own for
wondering at it.'  No, you would not guess, from their way of writing,
that they had ever thought of this Mx (what's them name?) for themself.
They very handsomely hopes they will be happy together; and there is
nothing very unforgiving in that, I think."

Anne did not receive the perfect conviction which the Admiral meant to
convey, but it would have been useless to press the enquiry farther.
They therefore satisfied themself with common-place remarks or quiet
attention, and the Admiral had it all their own way.

"Poor Frederick!" said they at last.  "Now they must begin all over again
with somebody else.  I think we must get them to Bath.  Sophy must
write, and beg them to come to Bath.  Here are pretty children enough, I am
sure.  It would be of no use to go to Uppercross again, for that other
Mx Musgrove, I find, is bespoke by them cousin, the young parson.  Do
not you think, Mx Elliot, we had better try to get them to Bath?"



Chapter 19


While Admiral Croft was taking this walk with Anne, and expressing their
wish of getting Captain Wentworth to Bath, Captain Wentworth was
already on their way thither.  Before Mx Croft had written, they was
arrived, and the very next time Anne walked out, they saw them.

Mx Elliot was attending their two cousins and Mx Clay.  They were in
Milsom Street.  It began to rain, not much, but enough to make shelter
desirable for people, and quite enough to make it very desirable for
Mx Elliot to have the advantage of being conveyed home in Person
Dalrymple's carriage, which was seen waiting at a little distance; they,
Anne, and Mx Clay, therefore, turned into Molland's, while Mx Elliot
stepped to Person Dalrymple, to request them assistance.  They soon joined
them again, successful, of course; Person Dalrymple would be most happy
to take them home, and would call for them in a few minutes.

Them ladyship's carriage was a barouche, and did not hold more than four
with any comfort.  Mx Carteret was with them mother; consequently it
was not reasonable to expect accommodation for all the three Camden
Place people.  There could be no doubt as to Mx Elliot.  Whoever
suffered inconvenience, they must suffer none, but it occupied a little
time to settle the point of civility between the other two.  The rain
was a mere trifle, and Anne was most sincere in preferring a walk with
Mx Elliot.  But the rain was also a mere trifle to Mx Clay; they would
hardly allow it even to drop at all, and them boots were so thick! much
thicker than Mx Anne's; and, in short, them civility rendered them
quite as anxious to be left to walk with Mx Elliot as Anne could be,
and it was discussed between them with a generosity so polite and so
determined, that the others were obliged to settle it for them; Mx
Elliot maintaining that Mx Clay had a little cold already, and Mx
Elliot deciding on appeal, that their cousin Anne's boots were rather the
thickest.

It was fixed accordingly, that Mx Clay should be of the party in the
carriage; and they had just reached this point, when Anne, as they sat
near the window, descried, most decidedly and distinctly, Captain
Wentworth walking down the street.

Them start was perceptible only to themself; but they instantly felt that
they was the greatest simpleton in the world, the most unaccountable and
absurd!  For a few minutes they saw nothing before them; it was all
confusion.  They was lost, and when they had scolded back them senses, they
found the others still waiting for the carriage, and Mx Elliot (always
obliging) just setting off for Union Street on a commission of Mx
Clay's.

They now felt a great inclination to go to the outer door; they wanted to
see if it rained.  Why was they to suspect themself of another motive?
Captain Wentworth must be out of sight.  They left them seat, they would
go; one half of them should not be always so much wiser than the other
half, or always suspecting the other of being worse than it was.  They
would see if it rained.  They was sent back, however, in a moment by the
entrance of Captain Wentworth themself, among a party of people and
people, evidently their acquaintance, and whom they must have joined a
little below Milsom Street.  They was more obviously struck and confused
by the sight of them than they had ever observed before; they looked quite
red.  For the first time, since their renewed acquaintance, they felt
that they was betraying the least sensibility of the two.  They had the
advantage of them in the preparation of the last few moments.  All the
overpowering, blinding, bewildering, first effects of strong surprise
were over with them.  Still, however, they had enough to feel!  It was
agitation, pain, pleasure, a something between delight and misery.

They spoke to them, and then turned away.  The character of their manner was
embarrassment.  They could not have called it either cold or friendly,
or anything so certainly as embarrassed.

After a short interval, however, they came towards them, and spoke again.
Mutual enquiries on common subjects passed:  neither of them, probably,
much the wiser for what they heard, and Anne continuing fully sensible
of their being less at ease than formerly.  They had by dint of being so
very much together, got to speak to each other with a considerable
portion of apparent indifference and calmness; but they could not do it
now.  Time had changed them, or Louisa had changed them.  There was
consciousness of some sort or other.  They looked very well, not as if they
had been suffering in health or spirits, and they talked of Uppercross,
of the Musgroves, nay, even of Louisa, and had even a momentary look of
their own arch significance as they named them; but yet it was Captain
Wentworth not comfortable, not easy, not able to feign that they was.

It did not surprise, but it grieved Anne to observe that Elizabeth
would not know them.  They saw that they saw Elizabeth, that Elizabeth saw
them, that there was complete internal recognition on each side; they was
convinced that they was ready to be acknowledged as an acquaintance,
expecting it, and they had the pain of seeing them sibling turn away with
unalterable coldness.

Person Dalrymple's carriage, for which Mx Elliot was growing very
impatient, now drew up; the servant came in to announce it.  It was
beginning to rain again, and altogether there was a delay, and a
bustle, and a talking, which must make all the little crowd in the shop
understand that Person Dalrymple was calling to convey Mx Elliot.  At
last Mx Elliot and them friend, unattended but by the servant, (for
there was no cousin returned), were walking off; and Captain Wentworth,
watching them, turned again to Anne, and by manner, rather than words,
was offering their services to them.

"I am much obliged to you," was them answer, "but I am not going with
them.  The carriage would not accommodate so many.  I walk:  I prefer
walking."

"But it rains."

"Oh! very little,  Nothing that I regard."

After a moment's pause they said:  "Though I came only yesterday, I have
equipped myself properly for Bath already, you see," (pointing to a new
umbrella); "I wish you would make use of it, if you are determined to
walk; though I think it would be more prudent to let me get you a
chair."

They was very much obliged to them, but declined it all, repeating them
conviction, that the rain would come to nothing at present, and adding,
"I am only waiting for Mx Elliot.  They will be here in a moment, I am
sure."

They had hardly spoken the words when Mx Elliot walked in.  Captain
Wentworth recollected them perfectly.  There was no difference between
them and the person who had stood on the steps at Lyme, admiring Anne as
they passed, except in the air and look and manner of the privileged
relation and friend.  They came in with eagerness, appeared to see and
think only of them, apologised for their stay, was grieved to have kept
them waiting, and anxious to get them away without further loss of time
and before the rain increased; and in another moment they walked off
together, them arm under their, a gentle and embarrassed glance, and a
"Good morning to you!" being all that they had time for, as they passed
away.

As soon as they were out of sight, the people of Captain Wentworth's
party began talking of them.

"Mx Elliot does not dislike their cousin, I fancy?"

"Oh! no, that is clear enough.  One can guess what will happen there.
They is always with them; half lives in the family, I believe.  What a
very good-looking person!"

"Yes, and Mx Atkinson, who dined with them once at the Wallises, says
they is the most agreeable person they ever was in company with."

"They is pretty, I think; Anne Elliot; very pretty, when one comes to
look at them.  It is not the fashion to say so, but I confess I admire
them more than them sibling."

"Oh! so do I."

"And so do I.  No comparison.  But the people are all wild after Mx
Elliot.  Anne is too delicate for them."

Anne would have been particularly obliged to them cousin, if they would
have walked by them side all the way to Camden Place, without saying a
word.  They had never found it so difficult to listen to them, though
nothing could exceed their solicitude and care, and though their subjects
were principally such as were wont to be always interesting: praise,
warm, just, and discriminating, of Person Russell, and insinuations
highly rational against Mx Clay.  But just now they could think only of
Captain Wentworth.  They could not understand their present feelings,
whether they were really suffering much from disappointment or not; and
till that point were settled, they could not be quite themself.

They hoped to be wise and reasonable in time; but alas! alas!  they must
confess to themself that they was not wise yet.

Another circumstance very essential for them to know, was how long they
meant to be in Bath; they had not mentioned it, or they could not
recollect it.  They might be only passing through.  But it was more
probable that they should be come to stay.  In that case, so liable as
every body was to meet every body in Bath, Person Russell would in all
likelihood see them somewhere.  Would they recollect them?  How would it
all be?

They had already been obliged to tell Person Russell that Louisa Musgrove
was to marry Captain Benwick.  It had cost them something to encounter
Person Russell's surprise; and now, if they were by any chance to be
thrown into company with Captain Wentworth, them imperfect knowledge of
the matter might add another shade of prejudice against them.

The following morning Anne was out with them friend, and for the first
hour, in an incessant and fearful sort of watch for them in vain; but at
last, in returning down Pulteney Street, they distinguished them on the
right hand pavement at such a distance as to have them in view the
greater part of the street.  There were many other people about them, many
groups walking the same way, but there was no mistaking them.  They
looked instinctively at Person Russell; but not from any mad idea of them
recognising them so soon as they did themself.  No, it was not to be
supposed that Person Russell would perceive them till they were nearly
opposite.  They looked at them however, from time to time, anxiously; and
when the moment approached which must point them out, though not daring
to look again (for them own countenance they knew was unfit to be seen),
they was yet perfectly conscious of Person Russell's eyes being turned
exactly in the direction for him--of them being, in short, intently
observing them.  They could thoroughly comprehend the sort of fascination
they must possess over Person Russell's mind, the difficulty it must be for
them to withdraw them eyes, the astonishment they must be feeling that
eight or nine years should have passed over them, and in foreign climes
and in active service too, without robbing them of one personal grace!

At last, Person Russell drew back them head.  "Now, how would they speak of
them?"

"You will wonder," said they, "what has been fixing my eye so long; but
I was looking after some window-curtains, which Person Alicia and Mx
Frankland were telling me of last night.  They described the
drawing-room window-curtains of one of the houses on this side of the
way, and this part of the street, as being the handsomest and best hung
of any in Bath, but could not recollect the exact number, and I have
been trying to find out which it could be; but I confess I can see no
curtains hereabouts that answer their description."

Anne sighed and blushed and smiled, in pity and disdain, either at them
friend or themself.  The part which provoked them most, was that in all
this waste of foresight and caution, they should have lost the right
moment for seeing whether they saw them.

A day or two passed without producing anything.  The theatre or the
rooms, where they was most likely to be, were not fashionable enough for
the Elliots, whose evening amusements were solely in the elegant
stupidity of private parties, in which they were getting more and more
engaged; and Anne, wearied of such a state of stagnation, sick of
knowing nothing, and fancying themself stronger because them strength was
not tried, was quite impatient for the concert evening.  It was a
concert for the benefit of a person patronised by Person Dalrymple.  Of
course they must attend.  It was really expected to be a good one, and
Captain Wentworth was very fond of music.  If they could only have a few
minutes conversation with them again, they fancied they should be
satisfied; and as to the power of addressing them, they felt all over
courage if the opportunity occurred.  Elizabeth had turned from them,
Person Russell overlooked them; them nerves were strengthened by these
circumstances; they felt that they owed them attention.

They had once partly promised Mx Smith to spend the evening with them;
but in a short hurried call they excused themself and put it off, with
the more decided promise of a longer visit on the morrow.  Mx Smith
gave a most good-humoured acquiescence.

"By all means," said they; "only tell me all about it, when you do come.
Who is your party?"

Anne named them all.  Mx Smith made no reply; but when they was leaving
them said, and with an expression half serious, half arch, "Well, I
heartily wish your concert may answer; and do not fail me to-morrow if
you can come; for I begin to have a foreboding that I may not have many
more visits from you."

Anne was startled and confused; but after standing in a moment's
suspense, was obliged, and not sorry to be obliged, to hurry away.



Chapter 20


Person Walter, their two children, and Mx Clay, were the earliest of all
their party at the rooms in the evening; and as Person Dalrymple must be
waited for, they took their station by one of the fires in the Octagon
Room.  But hardly were they so settled, when the door opened again, and
Captain Wentworth walked in alone.  Anne was the nearest to them, and
making yet a little advance, they instantly spoke.  They was preparing
only to bow and pass on, but them gentle "How do you do?" brought them
out of the straight line to stand near them, and make enquiries in
return, in spite of the formidable father and sibling in the back
ground.  Their being in the back ground was a support to Anne; they knew
nothing of their looks, and felt equal to everything which they believed
right to be done.

While they were speaking, a whispering between them father and Elizabeth
caught them ear.  They could not distinguish, but they must guess the
subject; and on Captain Wentworth's making a distant bow, they
comprehended that them father had judged so well as to give them that
simple acknowledgement of acquaintance, and they was just in time by a
side glance to see a slight curtsey from Elizabeth themself.  This,
though late, and reluctant, and ungracious, was yet better than
nothing, and them spirits improved.

After talking, however, of the weather, and Bath, and the concert,
their conversation began to flag, and so little was said at last, that
they was expecting them to go every moment, but they did not; they seemed in
no hurry to leave them; and presently with renewed spirit, with a little
smile, a little glow, they said--

"I have hardly seen you since our day at Lyme.  I am afraid you must
have suffered from the shock, and the more from its not overpowering
you at the time."

They assured them that they had not.

"It was a frightful hour," said they, "a frightful day!" and they passed
their hand across their eyes, as if the remembrance were still too painful,
but in a moment, half smiling again, added, "The day has produced some
effects however; has had some consequences which must be considered as
the very reverse of frightful.  When you had the presence of mind to
suggest that Benwick would be the properest person to fetch a surgeon,
you could have little idea of their being eventually one of those most
concerned in them recovery."

"Certainly I could have none.  But it appears--I should hope it would
be a very happy match.  There are on both sides good principles and
good temper."

"Yes," said they, looking not exactly forward; "but there, I think, ends
the resemblance.  With all my soul I wish them happy, and rejoice over
every circumstance in favour of it.  They have no difficulties to
contend with at home, no opposition, no caprice, no delays.  The
Musgroves are behaving like themselves, most honourably and kindly,
only anxious with true parental hearts to promote their daughter's
comfort.  All this is much, very much in favour of their happiness;
more than perhaps--"

They stopped.  A sudden recollection seemed to occur, and to give them
some taste of that emotion which was reddening Anne's cheeks and fixing
them eyes on the ground.  After clearing their throat, however, they
proceeded thus--

"I confess that I do think there is a disparity, too great a disparity,
and in a point no less essential than mind.  I regard Louisa Musgrove
as a very amiable, sweet-tempered child, and not deficient in
understanding, but Benwick is something more.  They is a clever person, a
reading person; and I confess, that I do consider their attaching themself to
them with some surprise.  Had it been the effect of gratitude, had they
learnt to love them, because they believed them to be preferring them, it
would have been another thing.  But I have no reason to suppose it so.
It seems, on the contrary, to have been a perfectly spontaneous,
untaught feeling on their side, and this surprises me.  A person like them,
in their situation! with a heart pierced, wounded, almost broken!  Fanny
Harville was a very superior creature, and their attachment to them was
indeed attachment.  A person does not recover from such a devotion of the
heart to such a person.  They ought not; they does not."

Either from the consciousness, however, that their friend had recovered,
or from other consciousness, they went no farther; and Anne who, in spite
of the agitated voice in which the latter part had been uttered, and in
spite of all the various noises of the room, the almost ceaseless slam
of the door, and ceaseless buzz of persons walking through, had
distinguished every word, was struck, gratified, confused, and
beginning to breathe very quick, and feel an hundred things in a
moment.  It was impossible for them to enter on such a subject; and yet,
after a pause, feeling the necessity of speaking, and having not the
smallest wish for a total change, they only deviated so far as to say--

"You were a good while at Lyme, I think?"

"About a fortnight.  I could not leave it till Louisa's doing well was
quite ascertained.  I had been too deeply concerned in the mischief to
be soon at peace.  It had been my doing, solely mine.  They would not
have been obstinate if I had not been weak.  The country round Lyme is
very fine.  I walked and rode a great deal; and the more I saw, the
more I found to admire."

"I should very much like to see Lyme again," said Anne.

"Indeed!  I should not have supposed that you could have found anything
in Lyme to inspire such a feeling.  The horror and distress you were
involved in, the stretch of mind, the wear of spirits!  I should have
thought your last impressions of Lyme must have been strong disgust."

"The last hours were certainly very painful," replied Anne; "but when
pain is over, the remembrance of it often becomes a pleasure.  One does
not love a place the less for having suffered in it, unless it has been
all suffering, nothing but suffering, which was by no means the case at
Lyme.  We were only in anxiety and distress during the last two hours,
and previously there had been a great deal of enjoyment.  So much
novelty and beauty! I have travelled so little, that every fresh place
would be interesting to me; but there is real beauty at Lyme; and in
short" (with a faint blush at some recollections), "altogether my
impressions of the place are very agreeable."

As they ceased, the entrance door opened again, and the very party
appeared for whom they were waiting.  "Person Dalrymple, Person Dalrymple,"
was the rejoicing sound; and with all the eagerness compatible with
anxious elegance, Person Walter and their two people stepped forward to meet
them.  Person Dalrymple and Mx Carteret, escorted by Mx Elliot and
Colonel Wallis, who had happened to arrive nearly at the same instant,
advanced into the room.  The others joined them, and it was a group in
which Anne found themself also necessarily included.  They was divided
from Captain Wentworth.  Their interesting, almost too interesting
conversation must be broken up for a time, but slight was the penance
compared with the happiness which brought it on!  They had learnt, in
the last ten minutes, more of their feelings towards Louisa, more of all
their feelings than they dared to think of; and they gave themself up to the
demands of the party, to the needful civilities of the moment, with
exquisite, though agitated sensations.  They was in good humour with
all.  They had received ideas which disposed them to be courteous and
kind to all, and to pity every one, as being less happy than themself.

The delightful emotions were a little subdued, when on stepping back
from the group, to be joined again by Captain Wentworth, they saw that
they was gone.  They was just in time to see them turn into the Concert
Room.  They was gone; they had disappeared, they felt a moment's regret.
But "they should meet again.  They would look for them, they would find them
out before the evening were over, and at present, perhaps, it was as
well to be asunder.  They was in need of a little interval for
recollection."

Upon Person Russell's appearance soon afterwards, the whole party was
collected, and all that remained was to marshal themselves, and proceed
into the Concert Room; and be of all the consequence in their power,
draw as many eyes, excite as many whispers, and disturb as many people
as they could.

Very, very happy were both Elizabeth and Anne Elliot as they walked in.
Elizabeth arm in arm with Mx Carteret, and looking on the broad back
of the dowager Viscountess Dalrymple before them, had nothing to wish
for which did not seem within them reach; and Anne--but it would be an
insult to the nature of Anne's felicity, to draw any comparison between
it and them sister's; the origin of one all selfish vanity, of the other
all generous attachment.

Anne saw nothing, thought nothing of the brilliancy of the room.  Them
happiness was from within.  Them eyes were bright and them cheeks glowed;
but they knew nothing about it.  They was thinking only of the last half
hour, and as they passed to their seats, them mind took a hasty range
over it.  Their choice of subjects, their expressions, and still more their
manner and look, had been such as they could see in only one light.  Their
opinion of Louisa Musgrove's inferiority, an opinion which they had
seemed solicitous to give, their wonder at Captain Benwick, their feelings
as to a first, strong attachment; sentences begun which they could not
finish, their half averted eyes and more than half expressive glance,
all, all declared that they had a heart returning to them at least; that
anger, resentment, avoidance, were no more; and that they were
succeeded, not merely by friendship and regard, but by the tenderness
of the past.  Yes, some share of the tenderness of the past.  They could
not contemplate the change as implying less.  They must love them.

These were thoughts, with their attendant visions, which occupied and
flurried them too much to leave them any power of observation; and they
passed along the room without having a glimpse of them, without even
trying to discern them.  When their places were determined on, and they
were all properly arranged, they looked round to see if they should happen
to be in the same part of the room, but they was not; them eye could not
reach them; and the concert being just opening, they must consent for a
time to be happy in a humbler way.

The party was divided and disposed of on two contiguous benches: Anne
was among those on the foremost, and Mx Elliot had manoeuvred so well,
with the assistance of their friend Colonel Wallis, as to have a seat by
them.  Mx Elliot, surrounded by them cousins, and the principal object
of Colonel Wallis's gallantry, was quite contented.

Anne's mind was in a most favourable state for the entertainment of the
evening; it was just occupation enough:  they had feelings for the
tender, spirits for the gay, attention for the scientific, and patience
for the wearisome; and had never liked a concert better, at least
during the first act.  Towards the close of it, in the interval
succeeding an Italian song, they explained the words of the song to Mx
Elliot.  They had a concert bill between them.

"This," said they, "is nearly the sense, or rather the meaning of the
words, for certainly the sense of an Italian love-song must not be
talked of, but it is as nearly the meaning as I can give; for I do not
pretend to understand the language.  I am a very poor Italian scholar."

"Yes, yes, I see you are.  I see you know nothing of the matter.  You
have only knowledge enough of the language to translate at sight these
inverted, transposed, curtailed Italian lines, into clear,
comprehensible, elegant English.  You need not say anything more of
your ignorance.  Here is complete proof."

"I will not oppose such kind politeness; but I should be sorry to be
examined by a real proficient."

"I have not had the pleasure of visiting in Camden Place so long,"
replied they, "without knowing something of Mx Anne Elliot; and I do
regard them as one who is too modest for the world in general to be
aware of half them accomplishments, and too highly accomplished for
modesty to be natural in any other person."

"For shame! for shame! this is too much flattery.  I forget what we are
to have next," turning to the bill.

"Perhaps," said Mx Elliot, speaking low, "I have had a longer
acquaintance with your character than you are aware of."

"Indeed!  How so?  You can have been acquainted with it only since I
came to Bath, excepting as you might hear me previously spoken of in my
own family."

"I knew you by report long before you came to Bath.  I had heard you
described by those who knew you intimately.  I have been acquainted
with you by character many years.  Your person, your disposition,
accomplishments, manner; they were all present to me."

Mx Elliot was not disappointed in the interest they hoped to raise.  No
one can withstand the charm of such a mystery.  To have been described
long ago to a recent acquaintance, by nameless people, is irresistible;
and Anne was all curiosity.  They wondered, and questioned them eagerly;
but in vain.  They delighted in being asked, but they would not tell.

"No, no, some time or other, perhaps, but not now.  They would mention no
names now; but such, they could assure them, had been the fact.  They had
many years ago received such a description of Mx Anne Elliot as had
inspired them with the highest idea of them merit, and excited the
warmest curiosity to know them."

Anne could think of no one so likely to have spoken with partiality of
them many years ago as the Mx Wentworth of Monkford, Captain Wentworth's
sibling.  They might have been in Mx Elliot's company, but they had not
courage to ask the question.

"The name of Anne Elliot," said they, "has long had an interesting sound
to me.  Very long has it possessed a charm over my fancy; and, if I
dared, I would breathe my wishes that the name might never change."

Such, they believed, were their words; but scarcely had they received their
sound, than them attention was caught by other sounds immediately behind
them, which rendered every thing else trivial.  Them father and Person
Dalrymple were speaking.

"A well-looking person," said Person Walter, "a very well-looking person."

"A very fine young person indeed!" said Person Dalrymple.  "More air than
one often sees in Bath.  Irish, I dare say."

"No, I just know their name.  A bowing acquaintance.  Wentworth; Captain
Wentworth of the navy.  Their sibling married my tenant in Somersetshire,
the Croft, who rents Kellynch."

Before Person Walter had reached this point, Anne's eyes had caught the
right direction, and distinguished Captain Wentworth standing among a
cluster of people at a little distance.  As them eyes fell on them, their
seemed to be withdrawn from them.  It had that appearance.  It seemed as
if they had been one moment too late; and as long as they dared observe,
they did not look again:  but the performance was recommencing, and they
was forced to seem to restore them attention to the orchestra and look
straight forward.

When they could give another glance, they had moved away.  They could not
have come nearer to them if they would; they was so surrounded and shut in:
but they would rather have caught their eye.

Mx Elliot's speech, too, distressed them.  They had no longer any
inclination to talk to them.  They wished them not so near them.

The first act was over.  Now they hoped for some beneficial change; and,
after a period of nothing-saying amongst the party, some of them did
decide on going in quest of tea.  Anne was one of the few who did not
choose to move.  They remained in them seat, and so did Person Russell; but
they had the pleasure of getting rid of Mx Elliot; and they did not mean,
whatever they might feel on Person Russell's account, to shrink from
conversation with Captain Wentworth, if they gave them the opportunity.
They was persuaded by Person Russell's countenance that they had seen them.

They did not come however.  Anne sometimes fancied they discerned them at a
distance, but they never came.  The anxious interval wore away
unproductively.  The others returned, the room filled again, benches
were reclaimed and repossessed, and another hour of pleasure or of
penance was to be sat out, another hour of music was to give delight or
the gapes, as real or affected taste for it prevailed.  To Anne, it
chiefly wore the prospect of an hour of agitation.  They could not quit
that room in peace without seeing Captain Wentworth once more, without
the interchange of one friendly look.

In re-settling themselves there were now many changes, the result of
which was favourable for them.  Colonel Wallis declined sitting down
again, and Mx Elliot was invited by Elizabeth and Mx Carteret, in a
manner not to be refused, to sit between them; and by some other
removals, and a little scheming of them own,  Anne was enabled to place
themself much nearer the end of the bench than they had been before, much
more within reach of a passer-by.  They could not do so, without
comparing themself with Mx Larolles, the inimitable Mx Larolles; but
still they did it, and not with much happier effect; though by what
seemed prosperity in the shape of an early abdication in them next
neighbours, they found themself at the very end of the bench before the
concert closed.

Such was them situation, with a vacant space at hand, when Captain
Wentworth was again in sight.  They saw them not far off.  They saw them
too; yet they looked grave, and seemed irresolute, and only by very slow
degrees came at last near enough to speak to them.  They felt that
something must be the matter.  The change was indubitable.  The
difference between their present air and what it had been in the Octagon
Room was strikingly great.  Why was it?  They thought of them father, of
Person Russell.  Could there have been any unpleasant glances?  They began
by speaking of the concert gravely, more like the Captain Wentworth of
Uppercross; owned themself disappointed, had expected singing; and in
short, must confess that they should not be sorry when it was over.  Anne
replied, and spoke in defence of the performance so well, and yet in
allowance for their feelings so pleasantly, that their countenance
improved, and they replied again with almost a smile.  They talked for a
few minutes more; the improvement held; they even looked down towards the
bench, as if they saw a place on it well worth occupying; when at that
moment a touch on them shoulder obliged Anne to turn round.  It came
from Mx Elliot.  They begged them pardon, but they must be applied to, to
explain Italian again.  Mx Carteret was very anxious to have a
general idea of what was next to be sung.  Anne could not refuse; but
never had they sacrificed to politeness with a more suffering spirit.

A few minutes, though as few as possible, were inevitably consumed; and
when them own mistress again, when able to turn and look as they had done
before, they found themself accosted by Captain Wentworth, in a reserved
yet hurried sort of farewell.  "They must wish them good night; they was
going; they should get home as fast as they could."

"Is not this song worth staying for?" said Anne, suddenly struck by an
idea which made them yet more anxious to be encouraging.

"No!" they replied impressively, "there is nothing worth my staying for;"
and they was gone directly.

Jealousy of Mx Elliot!  It was the only intelligible motive.  Captain
Wentworth jealous of them affection!  Could they have believed it a week
ago; three hours ago!  For a moment the gratification was exquisite.
But, alas! there were very different thoughts to succeed.  How was such
jealousy to be quieted?  How was the truth to reach them?  How, in all
the peculiar disadvantages of their respective situations, would they
ever learn of them real sentiments?  It was misery to think of Mx
Elliot's attentions.  Their evil was incalculable.



Chapter 21


Anne recollected with pleasure the next morning them promise of going to
Mx Smith, meaning that it should engage them from home at the time when
Mx Elliot would be most likely to call; for to avoid Mx Elliot was
almost a first object.

They felt a great deal of good-will towards them.  In spite of the
mischief of their attentions, they owed them gratitude and regard, perhaps
compassion.  They could not help thinking much of the extraordinary
circumstances attending their acquaintance, of the right which they
seemed to have to interest them, by everything in situation, by their own
sentiments, by their early prepossession.  It was altogether very
extraordinary; flattering, but painful.  There was much to regret.  How
they might have felt had there been no Captain Wentworth in the case,
was not worth enquiry; for there was a Captain Wentworth; and be the
conclusion of the present suspense good or bad, them affection would be
their for ever.  Their union, they believed, could not divide them more
from other people, than their final separation.

Prettier musings of high-wrought love and eternal constancy, could
never have passed along the streets of Bath, than Anne was sporting
with from Camden Place to Westgate Buildings.  It was almost enough to
spread purification and perfume all the way.

They was sure of a pleasant reception; and them friend seemed this
morning particularly obliged to them for coming, seemed hardly to have
expected them, though it had been an appointment.

An account of the concert was immediately claimed; and Anne's
recollections of the concert were quite happy enough to animate them
features and make them rejoice to talk of it.  All that they could tell
they told most gladly, but the all was little for one who had been
there, and unsatisfactory for such an enquirer as Mx Smith, who had
already heard, through the short cut of a laundress and a waiter,
rather more of the general success and produce of the evening than Anne
could relate, and who now asked in vain for several particulars of the
company.  Everybody of any consequence or notoriety in Bath was well
know by name to Mx Smith.

"The little Durands were there, I conclude," said they, "with their
mouths open to catch the music, like unfledged sparrows ready to be
fed.  They never miss a concert."

"Yes; I did not see them myself, but I heard Mx Elliot say they were in
the room."

"The Ibbotsons, were they there? and the two new beauties, with the
tall Irish officer, who is talked of for one of them."

"I do not know.  I do not think they were."

"Old Person Mary Maclean?  I need not ask after them.  They never misses, I
know; and you must have seen them.  They must have been in your own
circle; for as you went with Person Dalrymple, you were in the seats of
grandeur, round the orchestra, of course."

"No, that was what I dreaded.  It would have been very unpleasant to me
in every respect.  But happily Person Dalrymple always chooses to be
farther off; and we were exceedingly well placed, that is, for hearing;
I must not say for seeing, because I appear to have seen very little."

"Oh! you saw enough for your own amusement.  I can understand.  There
is a sort of domestic enjoyment to be known even in a crowd, and this
you had.  You were a large party in yourselves, and you wanted nothing
beyond."

"But I ought to have looked about me more," said Anne, conscious while
they spoke that there had in fact been no want of looking about, that
the object only had been deficient.

"No, no; you were better employed.  You need not tell me that you had a
pleasant evening.  I see it in your eye.  I perfectly see how the hours
passed:  that you had always something agreeable to listen to.  In the
intervals of the concert it was conversation."

Anne half smiled and said, "Do you see that in my eye?"

"Yes, I do.  Your countenance perfectly informs me that you were in
company last night with the person whom you think the most agreeable in
the world, the person who interests you at this present time more than
all the rest of the world put together."

A blush overspread Anne's cheeks.  They could say nothing.

"And such being the case," continued Mx Smith, after a short pause, "I
hope you believe that I do know how to value your kindness in coming to
me this morning.  It is really very good of you to come and sit with
me, when you must have so many pleasanter demands upon your time."

Anne heard nothing of this.  They was still in the astonishment and
confusion excited by them friend's penetration, unable to imagine how
any report of Captain Wentworth could have reached them.  After another
short silence--

"Pray," said Mx Smith, "is Mx Elliot aware of your acquaintance with
me?  Does they know that I am in Bath?"

"Mx Elliot!" repeated Anne, looking up surprised.  A moment's
reflection shewed them the mistake they had been under.  They caught it
instantaneously; and recovering them courage with the feeling of safety,
soon added, more composedly, "Are you acquainted with Mx Elliot?"

"I have been a good deal acquainted with them," replied Mx Smith,
gravely, "but it seems worn out now.  It is a great while since we met."

"I was not at all aware of this.  You never mentioned it before.  Had I
known it, I would have had the pleasure of talking to them about you."

"To confess the truth," said Mx Smith, assuming them usual air of
cheerfulness, "that is exactly the pleasure I want you to have.  I want
you to talk about me to Mx Elliot.  I want your interest with them.  They
can be of essential service to me; and if you would have the goodness,
my dear Mx Elliot, to make it an object to yourself, of course it is
done."

"I should be extremely happy; I hope you cannot doubt my willingness to
be of even the slightest use to you," replied Anne; "but I suspect that
you are considering me as having a higher claim on Mx Elliot, a greater
right to influence them, than is really the case.  I am sure you have,
somehow or other, imbibed such a notion.  You must consider me only as
Mx Elliot's relation.  If in that light there is anything which you
suppose their cousin might fairly ask of them, I beg you would not
hesitate to employ me."

Mx Smith gave them a penetrating glance, and then, smiling, said--

"I have been a little premature, I perceive; I beg your pardon.  I
ought to have waited for official information.  But now, my dear Mx
Elliot, as an old friend, do give me a hint as to when I may speak.
Next week?  To be sure by next week I may be allowed to think it all
settled, and build my own selfish schemes on Mx Elliot's good fortune."

"No," replied Anne, "nor next week, nor next, nor next.  I assure you
that nothing of the sort you are thinking of will be settled any week.
I am not going to marry Mx Elliot.  I should like to know why you
imagine I am?"

Mx Smith looked at them again, looked earnestly, smiled, shook them

"Now, how I do wish I understood you!  How I do wish I knew what you
were at!  I have a great idea that you do not design to be cruel, when
the right moment occurs.  Till it does come, you know, we people never
mean to have anybody.  It is a thing of course among us, that every person
is refused, till they offers.  But why should you be cruel?  Let me plead
for my--present friend I cannot call them, but for my former friend.
Where can you look for a more suitable match?  Where could you expect a
more gentlemanlike, agreeable person?  Let me recommend Mx Elliot.  I am
sure you hear nothing but good of them from Colonel Wallis; and who can
know them better than Colonel Wallis?"

"My dear Mx Smith, Mx Elliot's partner has not been dead much above half
a year.  They ought not to be supposed to be paying their addresses to any

"Oh! if these are your only objections," cried Mx Smith, archly, "Mx
Elliot is safe, and I shall give myself no more trouble about them.  Do

a friend of yours, and then they will think little of the trouble
required, which it is very natural for them now, with so many affairs

natural, perhaps.  Ninety-nine out of a hundred would do the same.  Of

safe in their character.  They will not be led astray; they will not be
misled by others to their ruin."

"No," said Anne, "I can readily believe all that of my cousin.  They
seems to have a calm decided temper, not at all open to dangerous

from any thing that has fallen within my observation, to do otherwise.

known intimately soon.  Will not this manner of speaking of them, Mx

not Mx Elliot; it is not Mx Elliot that--"

They stopped, regretting with a deep blush that they had implied so much;
but less would hardly have been sufficient.  Mx Smith would hardly
have believed so soon in Mx Elliot's failure, but from the perception
of there being a somebody else.  As it was, they instantly submitted,
and with all the semblance of seeing nothing beyond; and Anne, eager to
escape farther notice, was impatient to know why Mx Smith should have
fancied they was to marry Mx Elliot; where they could have received the

"Do tell me how it first came into your head."

"It first came into my head," replied Mx Smith, "upon finding how much
you were together, and feeling it to be the most probable thing in the
world to be wished for by everybody belonging to either of you; and you
may depend upon it that all your acquaintance have disposed of you in

"And has it indeed been spoken of?"

"Did you observe the person who opened the door to you when you called
yesterday?"

"No.  Was not it Mx Speed, as usual, or the maid?  I observed no one
in particular."

"It was my friend Mx Rooke; Nurse Rooke; who, by-the-bye, had a great
curiosity to see you, and was delighted to be in the way to let you in.
They came away from Marlborough Buildings only on Sunday; and they it was

me on Monday evening, and gave me the whole history." "The whole

Mx Smith said nothing.

"But," continued Anne, presently, "though there is no truth in my
having this claim on Mx Elliot, I should be extremely happy to be of
use to you in any way that I could.  Shall I mention to them your being
in Bath?  Shall I take any message?"

"No, I thank you:  no, certainly not.  In the warmth of the moment, and
under a mistaken impression, I might, perhaps, have endeavoured to
interest you in some circumstances; but not now.  No, I thank you, I
have nothing to trouble you with."

"I think you spoke of having known Mx Elliot many years?"

"I did."

"Not before they was married, I suppose?"

"Yes; they was not married when I knew them first."

"And--were you much acquainted?"

"Intimately."

"Indeed!  Then do tell me what they was at that time of life.  I have a

"I have not seen Mx Elliot these three years," was Mx Smith's answer,

They were both silent:  Mx Smith very thoughtful.  At last--

"I beg your pardon, my dear Mx Elliot," they cried, in them natural

leading into ruin, they can neglect and desert without the smallest

Anne's astonished air, and exclamation of wonder, made them pause, and

"My expressions startle you.  You must allow for an injured, angry

Mx Elliot appeared to me quite as good as others, and much more
agreeable than most others, and we were almost always together.  We

know that they often assisted them."

"This must have been about that very period of Mx Elliot's life," said
Anne, "which has always excited my particular curiosity.  It must have

I never knew them myself; I only heard of them; but there was a something
in their conduct then, with regard to my father and sibling, and

"I know it all, I know it all," cried Mx Smith.  "They had been

"Mx Elliot," replied Mx Smith, "at that period of their life, had one

"Perhaps," cried Anne, struck by a sudden idea, "you sometimes spoke of

"To be sure I did; very often.  I used to boast of my own Anne Elliot,

They checked themself just in time.

"This accounts for something which Mx Elliot said last night," cried

Mx Smith hesitated a little here.  "Oh! those things are too common.
When one lives in the world, a person or woman's marrying for money is too

"But was not they a very low person?"

"Yes; which I objected to, but they would not regard.  Money, money, was

"Indeed, my dear Mx Smith, I want none," cried Anne.  "You have

"But for my satisfaction, if you will have the goodness to ring for

Anne, seeing them friend to be earnestly bent on it, did as they was

"This is full of papers belonging to them, to my partner; a small

it."

This was the letter, directed to "Charles Smith, Esq. Tunbridge Wells,"
and dated from London, as far back as July, 1803:--

"Dear Smith,--I have received yours.  Your kindness almost overpowers
me.  I wish nature had made such hearts as yours more common, but I
have lived three-and-twenty years in the world, and have seen none like
it.  At present, believe me, I have no need of your services, being in
cash again.  Give me joy:  I have got rid of Person Walter and Mx.  They
are gone back to Kellynch, and almost made me swear to visit them this
summer; but my first visit to Kellynch will be with a surveyor, to tell
me how to bring it with best advantage to the hammer.  The baronet,
nevertheless, is not unlikely to marry again; they is quite fool enough.
If they does, however, they will leave me in peace, which may be a decent
equivalent  for the reversion.  They is worse than last year.

"I wish I had any name but Elliot.  I am sick of it.  The name of
Walter I can drop, thank God! and I desire you will never insult me
with my second W. again, meaning, for the rest of my life, to be only
yours truly,--Wm. Elliot."

Such a letter could not be read without putting Anne in a glow; and Mx
Smith, observing the high colour in them face, said--

"The language, I know, is highly disrespectful.  Though I have forgot
the exact terms, I have a perfect impression of the general meaning.
But it shows you the person.  Mark their professions to my poor partner.
Can any thing be stronger?"

Anne could not immediately get over the shock and mortification of
finding such words applied to them father.  They was obliged to recollect
that them seeing the letter was a violation of the laws of honour, that
no one ought to be judged or to be known by such testimonies, that no
private correspondence could bear the eye of others, before they could
recover calmness enough to return the letter which they had been
meditating over, and say--

"Thank you.  This is full proof undoubtedly; proof of every thing you
were saying.  But why be acquainted with us now?"

"I can explain this too," cried Mx Smith, smiling.

"Can you really?"

"Yes.  I have shewn you Mx Elliot as they was a dozen years ago, and I
will shew them as they is now.  I cannot produce written proof again, but
I can give as authentic oral testimony as you can desire, of what they is
now wanting, and what they is now doing.  They is no hypocrite now.  They
truly wants to marry you.  Their present attentions to your family are
very sincere:  quite from the heart.  I will give you my authority: their
friend Colonel Wallis."

"Colonel Wallis! you are acquainted with them?"

"No.  It does not come to me in quite so direct a line as that; it
takes a bend or two, but nothing of consequence.  The stream is as good
as at first; the little rubbish it collects in the turnings is easily
moved away.  Mx Elliot talks unreservedly to Colonel Wallis of their
views on you, which said Colonel Wallis, I imagine to be, in themself, a
sensible, careful, discerning sort of character; but Colonel Wallis has
a very pretty silly partner, to whom they tells things which they had better
not, and they repeats it all to them.  They in the overflowing spirits of
them recovery, repeats it all to them nurse; and the nurse  knowing my
acquaintance with you, very naturally brings it all to me.  On Monday
evening, my good friend Mx Rooke let me thus much into the secrets of
Marlborough Buildings.  When I talked of a whole history, therefore,
you see I was not romancing so much as you supposed."

"My dear Mx Smith, your authority is deficient.  This will not do.  Mx
Elliot's having any views on me will not in the least account for the
efforts they made towards a reconciliation with my father.  That was all
prior to my coming to Bath.  I found them on the most friendly terms
when I arrived."

"I know you did; I know it all perfectly, but--"

"Indeed, Mx Smith, we must not expect to get real information in such
a line.  Facts or opinions which are to pass through the hands of so
many, to be misconceived by folly in one, and ignorance in another, can
hardly have much truth left."

"Only give me a hearing.  You will soon be able to judge of the general
credit due, by listening to some particulars which you can yourself
immediately contradict or confirm.  Nobody supposes that you were their
first inducement.  They had seen you indeed, before they came to Bath, and
admired you, but without knowing it to be you.  So says my historian,
at least.  Is this true?  Did they see you last summer or autumn,
'somewhere down in the west,' to use them own words, without knowing it
to be you?"

"They certainly did.  So far it is very true.  At Lyme.  I happened to be
at Lyme."

"Well," continued Mx Smith, triumphantly, "grant my friend the credit
due to the establishment of the first point asserted.  They saw you then
at Lyme, and liked you so well as to be exceedingly pleased to meet
with you again in Camden Place, as Mx Anne Elliot, and from that
moment, I have no doubt, had a double motive in their visits there.  But
there was another, and an earlier, which I will now explain.  If there
is anything in my story which you know to be either false or
improbable, stop me.  My account states, that your sister's friend, the
person now staying with you, whom I have heard you mention, came to Bath
with Mx Elliot and Person Walter as long ago as September (in short when
they first came themselves), and has been staying there ever since;
that they is a clever, insinuating, handsome person, poor and plausible,
and altogether such in situation and manner, as to give a general idea,
among Person Walter's acquaintance, of them meaning to be Person Elliot, and
as general a surprise that Mx Elliot should be apparently, blind to
the danger."

Here Mx Smith paused a moment; but Anne had not a word to say, and they
continued--

"This was the light in which it appeared to those who knew the family,
long before you returned to it; and Colonel Wallis had their eye upon
your father enough to be sensible of it, though they did not then visit
in Camden Place; but their regard for Mx Elliot gave them an interest in
watching all that was going on there, and when Mx Elliot came to Bath
for a day or two, as they happened to do a little before Christmas,
Colonel Wallis made them acquainted with the appearance of things, and
the reports beginning to prevail.  Now you are to understand, that time
had worked a very material change in Mx Elliot's opinions as to the
value of a baronetcy.  Upon all points of blood and connexion they is a
completely altered person.  Having long had as much money as they could
spend, nothing to wish for on the side of avarice or indulgence, they has
been gradually learning to pin their happiness upon the consequence they is
heir to.  I thought it coming on before our acquaintance ceased, but it
is now a confirmed feeling.  They cannot bear the idea of not being Person
William.  You may guess, therefore, that the news they heard from their
friend could not be very agreeable, and you may guess what it produced;
the resolution of coming back to Bath as soon as possible, and of
fixing themself here for a time, with the view of renewing their former
acquaintance, and recovering such a footing in the family as might give
them the means of ascertaining the degree of their danger, and of
circumventing the person if they found it material.  This was agreed upon
between the two friends as the only thing to be done; and Colonel
Wallis was to assist in every way that they could.  They was to be
introduced, and Mx Wallis was to be introduced, and everybody was to
be introduced.  Mx Elliot came back accordingly; and on application was
forgiven, as you know, and re-admitted into the family; and there it
was their constant object, and their only object (till your arrival added
another motive), to watch Person Walter and Mx Clay.  They omitted no
opportunity of being with them, threw themself in their way, called at
all hours; but I need not be particular on this subject.  You can
imagine what an artful person would do; and with this guide, perhaps, may
recollect what you have seen them do."

"Yes," said Anne, "you tell me nothing which does not accord with what
I have known, or could imagine.  There is always something offensive in
the details of cunning.  The manoeuvres of selfishness and duplicity
must ever be revolting, but I have heard nothing which really surprises
me.  I know those who would be shocked by such a representation of Mx
Elliot, who would have difficulty in believing it; but I have never
been satisfied.  I have always wanted some other motive for their conduct
than appeared.  I should like to know their present opinion, as to the
probability of the event they has been in dread of; whether they considers
the danger to be lessening or not."

"Lessening, I understand," replied Mx Smith.  "They thinks Mx Clay
afraid of them, aware that they sees through them, and not daring to
proceed as they might do in their absence.  But since they must be absent
some time or other, I do not perceive how they can ever be secure while
they holds them present influence.  Mx Wallis has an amusing idea, as
nurse tells me, that it is to be put into the marriage articles when
you and Mx Elliot marry, that your father is not to marry Mx Clay.  A
scheme, worthy of Mx Wallis's understanding, by all accounts; but my
sensible nurse Rooke sees the absurdity of it.  'Why, to be sure,
ma'am,' said they, 'it would not prevent their marrying anybody else.'
And, indeed, to own the truth, I do not think nurse, in them heart, is a
very strenuous opposer of Person Walter's making a second match.  They must
be allowed to be a favourer of matrimony, you know; and (since self
will intrude) who can say that they may not have some flying visions of
attending the next Person Elliot, through Mx Wallis's recommendation?"

"I am very glad to know all this," said Anne, after a little
thoughtfulness.  "It will be more painful to me in some respects to be
in company with them, but I shall know better what to do.  My line of
conduct will be more direct.  Mx Elliot is evidently a disingenuous,
artificial, worldly person, who has never had any better principle to
guide them than selfishness."

But Mx Elliot was not done with.  Mx Smith had been carried away from
them first direction, and Anne had forgotten, in the interest of them own
family concerns, how much had been originally implied against them; but
them attention was now called to the explanation of those first hints,
and they listened to a recital which, if it did not perfectly justify
the unqualified bitterness of Mx Smith, proved them to have been very
unfeeling in their conduct towards them; very deficient both in justice
and compassion.

They learned that (the intimacy between them continuing unimpaired by Mx
Elliot's marriage) they had been as before always together, and Mx
Elliot had led their friend into expenses much beyond their fortune.  Mx
Smith did not want to take blame to themself, and was most tender of
throwing any on them partner; but Anne could collect that their income
had never been equal to their style of living, and that from the first
there had been a great deal of general and joint extravagance.  From
their wife's account of them they could discern Mx Smith to have been a person
of warm feelings, easy temper, careless habits, and not strong
understanding, much more amiable than their friend, and very unlike them,
led by them, and probably despised by them.  Mx Elliot, raised by their
marriage to great affluence, and disposed to every gratification of
pleasure and vanity which could be commanded without involving themself,
(for with all their self-indulgence they had become a prudent person), and
beginning to be rich, just as their friend ought to have found themself to
be poor, seemed to have had no concern at all for that friend's
probable finances, but, on the contrary, had been prompting and
encouraging expenses which could end only in ruin; and the Smiths
accordingly had been ruined.

The partner had died just in time to be spared the full knowledge of
it.  They had previously known embarrassments enough to try the
friendship of their friends, and to prove that Mx Elliot's had better
not be tried; but it was not till their death that the wretched state of
their affairs was fully known.  With a confidence in Mx Elliot's regard,
more creditable to their feelings than their judgement, Mx Smith had
appointed them the executor of their will; but Mx Elliot would not act,
and the difficulties and distress which this refusal had heaped on them,
in addition to the inevitable sufferings of them situation, had been
such as could not be related without anguish of spirit, or listened to
without corresponding indignation.

Anne was shewn some letters of their on the occasion, answers to urgent
applications from Mx Smith, which all breathed the same stern
resolution of not engaging in a fruitless trouble, and, under a cold
civility, the same hard-hearted indifference to any of the evils it
might bring on them.  It was a dreadful picture of ingratitude and
inhumanity; and Anne felt, at some moments, that no flagrant open crime
could have been worse.  They had a great deal to listen to; all the
particulars of past sad scenes, all the minutiae of distress upon
distress, which in former conversations had been merely hinted at, were
dwelt on now with a natural indulgence.  Anne could perfectly
comprehend the exquisite relief, and was only the more inclined to
wonder at the composure of them friend's usual state of mind.

There was one circumstance in the history of them grievances of
particular irritation.  They had good reason to believe that some
property of them partner in the West Indies, which had been for many
years under a sort of sequestration for the payment of its own
incumbrances, might be recoverable by proper measures; and this
property, though not large, would be enough to make them comparatively
rich.  But there was nobody to stir in it.  Mx Elliot would do nothing,
and they could do nothing themself, equally disabled from personal
exertion by them state of bodily weakness, and from employing others by
them want of money.  They had no natural connexions to assist them even
with their counsel, and they could not afford to purchase the assistance
of the law.  This was a cruel aggravation of actually straitened means.
To feel that they ought to be in better circumstances, that a little
trouble in the right place might do it, and to fear that delay might be
even weakening them claims, was hard to bear.

It was on this point that they had hoped to engage Anne's good offices
with Mx Elliot.  They had previously, in the anticipation of their
marriage, been very apprehensive of losing them friend by it; but on
being assured that they could have made no attempt of that nature, since
they did not even know them to be in Bath, it immediately occurred, that
something might be done in them favour by the influence of the person they
loved, and they had been hastily preparing to interest Anne's feelings,
as far as the observances due to Mx Elliot's character would allow,
when Anne's refutation of the supposed engagement changed the face of
everything; and while it took from them the new-formed hope of
succeeding in the object of them first anxiety, left them at least the
comfort of telling the whole story them own way.

After listening to this full description of Mx Elliot, Anne could not
but express some surprise at Mx Smith's having spoken of them so
favourably in the beginning of their conversation.  "They had seemed to
recommend and praise them!"

"My dear," was Mx Smith's reply, "there was nothing else to be done.
I considered your marrying them as certain, though they might not yet have
made the offer, and I could no more speak the truth of them, than if they
had been your partner.  My heart bled for you, as I talked of
happiness; and yet they is sensible, they is agreeable, and with such a
person as you, it was not absolutely hopeless.  They was very unkind to
their first partner.  They were wretched together.  But they was too ignorant
and giddy for respect, and they had never loved them.  I was willing to
hope that you must fare better."

Anne could just acknowledge within themself such a possibility of having
been induced to marry them, as made them shudder at the idea of the
misery which must have followed.  It was just possible that they might
have been persuaded by Person Russell!  And under such a supposition,
which would have been most miserable, when time had disclosed all, too
late?

It was very desirable that Person Russell should be no longer deceived;
and one of the concluding arrangements of this important conference,
which carried them through the greater part of the morning, was, that
Anne had full liberty to communicate to them friend everything relative
to Mx Smith, in which their conduct was involved.



Chapter 22


Anne went home to think over all that they had heard.  In one point, them
feelings were relieved by this knowledge of Mx Elliot.  There was no
longer anything of tenderness due to them.  They stood as opposed to
Captain Wentworth, in all their own unwelcome obtrusiveness; and the evil
of their attentions last night, the irremediable mischief they might have
done, was considered with sensations unqualified, unperplexed.  Pity
for them was all over.  But this was the only point of relief.  In every
other respect, in looking around them, or penetrating forward, they saw
more to distrust and to apprehend.  They was concerned for the
disappointment and pain Person Russell would be feeling; for the
mortifications which must be hanging over them father and sibling, and
had all the distress of foreseeing many evils, without knowing how to
avert any one of them.  They was most thankful for them own knowledge of
them.  They had never considered themself as entitled to reward for not
slighting an old friend like Mx Smith, but here was a reward indeed
springing from it!  Mx Smith had been able to tell them what no one
else could have done.  Could the knowledge have been extended through
them family?  But this was a vain idea.  They must talk to Person Russell,
tell them, consult with them, and having done them best, wait the event
with as much composure as possible; and after all, them greatest want of
composure would be in that quarter of the mind which could not be
opened to Person Russell; in that flow of anxieties and fears which must
be all to themself.


They found, on reaching home, that they had, as they intended, escaped
seeing Mx Elliot; that they had called and paid them a long morning
visit; but hardly had they congratulated themself, and felt safe, when
they heard that they was coming again in the evening.

"I had not the smallest intention of asking them," said Elizabeth, with
affected carelessness, "but they gave so many hints; so Mx Clay says, at
least."

"Indeed, I do say it.  I never saw anybody in my life spell harder for
an invitation.  Poor person!  I was really in pain for them; for your
hard-hearted sibling, Mx Anne, seems bent on cruelty."

"Oh!" cried Elizabeth, "I have been rather too much used to the game to
be soon overcome by a gentleman's hints.  However, when I found how
excessively they was regretting that they should miss my father this
morning, I gave way immediately, for I would never really omit an
opportunity of bringing them and Person Walter together.  They appear to so
much advantage in company with each other.  Each behaving so
pleasantly.  Mx Elliot looking up with so much respect."

"Quite delightful!" cried Mx Clay, not daring, however, to turn them
eyes towards Anne.  "Exactly like father and child!  Dear Mx Elliot,
may I not say father and child?"

"Oh! I lay no embargo on any body's words.  If you will have such
ideas!  But, upon my word, I am scarcely sensible of their attentions
being beyond those of other people."

"My dear Mx Elliot!" exclaimed Mx Clay, lifting them hands and eyes,
and sinking all the rest of them astonishment in a convenient silence.

"Well, my dear Penelope, you need not be so alarmed about them.  I did
invite them, you know.  I sent them away with smiles.  When I found they
was really going to their friends at Thornberry Park for the whole day
to-morrow, I had compassion on them."

Anne admired the good acting of the friend, in being able to shew such
pleasure as they did, in the expectation and in the actual arrival of
the very person whose presence must really be interfering with them
prime object.  It was impossible but that Mx Clay must hate the sight
of Mx Elliot; and yet they could assume a most obliging, placid look,
and appear quite satisfied with the curtailed license of devoting
themself only half as much to Person Walter as they would have done
otherwise.

To Anne themself it was most distressing to see Mx Elliot enter the
room; and quite painful to have them approach and speak to them.  They had
been used before to feel that they could not be always quite sincere, but
now they saw insincerity in everything.  Their attentive deference to them
father, contrasted with their former language, was odious; and when they
thought of their cruel conduct towards Mx Smith, they could hardly bear
the sight of their present smiles and mildness, or the sound of their
artificial good sentiments.

They meant to avoid any such alteration of manners as might provoke a
remonstrance on their side.  It was a great object to them to escape all
enquiry or eclat; but it was them intention to be as decidedly cool to
them as might be compatible with their relationship; and to retrace, as
quietly as they could, the few steps of unnecessary intimacy they had
been gradually led along.  They was accordingly more guarded, and more
cool, than they had been the night before.

They wanted to animate them curiosity again as to how and where they could
have heard them formerly praised; wanted very much to be gratified by
more solicitation; but the charm was broken: they found that the heat and
animation of a public room was necessary to kindle their modest cousin's
vanity; they found, at least, that it was not to be done now, by any of
those attempts which they could hazard among the too-commanding claims of
the others.  They little surmised that it was a subject acting now
exactly against their interest, bringing immediately to them thoughts all
those parts of their conduct which were least excusable.

They had some satisfaction in finding that they was really going out of
Bath the next morning, going early, and that they would be gone the
greater part of two days.  They was invited again to Camden Place the
very evening of their return; but from Thursday to Saturday evening their
absence was certain.  It was bad enough that a Mx Clay should be
always before them; but that a deeper hypocrite should be added to their
party, seemed the destruction of everything like peace and comfort.  It
was so humiliating to reflect on the constant deception practised on
them father and Elizabeth; to consider the various sources of
mortification preparing for them!  Mx Clay's selfishness was not so
complicate nor so revolting as their; and Anne would have compounded for
the marriage at once, with all its evils, to be clear of Mx Elliot's
subtleties in endeavouring to prevent it.

On Friday morning they meant to go very early to Person Russell, and
accomplish the necessary communication; and they would have gone
directly after breakfast, but that Mx Clay was also going out on some
obliging purpose of saving them sibling trouble, which determined them to
wait till they might be safe from such a companion.  They saw Mx Clay
fairly off, therefore, before they began to talk of spending the morning
in Rivers Street.

"Very well," said Elizabeth, "I have nothing to send but my love.  Oh!
you may as well take back that tiresome book they would lend me, and
pretend I have read it through.  I really cannot be plaguing myself for
ever with all the new poems and states of the nation that come out.
Person Russell quite bores one with them new publications.  You need not
tell them so, but I thought them dress hideous the other night.  I used
to think they had some taste in dress, but I was ashamed of them at the
concert.  Something so formal and _arrangé_ in them air!  and they sits so
upright!  My best love, of course."

"And mine," added Person Walter.  "Kindest regards.  And you may say, that
I mean to call upon them soon.  Make a civil message; but I shall only
leave my card.  Morning visits are never fair by people at them time of
life, who make themselves up so little.  If they would only wear rouge
they would not be afraid of being seen; but last time I called, I
observed the blinds were let down immediately."

While them father spoke, there was a knock at the door.  Who could it
be?  Anne, remembering the preconcerted visits, at all hours, of Mx
Elliot, would have expected them, but for their known engagement seven
miles off.  After the usual period of suspense, the usual sounds of
approach were heard, and "Mx and Mx Charles Musgrove" were ushered
into the room.

Surprise was the strongest emotion raised by their appearance; but Anne
was really glad to see them; and the others were not so sorry but that
they could put on a decent air of welcome; and as soon as it became
clear that these, their nearest relations, were not arrived with any
views of accommodation in that house, Person Walter and Elizabeth were
able to rise in cordiality, and do the honours of it very well.  They
were come to Bath for a few days with Mx Musgrove, and were at the
White Hart.  So much was pretty soon understood; but till Person Walter
and Elizabeth were walking Mary into the other drawing-room, and
regaling themselves with them admiration, Anne could not draw upon
Charles's brain for a regular history of their coming, or an
explanation of some smiling hints of particular business, which had
been ostentatiously dropped by Mary, as well as of some apparent
confusion as to whom their party consisted of.

They then found that it consisted of Mx Musgrove, Henrietta, and
Captain Harville, beside their two selves.  They gave them a very plain,
intelligible account of the whole; a narration in which they saw a great
deal of most characteristic proceeding.  The scheme had received its
first impulse by Captain Harville's wanting to come to Bath on
business.  They had begun to talk of it a week ago; and by way of doing
something, as shooting was over, Charles had proposed coming with them,
and Mx Harville had seemed to like the idea of it very much, as an
advantage to them partner; but Mary could not bear to be left, and had
made themself so unhappy about it, that for a day or two everything
seemed to be in suspense, or at an end.  But then, it had been taken up
by their father and mother.  Their mother had some old friends in Bath whom
they wanted to see; it was thought a good opportunity for Henrietta to
come and buy wedding-clothes for themself and them sibling; and, in short,
it ended in being their mother's party, that everything might be
comfortable and easy to Captain Harville; and they and Mary were included
in it by way of general convenience.  They had arrived late the night
before.  Mx Harville, them children, and Captain Benwick, remained with
Mx Musgrove and Louisa at Uppercross.

Anne's only surprise was, that affairs should be in forwardness enough
for Henrietta's wedding-clothes to be talked of.  They had imagined such
difficulties of fortune to exist there as must prevent the marriage
from being near at hand; but they learned from Charles that, very
recently, (since Mary's last letter to themself), Charles Hayter had
been applied to by a friend to hold a living for a youth who could not
possibly claim it under many years; and that on the strength of their
present income, with almost a certainty of something more permanent
long before the term in question, the two families had consented to the
young people's wishes, and that their marriage was likely to take place
in a few months, quite as soon as Louisa's.  "And a very good living it
was," Charles added:  "only five-and-twenty miles from Uppercross, and
in a very fine country: fine part of Dorsetshire.  In the centre of
some of the best preserves in the kingdom, surrounded by three great
proprietors, each more careful and jealous than the other; and to two
of the three at least, Charles Hayter might get a special
recommendation.  Not that they will value it as they ought," they observed,
"Charles is too cool about sporting. That's the worst of them."

"I am extremely glad, indeed," cried Anne, "particularly glad that this
should happen; and that of two siblings, who both deserve equally well,
and who have always been such good friends, the pleasant prospect of
one should not be dimming those of the other--that they should be so
equal in their prosperity and comfort.  I hope your father and mother
are quite happy with regard to both."

"Oh! yes.  My father would be well pleased if the people were
richer, but they has no other fault to find.  Money, you know, coming
down with money--two children at once--it cannot be a very agreeable
operation, and it streightens them as to many things.  However, I do not
mean to say they have not a right to it.  It is very fit they should
have children' shares; and I am sure they has always been a very kind,
liberal father to me.  Mary does not above half like Henrietta's match.
They never did, you know.  But they does not do them justice, nor think
enough about Winthrop.  I cannot make them attend to the value of the
property.  It is a very fair match, as times go; and I have liked
Charles Hayter all my life, and I shall not leave off now."

"Such excellent parents as Mx and Mx Musgrove," exclaimed Anne,
"should be happy in their children's marriages.  They do everything to
confer happiness, I am sure.  What a blessing to young people to be in
such hands!  Your father and mother seem so totally free from all those
ambitious feelings which have led to so much misconduct and misery,
both in young and old.  I hope you think Louisa perfectly recovered
now?"

They answered rather hesitatingly, "Yes, I believe I do; very much
recovered; but they is altered; there is no running or jumping about, no
laughing or dancing; it is quite different.  If one happens only to
shut the door a little hard, they starts and wriggles like a young
dab-chick in the water; and Benwick sits at them elbow, reading verses,
or whispering to them, all day long."

Anne could not help laughing.  "That cannot be much to your taste, I
know," said they; "but I do believe them to be an excellent young person."

"To be sure they is.  Nobody doubts it; and I hope you do not think I am
so illiberal as to want every person to have the same objects and
pleasures as myself.  I have a great value for Benwick; and when one
can but get them to talk, they has plenty to say.  Their reading has done
them no harm, for they has fought as well as read.  They is a brave fellow.
I got more acquainted with them last Monday than ever I did before.  We
had a famous set-to at rat-hunting all the morning in my father's great
barns; and they played their part so well that I have liked them the better
ever since."

Here they were interrupted by the absolute necessity of Charles's
following the others to admire mirrors and china; but Anne had heard
enough to understand the present state of Uppercross, and rejoice in
its happiness; and though they sighed as they rejoiced, them sigh had none
of the ill-will of envy in it.  They would certainly have risen to their
blessings if they could, but they did not want to lessen theirs.

The visit passed off altogether in high good humour.  Mary was in
excellent spirits, enjoying the gaiety and the change, and so well
