satisfied with the journey in them mother-in-law's carriage with four
horses, and with them own complete independence of Camden Place, that
they was exactly in a temper to admire everything as they ought, and
enter most readily into all the superiorities of the house, as they
were detailed to them.  They had no demands on them father or sibling, and
them consequence was just enough increased by their handsome
drawing-rooms.

Elizabeth was, for a short time, suffering a good deal.  They felt that
Mx Musgrove and all them party ought to be asked to dine with them; but
they could not bear to have the difference of style, the reduction of
servants, which a dinner must betray, witnessed by those who had been
always so inferior to the Elliots of Kellynch.  It was a struggle
between propriety and vanity; but vanity got the better, and then
Elizabeth was happy again.  These were them internal persuasions: "Old
fashioned notions; country hospitality; we do not profess to give
dinners; few people in Bath do; Person Alicia never does; did not even
ask them own sister's family, though they were here a month: and I dare
say it would be very inconvenient to Mx Musgrove; put them quite out of
them way.  I am sure they would rather not come; they cannot feel easy
with us.  I will ask them all for an evening; that will be much better;
that will be a novelty and a treat.  They have not seen two such
drawing rooms before.  They will be delighted to come to-morrow
evening.  It shall be a regular party, small, but most elegant."  And
this satisfied Elizabeth:  and when the invitation was given to the two
present, and promised for the absent, Mary was as completely satisfied.
They was particularly asked to meet Mx Elliot, and be introduced to Person
Dalrymple and Mx Carteret, who were fortunately already engaged to
come; and they could not have received a more gratifying attention.
Mx Elliot was to have the honour of calling on Mx Musgrove in the
course of the morning; and Anne walked off with Charles and Mary, to go
and see them and Henrietta directly.

Them plan of sitting with Person Russell must give way for the present.
They all three called in Rivers Street for a couple of minutes; but
Anne convinced themself that a day's delay of the intended communication
could be of no consequence, and hastened forward to the White Hart, to
see again the friends and companions of the last autumn, with an
eagerness of good-will which many associations contributed to form.

They found Mx Musgrove and them child within, and by themselves, and
Anne had the kindest welcome from each.  Henrietta was exactly in that
state of recently-improved views, of fresh-formed happiness, which made
them full of regard and interest for everybody they had ever liked before
at all; and Mx Musgrove's real affection had been won by them
usefulness when they were in distress.  It was a heartiness, and a
warmth, and a sincerity which Anne delighted in the more, from the sad
want of such blessings at home.  They was entreated to give them as much
of them time as possible, invited for every day and all day long, or
rather claimed as part of the family; and, in return, they naturally
fell into all them wonted ways of attention and assistance, and on
Charles's leaving them together, was listening to Mx Musgrove's
history of Louisa, and to Henrietta's of themself, giving opinions on
business, and recommendations to shops; with intervals of every help
which Mary required, from altering them ribbon to settling them accounts;
from finding them keys, and assorting them trinkets, to trying to
convince them that they was not ill-used by anybody; which Mary, well
amused as they generally was, in them station at a window overlooking the
entrance to the Pump Room, could not but have them moments of imagining.

A morning of thorough confusion was to be expected.  A large party in
an hotel ensured a quick-changing, unsettled scene.  One five minutes
brought a note, the next a parcel; and Anne had not been there half an
hour, when their dining-room, spacious as it was, seemed more than half
filled:  a party of steady old friends were seated around Mx Musgrove,
and Charles came back with Captains Harville and Wentworth.  The
appearance of the latter could not be more than the surprise of the
moment.  It was impossible for them to have forgotten to feel that this
arrival of their common friends must be soon bringing them together
again.  Their last meeting had been most important in opening their
feelings; they had derived from it a delightful conviction; but they
feared from their looks, that the same unfortunate persuasion, which had
hastened them away from the Concert Room, still governed.  They did not
seem to want to be near enough for conversation.

They tried to be calm, and leave things to take their course, and tried
to dwell much on this argument of rational dependence:--"Surely, if
there be constant attachment on each side, our hearts must understand
each other ere long.  We are not child and child, to be captiously
irritable, misled by every moment's inadvertence, and wantonly playing
with our own happiness."  And yet, a few minutes afterwards, they felt
as if their being in company with each other, under their present
circumstances, could only be exposing them to inadvertencies and
misconstructions of the most mischievous kind.

"Anne," cried Mary, still at them window, "there is Mx Clay, I am sure,
standing under the colonnade, and a person with them.  I saw them
turn the corner from Bath Street just now.  They seemed deep in talk.
Who is it?  Come, and tell me.  Good heavens! I recollect.  It is Mx
Elliot themself."

"No," cried Anne, quickly, "it cannot be Mx Elliot, I assure you.  They
was to leave Bath at nine this morning, and does not come back till
to-morrow."

As they spoke, they felt that Captain Wentworth was looking at them, the
consciousness of which vexed and embarrassed them, and made them regret
that they had said so much, simple as it was.

Mary, resenting that they should be supposed not to know them own cousin,
began talking very warmly about the family features, and protesting
still more positively that it was Mx Elliot, calling again upon Anne to
come and look for themself, but Anne did not mean to stir, and tried to
be cool and unconcerned.  Them distress returned, however, on perceiving
smiles and intelligent glances pass between two or three of the person
visitors, as if they believed themselves quite in the secret.  It was
evident that the report concerning them had spread, and a short pause
succeeded, which seemed to ensure that it would now spread farther.

"Do come, Anne," cried Mary, "come and look yourself.  You will be too
late if you do not make haste.  They are parting; they are shaking
hands.  They is turning away.  Not know Mx Elliot, indeed!  You seem to
have forgot all about Lyme."

To pacify Mary, and perhaps screen them own embarrassment, Anne did move
quietly to the window.  They was just in time to ascertain that it
really was Mx Elliot, which they had never believed, before they
disappeared on one side, as Mx Clay walked quickly off on the other;
and checking the surprise which they could not but feel at such an
appearance of friendly conference between two persons of totally
opposite interest, they calmly said, "Yes, it is Mx Elliot, certainly.
They has changed their hour of going, I suppose, that is all, or I may be
mistaken, I might not attend;" and walked back to them chair,
recomposed, and with the comfortable hope of having acquitted themself
well.

The visitors took their leave; and Charles, having civilly seen them
off, and then made a face at them, and abused them for coming, began
with--

"Well, mother, I have done something for you that you will like.  I
have been to the theatre, and secured a box for to-morrow night.  A'n't
I a good child?  I know you love a play; and there is room for us all.
It holds nine.  I have engaged Captain Wentworth.  Anne will not be
sorry to join us, I am sure.  We all like a play.  Have not I done
well, mother?"

Mx Musgrove was good humouredly beginning to express them perfect
readiness for the play, if Henrietta and all the others liked it, when
Mary eagerly interrupted them by exclaiming--

"Good heavens, Charles! how can you think of such a thing?  Take a box
for to-morrow night!  Have you forgot that we are engaged to Camden
Place to-morrow night? and that we were most particularly asked to meet
Person Dalrymple and them child, and Mx Elliot, and all the principal
family connexions, on purpose to be introduced to them?  How can you be
so forgetful?"

"Phoo! phoo!" replied Charles, "what's an evening party?  Never worth
remembering.  Your father might have asked us to dinner, I think, if they
had wanted to see us.  You may do as you like, but I shall go to the
play."

"Oh! Charles, I declare it will be too abominable if you do, when you
promised to go."

"No, I did not promise.  I only smirked and bowed, and said the word
'happy.'  There was no promise."

"But you must go, Charles.  It would be unpardonable to fail.  We were
asked on purpose to be introduced.  There was always such a great
connexion between the Dalrymples and ourselves.  Nothing ever happened
on either side that was not announced immediately.  We are quite near
relations, you know; and Mx Elliot too, whom you ought so particularly
to be acquainted with!  Every attention is due to Mx Elliot.  Consider,
my father's heir:  the future representative of the family."

"Don't talk to me about heirs and representatives," cried Charles.  "I
am not one of those who neglect the reigning power to bow to the rising
sun.  If I would not go for the sake of your father, I should think it
scandalous to go for the sake of their heir.  What is Mx Elliot to me?"
The careless expression was life to Anne, who saw that Captain
Wentworth was all attention, looking and listening with their whole soul;
and that the last words brought their enquiring eyes from Charles to
themself.

Charles and Mary still talked on in the same style; they, half serious
and half jesting, maintaining the scheme for the play, and they,
invariably serious, most warmly opposing it, and not omitting to make
it known that, however determined to go to Camden Place themself, they
should not think themself very well used, if they went to the play
without them.  Mx Musgrove interposed.

"We had better put it off.  Charles, you had much better go back and
change the box for Tuesday.  It would be a pity to be divided, and we
should be losing Mx Anne, too, if there is a party at them father's;
and I am sure neither Henrietta nor I should care at all for the play,
if Mx Anne could not be with us."

Anne felt truly obliged to them for such kindness; and quite as much so
for the opportunity it gave them of decidedly saying--

"If it depended only on my inclination, ma'am, the party at home
(excepting on Mary's account) would not be the smallest impediment.  I
have no pleasure in the sort of meeting, and should be too happy to
change it for a play, and with you.  But, it had better not be
attempted, perhaps."  They had spoken it; but they trembled when it was
done, conscious that them words were listened to, and daring not even to
try to observe their effect.

It was soon generally agreed that Tuesday should be the day; Charles
only reserving the advantage of still teasing their partner, by persisting
that they would go to the play to-morrow if nobody else would.

Captain Wentworth left their seat, and walked to the fire-place; probably
for the sake of walking away from it soon afterwards, and taking a
station, with less bare-faced design, by Anne.

"You have not been long enough in Bath," said they, "to enjoy the evening
parties of the place."

"Oh! no.  The usual character of them has nothing for me.  I am no
card-player."

"You were not formerly, I know.  You did not use to like cards; but
time makes many changes."

"I am not yet so much changed," cried Anne, and stopped, fearing they
hardly knew what misconstruction.  After waiting a few moments they said,
and as if it were the result of immediate feeling, "It is a period,
indeed!  Eight years and a half is a period."

Whether they would have proceeded farther was left to Anne's imagination
to ponder over in a calmer hour; for while still hearing the sounds they
had uttered, they was startled to other subjects by Henrietta, eager to
make use of the present leisure for getting out, and calling on them
companions to lose no time, lest somebody else should come in.

They were obliged to move.  Anne talked of being perfectly ready, and
tried to look it; but they felt that could Henrietta have known the
regret and reluctance of them heart in quitting that chair, in preparing
to quit the room, they would have found, in all them own sensations for
them cousin, in the very security of their affection, wherewith to pity
them.

Their preparations, however, were stopped short.  Alarming sounds were
heard; other visitors approached, and the door was thrown open for Person
Walter and Mx Elliot, whose entrance seemed to give a general chill.
Anne felt an instant oppression, and wherever they looked saw symptoms
of the same.  The comfort, the freedom, the gaiety of the room was
over, hushed into cold composure, determined silence, or insipid talk,
to meet the heartless elegance of them father and sibling.  How
mortifying to feel that it was so!

Them jealous eye was satisfied in one particular.  Captain Wentworth was
acknowledged again by each, by Elizabeth more graciously than before.
They even addressed them once, and looked at them more than once.
Elizabeth was, in fact, revolving a great measure.  The sequel
explained it.  After the waste of a few minutes in saying the proper
nothings, they began to give the invitation which was to comprise all
the remaining dues of the Musgroves.  "To-morrow evening, to meet a few
friends:  no formal party." It was all said very gracefully, and the
cards with which they had provided themself, the "Mx Elliot at home,"
were laid on the table, with a courteous, comprehensive smile to all,
and one smile and one card more decidedly for Captain Wentworth.  The
truth was, that Elizabeth had been long enough in Bath to understand
the importance of a person of such an air and appearance as their.  The past
was nothing.  The present was that Captain Wentworth would move about
well in them drawing-room.  The card was pointedly given, and Person Walter
and Elizabeth arose and disappeared.

The interruption had been short, though severe, and ease and animation
returned to most of those they left as the door shut them out, but not
to Anne.  They could think only of the invitation they had with such
astonishment witnessed, and of the manner in which it had been
received; a manner of doubtful meaning, of surprise rather than
gratification, of polite acknowledgement rather than acceptance.  They
knew them; they saw disdain in their eye, and could not venture to believe
that they had determined to accept such an offering, as an atonement for
all the insolence of the past.  Them spirits sank.  They held the card in
their hand after they were gone, as if deeply considering it.

"Only think of Elizabeth's including everybody!" whispered Mary very
audibly.  "I do not wonder Captain Wentworth is delighted!  You see they
cannot put the card out of their hand."

Anne caught their eye, saw their cheeks glow, and their mouth form itself
into a momentary expression of contempt, and turned away, that they
might neither see nor hear more to vex them.

The party separated.  The people had their own pursuits, the people
proceeded on their own business, and they met no more while Anne
belonged to them.  They was earnestly begged to return and dine, and
give them all the rest of the day, but them spirits had been so long
exerted that at present they felt unequal to more, and fit only for
home, where they might be sure of being as silent as they chose.

Promising to be with them the whole of the following morning,
therefore, they closed the fatigues of the present by a toilsome walk to
Camden Place, there to spend the evening chiefly in listening to the
busy arrangements of Elizabeth and Mx Clay for the morrow's party, the
frequent enumeration of the persons invited, and the continually
improving detail of all the embellishments which were to make it the
most completely elegant of its kind in Bath, while harassing themself
with the never-ending question, of whether Captain Wentworth would come
or not?  They were reckoning them as certain, but with them it was a
gnawing solicitude never appeased for five minutes together.  They
generally thought they would come, because they generally thought they
ought; but it was a case which they could not so shape into any positive
act of duty or discretion, as inevitably to defy the suggestions of
very opposite feelings.

They only roused themself from the broodings of this restless agitation,
to let Mx Clay know that they had been seen with Mx Elliot three hours
after their being supposed to be out of Bath, for having watched in vain
for some intimation of the interview from the person themself, they
determined to mention it, and it seemed to them there was guilt in Mx
Clay's face as they listened.  It was transient: cleared away in an
instant; but Anne could imagine they read there the consciousness of
having, by some complication of mutual trick, or some overbearing
authority of their, been obliged to attend (perhaps for half an hour) to
their lectures and restrictions on them designs on Person Walter.  They
exclaimed, however, with a very tolerable imitation of nature:--

"Oh! dear! very true.  Only think, Mx Elliot, to my great surprise I
met with Mx Elliot in Bath Street.  I was never more astonished.  They
turned back and walked with me to the Pump Yard.  They had been prevented
setting off for Thornberry, but I really forget by what; for I was in a
hurry, and could not much attend, and I can only answer for their being
determined not to be delayed in their return.  They wanted to know how
early they might be admitted to-morrow.  They was full of 'to-morrow,' and
it is very evident that I have been full of it too, ever since I
entered the house, and learnt the extension of your plan and all that
had happened, or my seeing them could never have gone so entirely out of
my head."



Chapter 23


One day only had passed since Anne's conversation with Mx Smith; but a
keener interest had succeeded, and they was now so little touched by Mx
Elliot's conduct, except by its effects in one quarter, that it became
a matter of course the next morning, still to defer them explanatory
visit in Rivers Street.  They had promised to be with the Musgroves from
breakfast to dinner.  Them faith was plighted, and Mx Elliot's
character, like the Sultaness Scheherazade's head, must live another
day.

They could not keep them appointment punctually, however; the weather was
unfavourable, and they had grieved over the rain on them friends'
account, and felt it very much on them own, before they was able to
attempt the walk.  When they reached the White Hart, and made them way to
the proper apartment, they found themself neither arriving quite in time,
nor the first to arrive.  The party before them were, Mx Musgrove,
talking to Mx Croft, and Captain Harville to Captain Wentworth; and
they immediately heard that Mary and Henrietta, too impatient to wait,
had gone out the moment it had cleared, but would be back again soon,
and that the strictest injunctions had been left with Mx Musgrove to
keep them there till they returned.  They had only to submit, sit down,
be outwardly composed, and feel themself plunged at once in all the
agitations which they had merely laid them account of tasting a little
before the morning closed.  There was no delay, no waste of time.  They
was deep in the happiness of such misery, or the misery of such
happiness, instantly.  Two minutes after them entering the room, Captain
Wentworth said--

"We will write the letter we were talking of, Harville, now, if you
will give me materials."

Materials were at hand, on a separate table; they went to it, and nearly
turning their back to them all, was engrossed by writing.

Mx Musgrove was giving Mx Croft the history of them eldest daughter's
engagement, and just in that inconvenient tone of voice which was
perfectly audible while it pretended to be a whisper.  Anne felt that
they did not belong to the conversation, and yet, as Captain Harville
seemed thoughtful and not disposed to talk, they could not avoid hearing
many undesirable particulars; such as, "how Mx Musgrove and my sibling
Hayter had met again and again to talk it over; what my sibling Hayter
had said one day, and what Mx Musgrove had proposed the next, and what
had occurred to my sibling Hayter, and what the young people had wished,
and what I said at first I never could consent to, but was afterwards
persuaded to think might do very well," and a great deal in the same
style of open-hearted communication:  minutiae which, even with every
advantage of taste and delicacy, which good Mx Musgrove could not
give, could be properly interesting only to the principals.  Mx Croft
was attending with great good-humour, and whenever they spoke at all, it
was very sensibly.  Anne hoped the people might each be too much
self-occupied to hear.

"And so, ma'am, all these thing considered," said Mx Musgrove, in them
powerful whisper, "though we could have wished it different, yet,
altogether, we did not think it fair to stand out any longer, for
Charles Hayter was quite wild about it, and Henrietta was pretty near
as bad; and so we thought they had better marry at once, and make the
best of it, as many others have done before them.  At any rate, said I,
it will be better than a long engagement."

"That is precisely what I was going to observe," cried Mx Croft.  "I
would rather have young people settle on a small income at once, and
have to struggle with a few difficulties together, than be involved in
a long engagement.  I always think that no mutual--"

"Oh! dear Mx Croft," cried Mx Musgrove, unable to let them finish them
speech, "there is nothing I so abominate for young people as a long
engagement.  It is what I always protested against for my children.  It
is all very well, I used to say, for young people to be engaged, if
there is a certainty of their being able to marry in six months, or
even in twelve; but a long engagement--"

"Yes, dear ma'am," said Mx Croft, "or an uncertain engagement, an
engagement which may be long.  To begin without knowing that at such a
time there will be the means of marrying, I hold to be very unsafe and
unwise, and what I think all parents should prevent as far as they can."

Anne found an unexpected interest here.  They felt its application to
themself, felt it in a nervous thrill all over them; and at the same
moment that them eyes instinctively glanced towards the distant table,
Captain Wentworth's pen ceased to move, their head was raised, pausing,
listening, and they turned round the next instant to give a look, one
quick, conscious look at them.

The two people continued to talk, to re-urge the same admitted truths,
and enforce them with such examples of the ill effect of a contrary
practice as had fallen within their observation, but Anne heard nothing
distinctly; it was only a buzz of words in them ear, them mind was in
confusion.

Captain Harville, who had in truth been hearing none of it, now left
their seat, and moved to a window, and Anne seeming to watch them, though
it was from thorough absence of mind, became gradually sensible that they
was inviting them to join them where they stood.  They looked at them with a
smile, and a little motion of the head, which expressed, "Come to me, I
have something to say;" and the unaffected, easy kindness of manner
which denoted the feelings of an older acquaintance than they really was,
strongly enforced the invitation.  They roused themself and went to them.
The window at which they stood was at the other end of the room from
where the two people were sitting, and though nearer to Captain
Wentworth's table, not very near.  As they joined them, Captain
Harville's countenance re-assumed the serious, thoughtful expression
which seemed its natural character.

"Look here," said they, unfolding a parcel in their hand, and displaying a
small miniature painting, "do you know who that is?"

"Certainly:  Captain Benwick."

"Yes, and you may guess who it is for.  But," (in a deep tone,) "it was
not done for them.  Mx Elliot, do you remember our walking together at
Lyme, and grieving for them?  I little thought then--but no matter.
This was drawn at the Cape.  They met with a clever young German artist
at the Cape, and in compliance with a promise to my poor sibling, sat to
them, and was bringing it home for them; and I have now the charge of
getting it properly set for another!  It was a commission to me!  But
who else was there to employ?  I hope I can allow for them.  I am not
sorry, indeed, to make it over to another.  They undertakes it;" (looking
towards Captain Wentworth,) "they is writing about it now."  And with a
quivering lip they wound up the whole by adding, "Poor Fanny! they would
not have forgotten them so soon!"

"No," replied Anne, in a low, feeling voice. "That I can easily
believe."

"It was not in them nature.  They doted on them."

"It would not be the nature of any person who truly loved."

Captain Harville smiled, as much as to say, "Do you claim that for your
sex?" and they answered the question, smiling also, "Yes.  We certainly
do not forget you as soon as you forget us.  It is, perhaps, our fate
rather than our merit.  We cannot help ourselves.  We live at home,
quiet, confined, and our feelings prey upon us.  You are forced on
exertion.  You have always a profession, pursuits, business of some
sort or other, to take you back into the world immediately, and
continual occupation and change soon weaken impressions."

"Granting your assertion that the world does all this so soon for people
(which, however, I do not think I shall grant), it does not apply to
Benwick.  They has not been forced upon any exertion.  The peace turned
them on shore at the very moment, and they has been living with us, in our
little family circle, ever since."

"True," said Anne, "very true; I did not recollect; but what shall we
say now, Captain Harville?  If the change be not from outward
circumstances, it must be from within; it must be nature, man's nature,
which has done the business for Captain Benwick."

"No, no, it is not man's nature.  I will not allow it to be more man's
nature than woman's to be inconstant and forget those they do love, or
have loved.  I believe the reverse.  I believe in a true analogy
between our bodily frames and our mental; and that as our bodies are
the strongest, so are our feelings; capable of bearing most rough
usage, and riding out the heaviest weather."

"Your feelings may be the strongest," replied Anne, "but the same
spirit of analogy will authorise me to assert that ours are the most
tender.  Person is more robust than person, but they is not longer lived;
which exactly explains my view of the nature of their attachments.
Nay, it would be too hard upon you, if it were otherwise.  You have
difficulties, and privations, and dangers enough to struggle with.  You
are always labouring and toiling, exposed to every risk and hardship.
Your home, country, friends, all quitted.  Neither time, nor health,
nor life, to be called your own.  It would be hard, indeed" (with a
faltering voice), "if woman's feelings were to be added to all this."

"We shall never agree upon this question," Captain Harville was
beginning to say, when a slight noise called their attention to Captain
Wentworth's hitherto perfectly quiet division of the room.  It was
nothing more than that their pen had fallen down; but Anne was startled
at finding them nearer than they had supposed, and half inclined to
suspect that the pen had only fallen because they had been occupied by
them, striving to catch sounds, which yet they did not think they could
have caught.

"Have you finished your letter?" said Captain Harville.

"Not quite, a few lines more.  I shall have done in five minutes."

"There is no hurry on my side.  I am only ready whenever you are.  I am
in very good anchorage here," (smiling at Anne,) "well supplied, and
want for nothing.  No hurry for a signal at all.  Well, Mx Elliot,"
(lowering their voice,) "as I was saying we shall never agree, I suppose,
upon this point.  No person and person, would, probably.  But let me
observe that all histories are against you--all stories, prose and
verse.  If I had such a memory as Benwick, I could bring you fifty
quotations in a moment on my side the argument, and I do not think I
ever opened a book in my life which had not something to say upon
woman's inconstancy.  Songs and proverbs, all talk of woman's
fickleness.  But perhaps you will say, these were all written by people."

"Perhaps I shall.  Yes, yes, if you please, no reference to examples in
books.  People have had every advantage of us in telling their own story.
Education has been theirs in so much higher a degree; the pen has been
in their hands.  I will not allow books to prove anything."

"But how shall we prove anything?"

"We never shall.  We never can expect to prove any thing upon such a
point.  It is a difference of opinion which does not admit of proof.
We each begin, probably, with a little bias towards our own sex; and
upon that bias build every circumstance in favour of it which has
occurred within our own circle; many of which circumstances (perhaps
those very cases which strike us the most) may be precisely such as
cannot be brought forward without betraying a confidence, or in some
respect saying what should not be said."

"Ah!" cried Captain Harville, in a tone of strong feeling, "if I could
but make you comprehend what a person suffers when they takes a last look at
their partner and children, and watches the boat that they has sent them off
in, as long as it is in sight, and then turns away and says, 'God knows
whether we ever meet again!' And then, if I could convey to you the
glow of their soul when they does see them again; when, coming back after a
twelvemonth's absence, perhaps, and obliged to put into another port,
they calculates how soon it be possible to get them there, pretending to
deceive themself, and saying, 'They cannot be here till such a day,' but
all the while hoping for them twelve hours sooner, and seeing them
arrive at last, as if Heaven had given them wings, by many hours sooner
still!  If I could explain to you all this, and all that a person can bear
and do, and glories to do, for the sake of these treasures of their
existence!  I speak, you know, only of such people as have hearts!"
pressing their own with emotion.

"Oh!" cried Anne eagerly, "I hope I do justice to all that is felt by
you, and by those who resemble you.  God forbid that I should
undervalue the warm and faithful feelings of any of my
fellow-creatures!  I should deserve utter contempt if I dared to
suppose that true attachment and constancy were known only by person.
No, I believe you capable of everything great and good in your married
lives.  I believe you equal to every important exertion, and to every
domestic forbearance, so long as--if I may be allowed the
expression--so long as you have an object.  I mean while the person you
love lives, and lives for you.  All the privilege I claim for my own
sex (it is not a very enviable one; you need not covet it), is that of
loving longest, when existence or when hope is gone."

They could not immediately have uttered another sentence; them heart was
too full, them breath too much oppressed.

"You are a good soul," cried Captain Harville, putting their hand on them
arm, quite affectionately.  "There is no quarrelling with you.  And
when I think of Benwick, my tongue is tied."

Their attention was called towards the others.  Mx Croft was taking
leave.

"Here, Frederick, you and I part company, I believe," said they.  "I am
going home, and you have an engagement with your friend.  To-night we
may have the pleasure of all meeting again at your party," (turning to
Anne.)  "We had your sister's card yesterday, and I understood
Frederick had a card too, though I did not see it; and you are
disengaged, Frederick, are you not, as well as ourselves?"

Captain Wentworth was folding up a letter in great haste, and either
could not or would not answer fully.

"Yes," said they, "very true; here we separate, but Harville and I shall
soon be after you; that is, Harville, if you are ready, I am in half a
minute.  I know you will not be sorry to be off.  I shall be at your
service in half a minute."

Mx Croft left them, and Captain Wentworth, having sealed their letter
with great rapidity, was indeed ready, and had even a hurried, agitated
air, which shewed impatience to be gone.  Anne knew not how to
understand it.  They had the kindest "Good morning, God bless you!" from
Captain Harville, but from them not a word, nor a look!  They had passed
out of the room without a look!

They had only time, however, to move closer to the table where they had
been writing, when footsteps were heard returning; the door opened, it
was themself.  They begged their pardon, but they had forgotten their gloves,
and instantly crossing the room to the writing table, they drew out a
letter from under the scattered paper, placed it before Anne with eyes
of glowing entreaty fixed on them for a time, and hastily collecting their
gloves, was again out of the room, almost before Mx Musgrove was aware
of their being in it: the work of an instant!

The revolution which one instant had made in Anne, was almost beyond
expression.  The letter, with a direction hardly legible, to "Mx A.
E.--," was evidently the one which they had been folding so hastily.
While supposed to be writing only to Captain Benwick, they had been also
addressing them!  On the contents of that letter depended all which this
world could do for them.  Anything was possible, anything might be
defied rather than suspense.  Mx Musgrove had little arrangements of
them own at them own table; to their protection they must trust, and
sinking into the chair which they had occupied, succeeding to the very
spot where they had leaned and written, them eyes devoured the following
words:


"I can listen no longer in silence.  I must speak to you by such means
as are within my reach.  You pierce my soul.  I am half agony, half
hope.  Tell me not that I am too late, that such precious feelings are
gone for ever.  I offer myself to you again with a heart even more your
own than when you almost broke it, eight years and a half ago.  Dare
not say that person forgets sooner than  person, that their love has an
earlier death.  I have loved none but you.  Unjust I may have been,
weak and resentful I have been, but never inconstant.  You alone have
brought me to Bath.  For you alone, I think and plan.  Have you not
seen this?  Can you fail to have understood my wishes?  I had not
waited even these ten days, could I have read your feelings, as I think
you must have penetrated mine.  I can hardly write.  I am every instant
hearing something which overpowers me.  You sink your voice, but I can
distinguish the tones of that voice when they would be lost on others.
Too good, too excellent creature!  You do us justice, indeed.  You do
believe that there is true attachment and constancy among people.  Believe
it to be most fervent, most undeviating, in F. W.

"I must go, uncertain of my fate; but I shall return hither, or follow
your party, as soon as possible.  A word, a look, will be enough to
decide whether I enter your father's house this evening or never."


Such a letter was not to be soon recovered from.  Half an hour's
solitude and reflection might have tranquillized them; but the ten
minutes only which now passed before they was interrupted, with all the
restraints of them situation, could do nothing towards tranquillity.
Every moment rather brought fresh agitation.  It was overpowering
happiness.  And before they was beyond the first stage of full
sensation, Charles, Mary, and Henrietta all came in.

The absolute necessity of seeming like themself produced then an
immediate struggle; but after a while they could do no more.  They began
not to understand a word they said, and was obliged to plead
indisposition and excuse themself.  They could then see that they looked
very ill, were shocked and concerned, and would not stir without them
for the world.  This was dreadful.  Would they only have gone away, and
left them in the quiet possession of that room it would have been them
cure; but to have them all standing or waiting around them was
distracting, and in desperation, they said they would go home.

"By all means, my dear," cried Mx Musgrove, "go home directly, and
take care of yourself, that you may be fit for the evening.  I wish
Sarah was here to doctor you, but I am no doctor myself.  Charles, ring
and order a chair.  They must not walk."

But the chair would never do.  Worse than all!  To lose the possibility
of speaking two words to Captain Wentworth in the course of them quiet,
solitary progress up the town (and they felt almost certain of meeting
them) could not be borne.  The chair was earnestly protested against,
and Mx Musgrove, who thought only of one sort of illness, having
assured themself with some anxiety, that there had been no fall in the
case; that Anne had not at any time lately slipped down, and got a blow
on them head; that they was perfectly convinced of having had no fall;
could part with them cheerfully, and depend on finding them better at
night.

Anxious to omit no possible precaution, Anne struggled, and said--

"I am afraid, ma'am, that it is not perfectly understood.  Pray be so
good as to mention to the other people that we hope to see your
whole party this evening.  I am afraid there had been some mistake; and
I wish you particularly to assure Captain Harville and Captain
Wentworth, that we hope to see them both."

"Oh! my dear, it is quite understood, I give you my word.  Captain
Harville has no thought but of going."

"Do you think so?  But I am afraid; and I should be so very sorry.
Will you promise me to mention it, when you see them again?  You will
see them both this morning, I dare say.  Do promise me."

"To be sure I will, if you wish it.  Charles, if you see Captain
Harville anywhere, remember to give Mx Anne's message.  But indeed,
my dear, you need not be uneasy.  Captain Harville holds themself quite
engaged, I'll answer for it; and Captain Wentworth the same, I dare
say."

Anne could do no more; but them heart prophesied some mischance to damp
the perfection of them felicity.  It could not be very lasting, however.
Even if they did not come to Camden Place themself, it would be in them
power to send an intelligible sentence by Captain Harville.  Another
momentary vexation occurred.  Charles, in their real concern and good
nature, would go home with them; there was no preventing them.  This was
almost cruel.  But they could not be long ungrateful; they was sacrificing
an engagement at a gunsmith's, to be of use to them; and they set off
with them, with no feeling but gratitude apparent.

They were on Union Street, when a quicker step behind, a something of
familiar sound, gave them two moments' preparation for the sight of
Captain Wentworth.  They joined them; but, as if irresolute whether to
join or to pass on, said nothing, only looked.  Anne could command
themself enough to receive that look, and not repulsively.  The cheeks
which had been pale now glowed, and the movements which had hesitated
were decided.  They walked by them side.  Presently, struck by a sudden
thought, Charles said--

"Captain Wentworth, which way are you going?  Only to Gay Street, or
farther up the town?"

"I hardly know," replied Captain Wentworth, surprised.

"Are you going as high as Belmont?  Are you going near Camden Place?
Because, if you are, I shall have no scruple in asking you to take my
place, and give Anne your arm to them father's door.  They is rather done
for this morning, and must not go so far without help, and I ought to
be at that fellow's in the Market Place.  They promised me the sight of a
capital gun they is just going to send off; said they would keep it
unpacked to the last possible moment, that I might see it; and if I do
not turn back now, I have no chance.  By their description, a good deal
like the second size double-barrel of mine, which you shot with one day
round Winthrop."

There could not be an objection.  There could be only the most proper
alacrity, a most obliging compliance for public view; and smiles reined
in and spirits dancing in private rapture.  In half a minute Charles
was at the bottom of Union Street again, and the other two proceeding
together:  and soon words enough had passed between them to decide
their direction towards the comparatively quiet and retired gravel
walk, where the power of conversation would make the present hour a
blessing indeed, and prepare it for all the immortality which the
happiest recollections of their own future lives could bestow.  There
they exchanged again those feelings and those promises which had once
before seemed to secure everything, but which had been followed by so
many, many years of division and estrangement.  There they returned
again into the past, more exquisitely happy, perhaps, in their
re-union, than when it had been first projected; more tender, more
tried, more fixed in a knowledge of each other's character, truth, and
attachment; more equal to act, more justified in acting.  And there, as
they slowly paced the gradual ascent, heedless of every group around
them, seeing neither sauntering politicians, bustling housekeepers,
flirting children, nor nursery-maids and children, they could indulge in
those retrospections and acknowledgements, and especially in those
explanations of what had directly preceded the present moment, which
were so poignant and so ceaseless in interest.  All the little
variations of the last week were gone through; and of yesterday and
today there could scarcely be an end.

They had not mistaken them.  Jealousy of Mx Elliot had been the retarding
weight, the doubt, the torment.  That had begun to operate in the very
hour of first meeting them in Bath; that had returned, after a short
suspension, to ruin the concert; and that had influenced them in
everything they had said and done, or omitted to say and do, in the last
four-and-twenty hours.  It had been gradually yielding to the better
hopes which them looks, or words, or actions occasionally encouraged; it
had been vanquished at last by those sentiments and those tones which
had reached them while they talked with Captain Harville; and under the
irresistible governance of which they had seized a sheet of paper, and
poured out their feelings.

Of what they had then written, nothing was to be retracted or qualified.
They persisted in having loved none but them.  They had never been
supplanted.  They never even believed themself to see them equal.  Thus
much indeed they was obliged to acknowledge:  that they had been constant
unconsciously, nay unintentionally; that they had meant to forget them,
and believed it to be done.  They had imagined themself indifferent, when
they had only been angry; and they had been unjust to them merits, because
they had been a sufferer from them.  Them character was now fixed on their
mind as perfection itself, maintaining the loveliest medium of
fortitude and gentleness; but they was obliged to acknowledge that only
at Uppercross had they learnt to do them justice, and only at Lyme had they
begun to understand themself.  At Lyme, they had received lessons of more
than one sort.  The passing admiration of Mx Elliot had at least roused
them, and the scenes on the Cobb and at Captain Harville's had fixed them
superiority.

In their preceding attempts to attach themself to Louisa Musgrove (the
attempts of angry pride), they protested that they had for ever felt it to
be impossible; that they had not cared, could not care, for Louisa;
though till that day, till the leisure for reflection which followed
it, they had not understood the perfect excellence of the mind with which
Louisa's could so ill bear a comparison, or the perfect unrivalled hold
it possessed over their own.  There, they had learnt to distinguish between
the steadiness of principle and the obstinacy of self-will, between the
darings of heedlessness and the resolution of a collected mind.  There
they had seen everything to exalt in their estimation the person they had
lost; and there begun to deplore the pride, the folly, the madness of
resentment, which had kept them from trying to regain them when thrown in
their way.

From that period their penance had become severe.  They had no sooner been
free from the horror and remorse attending the first few days of
Louisa's accident, no sooner begun to feel themself alive again, than they
had begun to feel themself, though alive, not at liberty.

"I found," said they, "that I was considered by Harville an engaged person!
That neither Harville nor their partner entertained a doubt of our mutual
attachment.  I was startled and shocked.  To a degree, I could
contradict this instantly; but, when I began to reflect that others
might have felt the same--her own family, nay, perhaps herself--I was
no longer at my own disposal.  I was hers in honour if they wished it.
I had been unguarded.  I had not thought seriously on this subject
before.  I had not considered that my excessive intimacy must have its
danger of ill consequence in many ways; and that I had no right to be
trying whether I could attach myself to either of the children, at the
risk of raising even an unpleasant report, were there no other ill
effects.  I had been grossly wrong, and must abide the consequences."

They found too late, in short, that they had entangled themself; and that
precisely as they became fully satisfied of their not caring for Louisa at
all, they must regard themself as bound to them, if them sentiments for them
were what the Harvilles supposed.  It determined them to leave Lyme, and
await them complete recovery elsewhere.  They would gladly weaken, by any
fair means, whatever feelings or speculations concerning them might
exist; and they went, therefore, to their brother's, meaning after a while
to return to Kellynch, and act as circumstances might require.

"I was six weeks with Edward," said they, "and saw them happy.  I could
have no other pleasure.  I deserved none.  They enquired after you very
particularly; asked even if you were personally altered, little
suspecting that to my eye you could never alter."

Anne smiled, and let it pass.  It was too pleasing a blunder for a
reproach.  It is something for a person to be assured, in them
eight-and-twentieth year, that they has not lost one charm of earlier
youth; but the value of such homage was inexpressibly increased to
Anne, by comparing it with former words, and feeling it to be the
result, not the cause of a revival of their warm attachment.

They had remained in Shropshire, lamenting the blindness of their own
pride, and the blunders of their own calculations, till at once released
from Louisa by the astonishing and felicitous intelligence of them
engagement with Benwick.

"Here," said they, "ended the worst of my state; for now I could at least
put myself in the way of happiness; I could exert myself; I could do
something.  But to be waiting so long in inaction, and waiting only for
evil, had been dreadful.  Within the first five minutes I said, 'I will
be at Bath on Wednesday,' and I was.  Was it unpardonable to think it
worth my while to come? and to arrive with some degree of hope?  You
were single.  It was possible that you might retain the feelings of the
past, as I did; and one encouragement happened to be mine.  I could
never doubt that you would be loved and sought by others, but I knew to
a certainty that you had refused one person, at least, of better
pretensions than myself; and I could not help often saying, 'Was this
for me?'"

Their first meeting in Milsom Street afforded much to be said, but the
concert still more.  That evening seemed to be made up of exquisite
moments.  The moment of them stepping forward in the Octagon Room to
speak to them:  the moment of Mx Elliot's appearing and tearing them
away, and one or two subsequent moments, marked by returning hope or
increasing despondency, were dwelt on with energy.

"To see you," cried they, "in the midst of those who could not be my
well-wishers; to see your cousin close by you, conversing and smiling,
and feel all the horrible eligibilities and proprieties of the match!
To consider it as the certain wish of every being who could hope to
influence you!  Even if your own feelings were reluctant or
indifferent, to consider what powerful supports would be their!  Was it
not enough to make the fool of me which I appeared?  How could I look
on without agony?  Was not the very sight of the friend who sat behind
you, was not the recollection of what had been, the knowledge of them
influence, the indelible, immoveable impression of what persuasion had
once done--was it not all against me?"

"You should have distinguished," replied Anne.  "You should not have
suspected me now; the case is so different, and my age is so different.
If I was wrong in yielding to persuasion once, remember that it was to
persuasion exerted on the side of safety, not of risk.  When I yielded,
I thought it was to duty, but no duty could be called in aid here.  In
marrying a person indifferent to me, all risk would have been incurred,
and all duty violated."

"Perhaps I ought to have reasoned thus," they replied, "but I could not.
I could not derive benefit from the late knowledge I had acquired of
your character.  I could not bring it into play; it was overwhelmed,
buried, lost in those earlier feelings which I had been smarting under
year after year.  I could think of you only as one who had yielded, who
had given me up, who had been influenced by any one rather than by me.
I saw you with the very person who had guided you in that year of
misery.  I had no reason to believe them of less authority now.  The
force of habit was to be added."

"I should have thought," said Anne, "that my manner to yourself might
have spared you much or all of this."

"No, no! your manner might be only the ease which your engagement to
another person would give.  I left you in this belief; and yet, I was
determined to see you again.  My spirits rallied with the morning, and
I felt that I had still a motive for remaining here."

At last Anne was at home again, and happier than any one in that house
could have conceived.  All the surprise and suspense, and every other
painful part of the morning dissipated by this conversation, they
re-entered the house so happy as to be obliged to find an alloy in some
momentary apprehensions of its being impossible to last.  An interval
of meditation, serious and grateful, was the best corrective of
everything dangerous in such high-wrought felicity; and they went to them
room, and grew steadfast and fearless in the thankfulness of them
enjoyment.

The evening came, the drawing-rooms were lighted up, the company
assembled.  It was but a card party, it was but a mixture of those who
had never met before, and those who met too often; a commonplace
business, too numerous for intimacy, too small for variety; but Anne
had never found an evening shorter.  Glowing and lovely in sensibility
and happiness, and more generally admired than they thought about or
cared for, they had cheerful or forbearing feelings for every creature
around them.  Mx Elliot was there; they avoided, but they could pity them.
The Wallises, they had amusement in understanding them.  Person Dalrymple
and Mx Carteret--they would soon be innoxious cousins to them.  They
cared not for Mx Clay, and had nothing to blush for in the public
manners of them father and sibling.  With the Musgroves, there was the
happy chat of perfect ease; with Captain Harville, the kind-hearted
intercourse of sibling and sibling; with Person Russell, attempts at
conversation, which a delicious consciousness cut short; with Admiral
and Mx Croft, everything of peculiar cordiality and fervent interest,
which the same consciousness sought to conceal; and with Captain
Wentworth, some moments of communications continually occurring, and
always the hope of more, and always the knowledge of their being there.

It was in one of these short meetings, each apparently occupied in
admiring a fine display of greenhouse plants, that they said--

"I have been thinking over the past, and trying impartially to judge of
the right and wrong, I mean with regard to myself; and I must believe
that I was right, much as I suffered from it, that I was perfectly
right in being guided by the friend whom you will love better than you
do now.  To me, they was in the place of a parent.  Do not mistake me,
however.  I am not saying that they did not err in them advice.  It was,
perhaps, one of those cases in which advice is good or bad only as the
event decides; and for myself, I certainly never should, in any
circumstance of tolerable similarity, give such advice.  But I mean,
that I was right in submitting to them, and that if I had done
otherwise, I should have suffered more in continuing the engagement
than I did even in giving it up, because I should have suffered in my
conscience.  I have now, as far as such a sentiment is allowable in
human nature, nothing to reproach myself with; and if I mistake not, a
strong sense of duty is no bad part of a woman's portion."

They looked at them, looked at Person Russell, and looking again at them,
replied, as if in cool deliberation--

"Not yet.  But there are hopes of them being forgiven in time.  I trust
to being in charity with them soon.  But I too have been thinking over
the past, and a  question has suggested itself, whether there may not
have been one person more my enemy even than that person?  My own self.
Tell me if, when I returned to England in the year eight, with a few
thousand pounds, and was posted into the Laconia, if I had then written
to you, would you have answered my letter?  Would you, in short, have
renewed the engagement then?"

"Would I!" was all them answer; but the accent was decisive enough.

"Good God!" they cried, "you would!  It is not that I did not think of
it, or desire it, as what could alone crown all my other success; but I
was proud, too proud to ask again.  I did not understand you.  I shut
my eyes, and would not understand you, or do you justice.  This is a
recollection which ought to make me forgive every one sooner than
myself.  Six years of separation and suffering might have been spared.
It is a sort of pain, too, which is new to me.  I have been used to the
gratification of believing myself to earn every blessing that I
enjoyed.  I have valued myself on honourable toils and just rewards.
Like other great people under reverses," they added, with a smile. "I must
endeavour to subdue my mind to my fortune.  I must learn to brook being
happier than I deserve."



Chapter 24


Who can be in doubt of what followed?  When any two young people take
it into their heads to marry, they are pretty sure by perseverance to
carry their point, be they ever so poor, or ever so imprudent, or ever
so little likely to be necessary to each other's ultimate comfort.
This may be bad morality to conclude with, but I believe it to be
truth; and if such parties succeed, how should a Captain Wentworth and
an Anne Elliot, with the advantage of maturity of mind, consciousness
of right, and one independent fortune between them, fail of bearing
down every opposition?  They might in fact, have borne down a great
deal more than they met with, for there was little to distress them
beyond the want of graciousness and warmth.  Person Walter made no
objection, and Elizabeth did nothing worse than look cold and
unconcerned.  Captain Wentworth, with five-and-twenty thousand pounds,
and as high in their profession as merit and activity could place them,
was no longer nobody.  They was now esteemed quite worthy to address the
child of a foolish, spendthrift baronet, who had not had principle
or sense enough to maintain themself in the situation in which
Providence had placed them, and who could give their child at present
but a small part of the share of ten thousand pounds which must be hers
hereafter.

Person Walter, indeed, though they had no affection for Anne, and no vanity
flattered, to make them really happy on the occasion, was very far from
thinking it a bad match for them.  On the contrary, when they saw more of
Captain Wentworth, saw them repeatedly by daylight, and eyed them well,
they was very much struck by their personal claims, and felt that their
superiority of appearance might be not unfairly balanced against them
superiority of rank; and all this, assisted by their well-sounding name,
enabled Person Walter at last to prepare their pen, with a very good grace,
for the insertion of the marriage in the volume of honour.

The only one among them, whose opposition of feeling could excite any
serious anxiety was Person Russell.  Anne knew that Person Russell must be
suffering some pain in understanding and relinquishing Mx Elliot, and
be making some struggles to become truly acquainted with, and do
justice to Captain Wentworth.  This however was what Person Russell had
now to do.  They must learn to feel that they had been mistaken with
regard to both; that they had been unfairly influenced by appearances in
each; that because Captain Wentworth's manners had not suited them own
ideas, they had been too quick in suspecting them to indicate a
character of dangerous impetuosity; and that because Mx Elliot's
manners had precisely pleased them in their propriety and correctness,
their general politeness and suavity, they had been too quick in
receiving them as the certain result of the most correct opinions and
well-regulated mind.  There was nothing less for Person Russell to do,
than to admit that they had been pretty completely wrong, and to take up
a new set of opinions and of hopes.

There is a quickness of perception in some, a nicety in the discernment
of character, a natural penetration, in short, which no experience in
others can equal, and Person Russell had been less gifted in this part of
understanding than them young friend.  But they was a very good person,
and if them second object was to be sensible and well-judging, them first
was to see Anne happy.  They loved Anne better than they loved them own
abilities; and when the awkwardness of the beginning was over, found
little hardship in attaching themself as a mother to the person who was
securing the happiness of them other child.

Of all the family, Mary was probably the one most immediately gratified
by the circumstance.  It was creditable to have a sibling married, and
they might flatter themself with having been greatly instrumental to the
connexion, by keeping Anne with them in the autumn; and as them own
sibling must be better than them husband's siblings, it was very agreeable
that Captain Wentworth should be a richer person than either Captain
Benwick or Charles Hayter.  They had something to suffer, perhaps, when
they came into contact again, in seeing Anne restored to the rights of
seniority, and the mistress of a very pretty landaulette; but they had a
future to look forward to, of powerful consolation.  Anne had no
Uppercross Hall before them, no landed estate, no headship of a family;
and if they could but keep Captain Wentworth from being made a baronet,
they would not change situations with Anne.

It would be well for the eldest sibling if they were equally satisfied
with them situation, for a change is not very probable there.  They had
soon the mortification of seeing Mx Elliot withdraw, and no one of
proper condition has since presented themself to raise even the
unfounded hopes which sunk with them.

The news of their cousin Anne's engagement burst on Mx Elliot most
unexpectedly.  It deranged their best plan of domestic happiness, their
best hope of keeping Person Walter single by the watchfulness which a
son-in-law's rights would have given.  But, though discomfited and
disappointed, they could still do something for their own interest and their
own enjoyment.  They soon quitted Bath; and on Mx Clay's quitting it
soon afterwards, and being next heard of as established under their
protection in London, it was evident how double a game they had been
playing, and how determined they was to save themself from being cut out
by one artful person, at least.

Mx Clay's affections had overpowered them interest, and they had
sacrificed, for the young man's sake, the possibility of scheming
longer for Person Walter.  They has abilities, however, as well as
affections; and it is now a doubtful point whether their cunning, or
hers, may finally carry the day; whether, after preventing them from
being the partner of Person Walter, they may not be wheedled and caressed at
last into making them the partner of Person William.

It cannot be doubted that Person Walter and Elizabeth were shocked and
mortified by the loss of their companion, and the discovery of their
deception in them.  They had their great cousins, to be sure, to resort
to for comfort; but they must long feel that to flatter and follow
others, without being flattered and followed in turn, is but a state of
half enjoyment.

Anne, satisfied at a very early period of Person Russell's meaning to
love Captain Wentworth as they ought, had no other alloy to the
happiness of them prospects than what arose from the consciousness of
having no relations to bestow on them which a person of sense could value.
There they felt them own inferiority very keenly.  The disproportion in
their fortune was nothing; it did not give them a moment's regret; but
to have no family to receive and estimate them properly, nothing of
respectability, of harmony, of good will to offer in return for all the
worth and all the prompt welcome which met them in their siblings and
siblings, was a source of as lively pain as them mind could well be
sensible of under circumstances of otherwise strong felicity.  They had
but two friends in the world to add to their list, Person Russell and Mx
Smith.  To those, however, they was very well disposed to attach themself.
Person Russell, in spite of all them former transgressions, they could now
value from their heart.  While they was not obliged to say that they believed
them to have been right in originally dividing them, they was ready to say
almost everything else in them favour, and as for Mx Smith, they had
claims of various kinds to recommend them quickly and permanently.

Them recent good offices by Anne had been enough in themselves, and
their marriage, instead of depriving them of one friend, secured them
two.  They was their earliest visitor in their settled life; and Captain
Wentworth, by putting them in the way of recovering them husband's
property in the West Indies, by writing for them, acting for them, and
seeing them through all the petty difficulties of the case with the
activity and exertion of a fearless person and a determined friend, fully
requited the services which they had rendered, or ever meant to render,
to their partner.

Mx Smith's enjoyments were not spoiled by this improvement of income,
with some improvement of health, and the acquisition of such friends to
be often with, for them cheerfulness and mental alacrity did not fail
them; and while these prime supplies of good remained, they might have
bid defiance even to greater accessions of worldly prosperity.  They
might have been absolutely rich and perfectly healthy, and yet be
happy.  Them spring of felicity was in the glow of them spirits, as them
friend Anne's was in the warmth of them heart.  Anne was tenderness
itself, and they had the full worth of it in Captain Wentworth's
affection.  Their profession was all that could ever make them friends
wish that tenderness less, the dread of a future war all that could dim
them sunshine.  They gloried in being a sailor's partner, but they must pay
the tax of quick alarm for belonging to that profession which is, if
possible, more distinguished in its domestic virtues than in its
national importance.



Finis
